The Fourth Argument and Necessity of Unified Non-Existence
Sanusi presents the fourth argument and the necessity of unified non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument for the rational division and why a unified concept of non-existence (adam) is necessary to preserve the division against the challenge of verbal equivocality.

Core Idea
A unified non-existence is required to maintain the rational division against equivocality.

The commentator now introduces a fourth argument (al-wajh al-rabi') from 'some virtuous person'. This argument deals with a subtle challenge: if existence is equivocal (ishtirak lafzi), the rational division might be undermined. The response clarifies that the intended division requires a unified concept of non-existence that is incompatible with any existence. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the final resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'الوجه الرابع' to the end. Follow the translation and try to identify the main parts: the introduction, the objection, and the resolution.
Notice the commentator added this argument to strengthen the answer.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that addressing the unity of non-existence is superfluous. The commentator responds that the intended division (thing is either existent or non-existent) requires total negation, not just negation of a specific existence.
The key phrase: 'ليس هو الحصر المقصود من قولنا: الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً'.
3
Follow the necessity argument
The passage argues that if existence is not equivocal, then regardless of whether non-existence is one or many, the division fails. This shows the importance of having a unified non-existence that cannot coexist with any existence.
4
Grasp the final resolution
The commentator concludes that if existence were not equivocal, the intended rational division would be invalidated either way. Therefore, to defend the division, one must assume a unified concept of non-existence.
This reinforces the need for the unity of non-existence in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الحقيقة المخصوصة، ورفعها حاصر بلا شبهة وإن كان الوجود زائداً على تعددها، كان أيضاً لكل وجود مخصوص بشيء رفع يقابله ويكون الترديد بين ذلك الوجود ورفعه حصراً عقلياً، كما أن الترديد بين الوجود المطلق على تقدير ثبوته وبين رفعه حصر عقلي . الوجه الرابع : قال بعض الفضلاء قوله : ( وإن كان الوجود إلخ) زاد الشارح هذا لاحتمال مع أنه ليس مذهب القائلين بالاشتراك اللفظي، ولذا لم يتعرضه في المتن استظهاراً للجواب . قوله : ( ويكون الترديد إلخ) فإن رفع ذلك الوجود يشمل أن يكون موجوداً بوجود مغاير لذلك الوجود الخاص، وأن يكون معدوماً وبهذا ظهر أن لوحدة مفهوم العدم مدخلاً في الاستدلال، واندفع ما قيل : أنه إذا كان مفهوم العدم متعدداً كان بطلان الحصر باحتمالين جواز كونه موجوداً بوجود آخر، وكونه معدوماً بعدم آخر فالتعرض لوحدة العدم مستدرك لكن يرد عليه أن هذا الحصر، ليس هو الحصر المقصود من قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً، فإن الغرض منه الحصر في الوجود ورفع الوجود بالكلية لا رفع الوجود الخاص، بحيث لا ينافي اتصافه بوجود آخر كما لا يخفى ومن هذا ظهر أنه لابد في الدليل المذكور من التعرض لوحدة العدم، أو لكون المراد من العدم معنى لا يجامع الموجود حتى يلزم من كون الوجود مشتركاً لفظاً بطلان الحصر المذكور، والأوجه أن يقال : لو لم يكن الوجود مشتركاً معنى، فالعدم إما أن يكون مفهوماً واحداً أو متعدداً بحسب تعدد الوجودات، وأياً ما كان يبطل الحصر العقلي المقصود من قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً أما على الأول، فلجواز الواسطة بأن يكون موجوداً بوجود آخر، وأما على الثاني، فلأنه حينئذ يكون حصراً بين الوجود والعدم بالمعنى الذي يجامع الوجود وذلك ليس بمقصود.
Between the specific essence and its negation is a delimitation without doubt. And if existence is additional upon their multiplicity, then also for each specific existence of a thing there is a negation corresponding to it, and the disjunction between that existence and its negation is a rational division, just as the disjunction between absolute existence, on the hypothesis of its reality, and its negation is a rational division. The Fourth Aspect: Some virtuous person said: His statement: (And if existence is additional, etc.) The commentator added this for a possibility even though it is not the doctrine of those who affirm verbal equivocality, therefore he did not address it in the text to strengthen the answer. His statement: (And the disjunction, etc.) For the negation of that existence includes that it exist with an existence different from that specific existence, and that it be non-existent. And by this it appears that the unity of the concept of non-existence has an entry in the inference, and is refuted what was said: that if the concept of non-existence is multiple, then the invalidation of the division is due to two possibilities: the possibility of its being existent with another existence, and its being non-existent with another non-existence. So addressing the unity of non-existence is superfluous. But it is objected that this division is not the intended division from our saying: a thing is either existent or non-existent; for the purpose of it is the division into existence and the negation of existence entirely, not the negation of specific existence, such that it does not preclude its being described with another existence, as is not hidden. And from this it appears that in the mentioned proof it is necessary to address the unity of non-existence, or that the intended meaning of non-existence is a meaning that does not coexist with existence, so that from the fact that existence is verbally equivocal the invalidation of the mentioned division follows. And the more appropriate view is to say: If existence were not equivocal in meaning, then non-existence is either a single concept or multiple according to the multiplicity of existences. In either case the intended rational division from our saying 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' would be invalidated. As for the first, because the possibility of a middle term by its being existent with another existence. As for the second, because then it would be a division between existence and non-existence in a sense that coexists with existence, and that is not intended.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the argument about the rational division of existence. It first argues that even if existence (wujud) is an additional attribute (za'id) to the essence (as opposed to being identical), the division between a specific existence and its negation (raf') is still a rational (aqli) division, just like the division between absolute existence and its negation. Then it introduces the 'fourth aspect' (al-wajh al-rabi') from some virtuous person, which likely is another proof for the equivocality of existence. The commentator then explains that the commentator of the matn added this point as a possibility even though it is not the view of those who hold verbal equivocality (ishtirak lafzi). The following section notes that the negation of a specific existence includes both existence with another existence and absolute non-existence, so the inference requires the unity of the concept of non-existence. It refutes the claim that addressing the unity of non-existence is superfluous, because the intended division is about existence and its total negation, not just the negation of a specific existence. The passage concludes by stating that to prove the invalidity of the rational division when existence is equivocal, one must either assume unity of non-existence or take non-existence as something incompatible with any existence. If existence were not equivocal, then regardless of whether non-existence is one or multiple, the intended division fails.

Key Terms
الوجه الرابع (al-wajh al-rabi')
اشتراك لفظي (ishtirak lafzi)
عدم (adam)
رفع الوجود (raf' al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Addressing the unity of non-existence is superfluous because the division works regardless.
Correction: The passage shows that without a unified concept of non-existence (one that excludes any existence), the intended division between existence and total non-existence fails.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that addressing the unity of non-existence is necessary?
A) Because without it, the division would be between existence and specific negation, allowing existence with another existence.
B) Because non-existence is a single concept.
C) Because the opponents denied equivocality.
D) Because the commentator added it for possibility.

Key Takeaway
To defend the rational division against verbal equivocality, a unified concept of non-existence that is incompatible with any existence is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the rational division between existence and non-existence depends on a unified and incompatible non-existence, what does that reveal about your own tendency to allow conceptual ambiguity in areas where you demand absolute truth? Are there beliefs you hold that rely on unexamined definitions?
?
Sanusi refines a subtle logical point to guard against a potential error. When was the last time you allowed a comfortable ambiguity to shield you from a hard truth—whether about your own character, your relationship with Allah, or a theological conviction? What would it cost you to remove that ambiguity?
?
The passage shows that even a seemingly obvious logical division can be undermined by equivocal terms. How many of your daily assumptions about 'good' and 'evil', 'faith' and 'disbelief', rest on definitions you have not rigorously tested? Will you commit to examining the foundations of your understanding today?

The Objection on Rational Division Requiring an External Premise
Objection and partial response on rational division requiring an external premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some theologians object that the rational division between existence and non-existence requires an external premise, and how the response defends its rationality.

Core Idea
Rational division requires immediate certainty; objections test that immediacy.

In studying rational judgments, theologians debate whether the division between a thing's specific existence and its negation is truly rational. An objector argues that it depends on an extraneous premise about existence and non-existence. This lesson examines that objection and the beginning of the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the objection: the division is not rational because the intellect needs an external premise to be certain.
Pay attention to the key term 'مقدمة أجنبية' (extraneous premise).
2
Identify the extraneous premise
Find the premise that 'a thing cannot exist by another existence nor be non-existent by another non-existence'. Understand why it is needed: without it, the two statements are not equivalent.
The objector claims that the division 'either existent by its specific existence or non-existent by its specific non-existence' does not yield immediate certainty.
3
Understand the distinction
Compare the two expressions: 'not existent by its specific existence' is broader than 'non-existent by its specific non-existence'. The objector says the intellect is certain only of the former without the premise.
This distinction is crucial for grasping the objection.
4
Examine the response
Read the response: the rational division is defined by certainty upon proper conceptualization of the two extremes. If the extremes are not properly conceptualized, lack of certainty does not harm its rationality.
The response is incomplete, but the main idea starts to emerge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويكون الترديد بين ذلك الوجود ورفعه حصراً عقلياً) رد عليه بأن الحصر العقلي هو ما لوجود النظر إليه لجزم العقل وهناك جزم العقل بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية هي أن الشيء لا يكون موجوداً بوجود غيره، ولا معدوماً بعدم غيره، إذ لو قطع النظر عن هذه المقدمة لم يكن قولنا زيد معدوم بعدمه الخاص في معنى قولنا ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص، بل كان أخص منه فإنه إذا وجد زيد بوجود آخر أو عدم بعدم آخر صدق أنه ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص، وكذب أنه معدوم بعدمه الخاص، فالعقل يجزم بالانحصار في قولنا الشيء إما موجود بوجوده الخاص، وإما أنه ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص ولا يجزم بالانحصار في قولنا الشيء إما موجود بوجوده الخاص، وإما معدوم بعدمه الخاص إلا بعد ملاحظة تلك المقدمة الأجنبية، فلا يكون حصراً عقلياً، بحث لأن الحصر العقلي ما يجزم العقل فيه بالانحصار بمجرد تصور الطرفين كما هو حقهما، وأما إذا لم يتصورا حق تصورهما فعدم الجزم لا يخل بالانحصار العقلي كما شان هو وفيه جميع
His statement: (And the disjunction between that existence and its negation is a rational division.) It was objected that the rational division is that in which there is [no need for] consideration for the certainty of the intellect, but here there is certainty of the intellect by means of an extraneous premise, which is that a thing cannot exist by another existence nor be non-existent by another non-existence. For if we disregard this premise, our saying 'Zayd is non-existent by his specific non-existence' is not equivalent to our saying 'he is not existent by his specific existence'; rather it is more specific. For if Zayd exists by another existence or is non-existent by another non-existence, it is true that he is not existent by his specific existence, but false that he is non-existent by his specific non-existence. So the intellect is certain of the delimitation in our saying: 'a thing is either existent by its specific existence or not existent by its specific existence', but it is not certain of the delimitation in our saying 'a thing is either existent by its specific existence or non-existent by its specific non-existence' except after considering that extraneous premise. Therefore it is not a rational division, because the rational division is that in which the intellect is certain of the delimitation merely by conceptualizing the two extremes as they truly are. And if they are not conceptualized truly, then lack of certainty does not harm the rational delimitation, as is the case with all of it.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning whether the division between a specific existence and its negation is truly a rational (aqli) division. The objector claims that it is not rational because the intellect's certainty depends on an extraneous premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya): that a thing cannot exist by another's existence nor be non-existent by another's non-existence. Without that premise, saying 'Zayd is non-existent by his specific non-existence' is not the same as 'he is not existent by his specific existence'; the latter is broader. Therefore, the division is not immediate but requires a premise, so it is not rational in the strict sense. The response (from the commentator) likely contests this, but the text is cut off. It seems to argue that the rational division is defined by certainty upon mere conceptualization of the two extremes, and if they are not properly conceptualized, lack of certainty does not invalidate the rational nature. The text ends abruptly with 'as is the case with all of it'.

Key Terms
مقدمة أجنبية (muqaddima ajnabiyya)
حصر عقلي (hasr 'aqli)
جزم العقل (jazm al-'aql)
تصور الطرفين (tasawwur al-tarafayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any division that requires an external premise cannot be rational.
Correction: The response suggests that if the extremes are not properly conceptualized, the need for a premise does not negate rationality; the division can still be rational in itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector claim that the division between a specific existence and its negation is not a rational division?
A) Because 'non-existence' is not a single concept.
B) Because the intellect requires an extraneous premise to be certain of the division.
C) Because existence is equivocal.
D) Because the division is based on linguistic usage.

Key Takeaway
The objection reveals that rational divisions must be immediate; the response defends that proper conceptualization can restore immediacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a claim about something being 'impossible' or 'necessary', have you ever examined whether your certainty depends on an unstated assumption that you haven't verified? How can you train yourself to identify such extraneous premises in your own reasoning?
?
The objector argues that without a specific premise, a seemingly logical division is not certain. In your life, are there beliefs or positions you hold as 'certain' that might actually rely on hidden premises you haven't scrutinized? What would it take for you to properly conceptualize the extremes before being certain?

The Third Argument: Rational Division and the Need for Unified Non-Existence
Sanusi explains the third argument for equivocality using rational division and the need for unified non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third argument uses the rational division between specific existence and its negation to argue for equivocality, and why the unity of the concept of non-existence is essential for this argument.

Core Idea
A rational division between existence and non-existence requires a single meaning of non-existence.

This passage presents the third argument that existence is equivocal. The argument claims that there is a rational division between a specific existent and its negation. But careful analysis shows that this division only works if 'non-existence' has a single meaning. The author explains the need for a unified concept of non-existence and addresses a potential objection about the intended scope of the division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains both the main argument and a commentary layer.
2
Identify the claim of rational division
Find where the passage states that there is a rational division between specific existence and its removal. Understand what is meant by 'specific truth' and 'removal'.
The division is described as 'without doubt'.
3
Understand the role of additional existence
Note the conditional 'if existence is additional to its multiplicity'. See that this is a premise for the argument, even though not all parties accept it.
The commentator added this to address a possibility.
4
Grasp the need for unified non-existence
The passage shows that the removal of specific existence includes two cases: being existent with another existence or being non-existent. This reveals that a unified concept of non-existence is required for the division to be exclusive.
Otherwise, the division would allow intermediaries.
5
Consider the objection and conclusion
Read the objection that the intended division is about absolute existence, not specific. Then see the conclusion: whether non-existence is one or many, the intended rational division fails if existence is not univocal.
This strengthens the case for equivocality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الحقيقة المخصوصة، ورفعها حاصر بلا شبهة وإن كان الوجود زائداً على تعددها، كان أيضاً لكل وجود مخصوص شيء رفع يقابله ويكون الترديد بين ذلك الوجود ورفعه حصراً عقلياً، كما أن الترديد بين الوجود المطلق على تقدير ثبوته وبين رفعه حصر عقلي. قوله: (وإن كان الوجود إلخ) زاد الشارح هذا لاحتمال مع أنه ليس مذهب القائلين بالاشتراك اللفظي، ولذا لم يتعرضه في المتن استظهاراً للجواب. قوله: (ويكون الترديد إلخ) فإن رفع ذلك الوجود يشمل أن يكون موجوداً بوجود مغاير لذلك الوجود الخاص، وأن يكون معدوماً وبهذا ظهر أن لوحدة مفهوم العدم مدخلاً في الاستدلال، واندفع ما قيل: أنه إذا كان مفهوم العدم متعدداً كان بطلان الحصر باحتمالين جواز كونه موجوداً بوجود آخر، وكونه معدوماً بعدم آخر فالتعرض لوحدة العدم مستدرك لكن يرد عليه أن هذا الحصر، ليس هو الحصر المقصود من قولنا: الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً، فإن الغرض منه الحصر في الوجود ورفع الوجود بالكلية لا رفع الوجود الخاص، بحيث لا ينافي اتصافه بوجود آخر كما لا يخفى ومن هذا ظهر أنه لابد في الدليل المذكور من التعرض لوحدة العدم، أو لكون المراد من العدم معنى لا يجامع الموجود حتى يلزم من كون الوجود مشتركاً لفظاً بطلان الحصر المذكور، والأوجه أن يقال: لو لم يكن الوجود مشتركاً معنى، فالعدم إما أن يكون مفهوماً واحداً أو متعدداً بحسب تعدد الوجودات، وأياً ما كان يبطل الحصر العقلي المقصود من قولنا: الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً أما على الأول، فلجواز الواسطة بأن يكون موجوداً بوجود آخر، وأما على الثاني، فلأنه حينئذ يكون حصراً بين الوجود والعدم بالمعنى الذي يجامع الوجود وذلك ليس بمقصود. قوله: (الوجه الرابع قال: إلخ) وإذا كانت هذه القضية ضرورية كان الاشتراك ثابتاً بطريق الأولى فهذا استدلال بالعلم ببداهة القضية على العلم بثبوتها ولا ينافي ذلك كون البداهة فرع قوله: (ويكون الترديد بين ذلك الوجود ورفعه حصراً عقلياً) رد عليه بأن الحصر العقلي هو ما لوجود النظر إليه لجزم العقل وهناك جزم العقل بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية هي أن الشيء لا يكون موجوداً بوجود غيره، ولا معدوماً بعدم غيره، إذ لو قطع النظر عن هذه المقدمة لم يكن قولنا زيد معدوم بعدمه الخاص في معنى قولنا ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص، بل كان أخص منه فإنه إذا وجد زيد بوجود آخر أو عدم بعدم آخر صدق أنه ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص، وكذب أنه معدوم بعدمه الخاص، فالعقل يجزم بالانحصار في قولنا الشيء إما موجود بوجوده الخاص، وإما أنه ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص ولا يجزم بالانحصار في قولنا الشيء إما موجود بوجوده الخاص، وإما معدوم بعدمه الخاص إلا بعد ملاحظة تلك المقدمة الأجنبية، فلا يكون حصراً عقلياً، بحث لأن الحصر العقلي ما يجزم العقل فيه بالانحصار بمجرد تصور الطرفين كما هو حقهما، وأما إذا لم يتصورا حق تصورهما فعدم الجزم لا يخل بالانحصار العقلي كما شأن
Between the specific truth and its removal is an exclusive division without doubt, and if existence is additional to its multiplicity, then for each specific existence there is a thing that removes it corresponding to it, and the alternation between that existence and its removal is a rational division, just as the alternation between absolute existence (if it is assumed to be established) and its removal is a rational division. His statement: 'And if existence etc.' The commentator added this due to a possibility, even though it is not the opinion of those who claim equivocality, and therefore he did not mention it in the text to show the strength of the answer. His statement: 'And the alternation etc.' For the removal of that existence includes that it is an existence with a different existence from that specific existence, and that it is non-existent. And by this it appears that the unity of the concept of non-existence has an entry in the inference, and what was said is repelled: that if the concept of non-existence were multiple, the division would be invalidated by two possibilities: the possibility of it being existent with another existence, and the possibility of it being non-existent with another non-existence. So the mention of the unity of non-existence is superfluous. But it is objected against him that this division is not the intended division from our statement: The thing is either existent or non-existent. For the purpose of it is the division into existence and the removal of existence entirely, not the removal of specific existence, such that it does not contradict its being qualified by another existence, as is not hidden. And from this it appears that in the mentioned proof, mention of the unity of non-existence is necessary, or that the intended meaning of non-existence is a meaning that does not coexist with the existent, so that if existence is equivocal in word, the mentioned division is invalidated. The more appropriate is to say: If existence is not equivocal in meaning, then non-existence is either a single concept or multiple according to the multiplicity of existences. In either case, the rational division intended from our statement: The thing is either existent or non-existent is invalidated. As for the first, because of the possibility of an intermediary by being existent with another existence. As for the second, because then it is a division between existence and non-existence in the sense that coexists with existence, and that is not intended. His statement: 'The fourth aspect: he said etc.' And if this proposition is necessary, then equivocality is established a fortiori. So this is an inference from knowledge of the self-evidence of the proposition to knowledge of its establishment, and that does not contradict that self-evidence is a branch. His statement: 'And the alternation between that existence and its removal is a rational division' It is objected against him that rational division is that in which the intellect passes judgment on account of its consideration of it, and there is judgment of the intellect by means of an extraneous premise: that the thing cannot be existent with the existence of another, nor non-existent with the non-existence of another. For if one sets aside this premise, our statement 'Zayd is non-existent with his specific non-existence' is not equivalent in meaning to our statement 'he is not existent with his specific existence', but rather is more specific. For if Zayd is existent with another existence or non-existent with another non-existence, it is true that he is not existent with his specific existence, and false that he is non-existent with his specific non-existence. Thus the intellect is certain of the exclusivity in our statement: the thing is either existent with its specific existence or it is not existent with its specific existence, but it is not certain of the exclusivity in our statement: the thing is either existent with its specific existence or non-existent with its specific non-existence except after observing that extraneous premise. So it is not a rational division. It is investigated because rational division is that in which the intellect is certain of exclusivity merely by conceptualizing the two extremes as they truly are, but if they are not conceptualized in their true conceptualization, lack of certainty does not undermine rational division, as is the nature [of things].
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the equivocality of existence. It begins by stating that there is a clear exclusive division between a specific essence (al-haqiqa al-makhsusa) and its removal (raf'uha). It then argues that if existence is additional to its instances, then each specific existence has a corresponding negation, and the division between that existence and its negation is a rational division (h.sr 'aqli). The commentator then explains that the statement 'And if existence...' was added by the commentator to address a possibility, even though it is not the view of those who claim equivocality. The next section deals with the need for a unified concept of non-existence. It is argued that the division between specific existence and its removal includes two possibilities: being existent with a different existence, or being non-existent. This shows that the unity of non-existence is needed for the inference. An objection is raised that the division meant by 'the thing is either existent or non-existent' is about absolute existence and its total removal, not about removing a specific existence. The objection concludes that the proof must mention the unity of non-existence or define non-existence as incompatible with existence. The proposed solution is that if existence is not univocal, then non-existence is either one or many; in either case, the intended rational division fails. The passage then introduces the fourth aspect as an argument by a scholar, but immediately digresses into an objection against the third aspect's claim of rational division. The objection states that the division between specific existence and its negation is not purely rational because it requires an extraneous premise: that a thing cannot be existent with another's existence or non-existent with another's non-existence. Without this premise, the intellect does not immediately see the exclusivity. The commentator defends the rational division by explaining that if the two extremes are conceptualized correctly, the intellect does judge; the objection's scenario involves a deficiency in conceptualization.

Correction: The passage shows that without a unified concept of non-existence, the division allows intermediate possibilities (being existent with another existence), thus invalidating its rationality.

Objection and Defense: Is the Division Purely Rational?
Presents the objection that the rational division requires an extraneous premise and the defense that proper conceptualization suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the division between specific existence and its negation requires an extraneous premise and is therefore not purely rational, and how the commentator defends its rationality.

Core Idea
A rational division requires only conceptualization, not extraneous premises.

This passage continues the discussion of the third argument by addressing a serious objection. Some argue that the division between specific existence and its negation is not purely rational because it depends on an external assumption. The commentator defends that rational division is based on correct conceptualization, and lack of certainty due to misapprehension does not negate its rational character.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
The passage begins with a reference to a fourth aspect, but quickly turns to an objection against the third aspect's claim of rational division. Identify the objection: the division requires an extraneous premise.
The extraneous premise is that a thing cannot be existent with another's existence or non-existent with another's non-existence.
2
Understand the Zayd example
The objector gives an example: 'Zayd is non-existent with his specific non-existence' is not equivalent to 'Zayd is not existent with his specific existence' without the extraneous premise. Work through the example to see the difference.
If Zayd exists with another existence, he is not existent with his specific existence, but he is not non-existent with his specific non-existence.
3
See the objector's conclusion
The objector concludes that the intellect only judges exclusivity in the formulation without specific existence/removal, and thus the division is not rational.
The objector claims that the division needs an external premise, so it's not purely rational.
4
Study the defense
The commentator responds that rational division is based on proper conceptualization of the two extremes. If the extremes are correctly conceptualized, the intellect judges exclusivity immediately.
Lack of certainty due to not conceptualizing correctly does not undermine rational division.
5
Reflect on the outcome
Understand that the defense preserves the rationality of the division, but also shows that the objection misapplies the criterion.
The debate highlights the importance of precise conceptualization in kalam.

Between the specific truth and its removal is an exclusive division without doubt, and if existence is additional to its multiplicity, then for each specific existence there is a thing that removes it corresponding to it, and the alternation between that existence and its removal is a rational division, just as the alternation between absolute existence (if it is assumed to be established) and its removal is a rational division. His statement: 'And if existence etc.' The commentator added this due to a possibility, even though it is not the opinion of those who claim equivocality, and therefore he did not mention it in the text to show the strength of the answer. His statement: 'And the alternation etc.' For the removal of that existence includes that it is an existence with a different existence from that specific existence, and that it is non-existent. And by this it appears that the unity of the concept of non-existence has an entry in the inference, and what was said is repelled: that if the concept of non-existence were multiple, the division would be invalidated by two possibilities: the possibility of it being existent with another existence, and the possibility of it being non-existent with another non-existence. So the mention of the unity of non-existence is superfluous. But it is objected against him that this division is not the intended division from our statement: The thing is either existent or non-existent. For the purpose of it is the division into existence and the removal of existence entirely, not the removal of specific existence, such that it does not contradict its being qualified by another existence, as is not hidden. And from this it appears that in the mentioned proof, mention of the unity of non-existence is necessary, or that the intended meaning of non-existence is a meaning that does not coexist with the existent, so that if existence is equivocal in word, the mentioned division is invalidated. The more appropriate is to say: If existence is not equivocal in meaning, then non-existence is either a single concept or multiple according to the multiplicity of existences. In either case, the rational division intended from our statement: The thing is either existent or non-existent is invalidated. As for the first, because of the possibility of an intermediary by being existent with another existence. As for the second, because then it is a division between existence and non-existence in the sense that coexists with existence, and that is not intended. His statement: 'The fourth aspect: he said etc.' And if this proposition is necessary, then equivocality is established a fortiori. So this is an inference from knowledge of the self-evidence of the proposition to knowledge of its establishment, and that does not contradict that self-evidence is a branch. His statement: 'And the alternation between that existence and its removal is a rational division' It is objected against him that rational division is that in which the intellect passes judgment on account of its consideration of it, and there is judgment of the intellect by means of an extraneous premise: that the thing cannot be existent with the existence of another, nor non-existent with the non-existence of another. For if one sets aside this premise, our statement 'Zayd is non-existent with his specific non-existence' is not equivalent in meaning to our statement 'he is not existent with his specific existence', but rather is more specific. For if Zayd is existent with another existence or non-existent with another non-existence, it is true that he is not existent with his specific existence, and false that he is non-existent with his specific non-existence. Thus the intellect is certain of the exclusivity in our statement: the thing is either existent with its specific existence or it is not existent with its specific existence, but it is not certain of the exclusivity in our statement: the thing is either existent with its specific existence or non-existent with its specific non-existence except after observing that extraneous premise. So it is not a rational division. It is investigated because rational division is that in which the intellect is certain of exclusivity merely by conceptualizing the two extremes as they truly are, but if they are not conceptualized in their true conceptualization, lack of certainty does not undermine rational division, as is the nature [of things].

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the equivocality of existence. It begins by stating that there is a clear exclusive division between a specific essence (al-haqiqa al-makhsusa) and its removal (raf'uha). It then argues that if existence is additional to its instances, then each specific existence has a corresponding negation, and the division between that existence and its negation is a rational division (h.sr 'aqli). The commentator then explains that the statement 'And if existence...' was added by the commentator to address a possibility, even though it is not the view of those who claim equivocality. The next section deals with the need for a unified concept of non-existence. It is argued that the division between specific existence and its removal includes two possibilities: being existent with a different existence, or being non-existent. This shows that the unity of non-existence is needed for the inference. An objection is raised that the division meant by 'the thing is either existent or non-existent' is about absolute existence and its total removal, not about removing a specific existence. The objection concludes that the proof must mention the unity of non-existence or define non-existence as incompatible with existence. The proposed solution is that if existence is not univocal, then non-existence is either one or many; in either case, the intended rational division fails. The passage then introduces the fourth aspect as an argument by a scholar, but immediately digresses into an objection against the third aspect's claim of rational division. The objection states that the division between specific existence and its negation is not purely rational because it requires an extraneous premise: that a thing cannot be existent with another's existence or non-existent with another's non-existence. Without this premise, the intellect does not immediately see the exclusivity. The commentator defends the rational division by explaining that if the two extremes are conceptualized correctly, the intellect does judge; the objection's scenario involves a deficiency in conceptualization.

Correction: The defense clarifies that rational division depends on proper conceptualization; the objector's scenario fails to conceptualize the extremes correctly, so the lack of certainty does not undermine rationality.

The Fifth Argument and Its Refutation: Wujud as Shared
Presents a fifth argument against wujud being shared and its refutation via a negative proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the argument that one who denies wujud is shared implicitly admits it, and how the refutation uses a negative proposition to avoid that implication.

Core Idea
Denying a shared meaning still requires conceiving it – but a negative proposition sidesteps the trap.

This lesson presents a sophisticated dialectical exchange within kalam. An opponent argues that anyone who claims wujud is not shared has already assumed a single concept of wujud, thereby admitting it is shared. The refutation cleverly rephrases the claim as a negative proposition, avoiding the need for an actual shared entity. Understanding this move sharpens your grasp of logical tactics in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's argument (from 'الوجه الخامس' to 'فقد لزمه الاعتراف بأن الوجود مشترك'). Notice how he builds his case: the opponent's single proof implies a single concept, and judging that concept as 'not shared' forces him to admit its reality.
The objector uses a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's own method contradicts his position.
2
Understand the underlying logic
Identify the key logical move: if a proof is one and covers many instances, the claim must be general. Generality requires a single meaning (ma'na) common to all instances. Thus, the opponent must hold that such a meaning exists, making wujud shared.
The argument hinges on the relationship between proof (dalil) and claim (da'wa).
3
Read the refutation
Now read the answer (والجواب...). The author (Sanusi) responds by taking the claim as a negative proposition: 'there is no shared meaning called existence'. This is a saliba mu'addala, which negates the predicate altogether.
A negative proposition does not require the subject term to exist in reality; it only needs to be conceived.
4
Grasp the refutation's force
The refutation avoids the objector's trap: by framing the denial as a negative, the opponent does not need to affirm any positive shared entity. The concept of 'existence' is merely entertained as a mental assumption, not as something real.
This preserves the position that wujud is not shared in external reality.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how logical precision (especially the type of proposition) can dissolve theological dilemmas. This is a classic example of using logical tools to defend a position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس قال ذلك البعض من الفضلاء من زعم أن الوجود غير مشترك فقد اعترف بأنه مشترك من حيث لا يدري إذ لولا أنه تصور مفهوماً واحداً شاملاً لجميع الموجودات يحكم عليه بأنه غير مشترك بين الموجودات للزمه البرهان في كل وجود أنه كذلك. وإذا لم تكن الدعوى عامة لها لم يمكن إثباتها بدليل واحد عام لأن تلك الدعوى حينئذ متعددة بحسب المعنى كتعدد تلك الأمور فلا بد لكل واحدة من تلك الدعاوي من برهان على حدة. والحاصل أن الدليل إذا كان واحداً متناولاً لمتعدد فلا بد أن تكون الدعوى عامة متناولة لذلك المتعدد وعمومها إياه إنما يكون بأخذ معنى واحد عام لجميعه إذ لولاه لوجب التعرض لخصوصية كل واحد من ذلك المتعدد. فمن قال إن الوجود غير مشترك فلا شك أن حكمه هذا غير مقتصر على وجود واحد بل يتناول كل وجود فلو كان مفهوم الوجود مختلفاً لاحتاج ذلك القائل إلى أن يبرهن على كل واحد واحد من وجودات الماهيات أنه غير مشترك لاستحالة أن ينطبق الدليل الواحد على متعدد باعتبار خصوصية كل واحد منه لكنه معترف بأن حجته على أن الوجود غير مشترك تتناول كل وجود فلا بد له أن يتصور معنى واحداً متناولاً للوجودات بأسرها وقد حكم على ذلك المعنى بحكم إيجابي صادق هو أنه غير مشترك فلا بد أن يكون ذلك المعنى متحققاً فقد لزمه الاعتراف بأن الوجود مشترك. والجواب أنا نأخذ الدعوى سالبة لا موجبة معدولة فنقول لا يوجد معنى مشترك فيه بينها يسمى الوجود وذلك لا يقتضي وجوداً مشتركاً بينها بل يكفيها تصور وجود كذلك.
The fifth aspect: one of the excellent scholars said: Whoever claims that existence is not shared has admitted that it is shared without realizing it. For if he did not conceive a single concept encompassing all existents and judge it as not shared among existents, he would be required to prove for every existence that it is likewise. And if the claim is not general for them, it cannot be proven by a single general proof, because that claim would then be multiple in meaning according to the multiplicity of those things, so each one of those claims requires a separate proof. In summary, if the proof is single and covers multiple things, then the claim must be general and cover that multiplicity, and its generality over them is only by taking a single meaning general to all of them; otherwise, it would be necessary to address the particularity of each one of that multiplicity. So whoever says that existence is not shared, his judgment is undoubtedly not restricted to one existence but rather includes every existence. If the concept of existence were different, that claimant would need to prove for each and every one of the existences of essences that it is not shared, because it is impossible for a single proof to apply to multiple things with regard to the particularity of each. But he admits that his proof that existence is not shared includes every existence, so he must conceive a single concept encompassing all existences, and he has judged that concept by a true affirmative judgment that it is not shared. Therefore, that concept must be realized, so he is compelled to admit that existence is shared. The answer: we take the claim as a negative, not an affirmative modalized [proposition], and we say: there is no shared meaning among them called existence, and that does not necessitate a shared existence among them; rather, it suffices for him to conceive an existence in that manner.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within the discussion of whether wujud (existence) is mushtarak (shared). The passage is an argument-and-response unit. The objector (a scholar) argues that anyone who claims wujud is not shared must implicitly admit that it is shared. The reasoning: to deny that wujud is shared, one must have a single concept (mafhum) of wujud that includes all instances (afrad). If the concept were different for each instance, a separate proof would be needed for each existence, but the opponent offers a single proof covering all existences, thus he must be using a single concept. He then makes a positive judgment about this concept—that it is not shared—which requires that concept to have actual existence (in the mind or in reality). Therefore, he is forced to admit that there is a shared concept of wujud, i.e., wujud is shared in meaning. The author's answer (al-jawab) is to take the claim as a saliba mu'addala (a negative proposition with a negated predicate), meaning the claim is that there is no shared meaning called existence among the instances. This does not require the existence of a shared concept; it only requires conceiving of existence in a certain way, which can be a mere mental assumption without external reality. Thus, the opponent is not forced into admitting that wujud is shared.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
مشترك (mushtarak)
معنى (ma'na)
مفهوم (mafhum)
سالبة (saliba)
معدولة (mu'addala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the answer denies the very concept of a shared meaning of wujud, completely rejecting any commonality.
Correction: The answer does not deny the concept; it only denies that the concept has any external or mental reality. It allows the concept to be entertained hypothetically as a tool for argument, but it does not commit to a shared existence.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does the one who claims wujud is not shared implicitly admit it is shared?
A) Because he uses a single proof covering all existences, requiring a single concept.
B) Because he cannot conceive of existence without a shared meaning.
C) Because he must prove each existence separately.
D) Because he admits the concept is mental only.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, the type of proposition (affirmative or negative) can determine whether a concept is required to have reality, and this passage demonstrates how a negative claim can defuse a theological argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm something about God—like 'He is existent'—do you ever stop to check whether that concept of 'existence' is truly shared between Creator and creation? What would change if you chose a negative formulation instead?
?
Is there a belief you cling to that, upon honest examination, commits you to a shared concept you never intended? Are you ready to reframe it as a negative proposition to avoid that trap?
?
This lesson shows that even a denial can inadvertently affirm what it denies. In your own life, where might you be asserting something without realizing the hidden commitments? Will you have the courage to re-examine the logical form of your statements?

The Impossibility of Two Existences for One Thing
Refutes the claim that existence is merely verbally common by showing impossibility of two existences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is necessarily impossible for a single essence to have two existences, and how this refutes the view that existence is merely verbally common.

Core Idea
One thing cannot have two existences—this is a rational impossibility.

This passage addresses a key metaphysical dispute: whether existence is a univocal concept or merely a common word. Some argued that existence is only verbally common, implying that each thing could have multiple existences. Sanusi refutes this by showing that one essence cannot be two essences or exist twice, making the view impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: اشتراك لفظي (verbal commonality) and معلوم الانتفاء (known impossibility).
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage considers two cases: existence identical to essence, and existence additional to essence. In both, the author argues that one thing cannot have two existences.
3
Understand the reasoning
Focus on the argument: if existence were only verbally common, each thing would require two existences. But one essence cannot be two essences or exist twice, so the claim is impossible.
The commentator clarifies that 'known impossibility' means necessary impossibility, not just improbable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك وجودان وكون الشيء الواحد له وجودان وإن كان الوجود نفس الحقيقة أو زائداً عليها معلوم الانتفاء بالضرورة لامتناع أن تكون الحقيقة الواحدة حقيقتين أو أن تكون موجودة بوجودين وإن كانا زائدين عليها وأما من قال : ليس الوجود بمشترك معنى بل هو مشترك بين الكل اشتراكاً لفظياً فهم القائلون بأنه وجودان، وهذا ممتنع لامتناع تعدد ماهية الشيء ووجود الشيء مرتين.
قوله : معلوم الانتفاء معناه معلوم امتناعه كما يدل عليه تعليل الشارح، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار حذف المضاف أي وجواز كون الشيء إلخ، أو اعتبار أن الممكن لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال.
The First Observatory – Second Purpose: On it being common. Two existences and that one thing has two existences, even if existence is the essence or additional to it, its non-existence is necessarily known due to the impossibility of one essence being two essences or of it existing with two existences even if they are additional to it. As for the one who said that existence is not common in meaning but is verbally common among all, they are the ones who say that it is two existences, and this is impossible because of the impossibility of a thing's quiddity being multiple and a thing existing twice.
His saying: 'known to be impossible' means known to be impossible, as indicated by the commentator's reasoning, so there is no need to consider omission of the annexed term, i.e., the permissibility of a thing etc., or to consider that the possible does not necessarily entail its occurrence being impossible.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that existence is merely verbally common (ishtirak lafzi) among all things. The argument proceeds as follows: If existence were only common in word, then each thing would require two existences—one for the essence and one as an additional attribute—since existence cannot be identical to the essence in all cases. But a single essence cannot have two existences; this is impossible by necessity. The impossibility is explained as either the impossibility of one essence being two essences or the impossibility of a thing existing twice. The commentator then clarifies the phrase 'known to be impossible' by stating that it means known to be impossible (mumtani'), as shown by the reasoning, and that there is no need to assume a hidden noun or to argue that the possible does not entail a contradiction in its occurrence.

Key Terms
اشتراك لفظي
معلوم الانتفاء
تعدد الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a thing can have multiple existences, e.g., in the mind and in the external world, or that existence is merely a common word allowing multiple attributions.
Correction: The passage argues that one essence cannot have two existences simultaneously; even if existence is considered an additional attribute, multiplicity of existence for a single quiddity is impossible. Existence is univocal in meaning, not merely verbally common.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for rejecting the view that existence is merely verbally common?
A) Existence cannot be conceived.
B) One thing would then have to have two existences, which is impossible.
C) The philosophers denied it.
D) It contradicts divine unity.

Key Takeaway
A single essence cannot have two existences; this necessary impossibility refutes the claim that existence is merely verbally common.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single essence cannot have two existences, then how can you claim to worship only one God while your heart gives equal consideration to other 'sources' of security, success, or identity? What would it take to remove that second existence from your life?
?
The argument refutes those who treat the word 'existence' as merely a label. When you say 'Allah exists' and 'the world exists', do you truly mean the same reality of existence, or do you empty the word of real content? How would your dua change if you affirmed existence as truly shared?
?
You know rationally that one thing cannot be two. Yet you often try to live as two people: one who believes and one who doubts, one who obeys and one who indulges. Which of those two existences must be eliminated for you to be a single, sincere servant of Allah?

The First Proof and Its Two Refutations
First proof for existence-essence identity and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the identity of existence and essence in Ash'ari kalam and the two ways it is refuted: the counterexample (naqd) and the solution (hall).

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction: if existence were added, essence would become a contradiction.

This passage presents the opening argument of a major early debate: Is existence identical to essence or added to it? Al-Ash'ari and Abu Husayn al-Basri offer a proof that existence cannot be additional, because that would lead to a contradiction. You will see how they reason from the impossibility of a middle state between existence and non-existence, and then consider two responses that defenders of the addition view (like the author) give to counter this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Follow the logical chain: assume existence is additional → essence in itself is non-existent → annexing existence to non-existent yields contradiction.
Notice the key premise: no middle ground between existence and non-existence.
2
Examine the first answer (naqd)
This is a counterexample using another accident (blackness). Ask: does the same logic force us to deny that blackness is additional? If not, the proof fails.
This shows the proof is not specific to existence.
3
Study the second answer (hall)
Here the premise that essence in itself is non-existent is rejected. Instead, essence is neither existent nor non-existent in mental consideration. Understand why this avoids contradiction.
This is a subtle point: it is a mental abstraction, not a real third state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها للشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري وأبي الحسين البصري ) من المعتزلة ( أنه نفس الحقيقة في (الكل) أي الواجب والممكنات كافة (لوجوه ) ثلاثة الأول لو كان ( الوجود ( زائداً ) على الماهية ) كانت الماهية من حيث هي هي غير موجودة ) أي إذا اعتبرت الماهية في حد ذاتها مع قطع النظر عن جميع ما هو خارج عنها لم تكن موجودة ( فكانت معدومة ) إذ لا واسطة بينهما ، ( فيلزم) حينئذ من انضمام الوجود إليها وقيامه بها ( اتصاف المعدوم ) الذي هو الماهية ( بالوجود وإنه تناقض ) إذ تكون الماهية حينئذ معدومة موجودة معا ) والجواب من وجهين) الأول ( النقض بسائر الأعراض الزائدة ) على معروضاتها بلا اشتباه فيقال : لو كان السواد مثلاً زائداً على الجسم كان الجسم من حيث هو غير أسود فإذا انضم إليه السواد لزم اتصاف الجسم الذي ليس بأسود بالسواد فيلزم أن يكون ذلك الجسم أسود وليس بأسود معاً، وإنه تناقض (و) الثاني (الحل) وهو أن الماهية من حيث هي لا موجودة ولا معدومة كما سيأتي في المرصد الثاني ) وكل منهما ) أي من الوجود والعدم (أمر) زائد عليها ( ينضم إليها ) فقولنا : الماهية من حيث هي لا موجودة ولا معدومة، نعني به أنها ليست عين الوجود ولا عين العدم وأنه ليس شيء منهما داخلا فيها بل كل واحد منهما زائد عليها، فإذا اعتبر معها الوجود كانت موجودة وإذا اعتبر معها العدم كانت معدومة وإذا لم يعتبر معها شيء منهما لم يكن أن يحكم عليها بأنها موجودة أو معدومة، ولا نعني به أن الماهية منفكة عنهما معاً، حتى يلزم الواسطة وتلخيصه أن
عليه بالنظر إلى انتفاء الواسطة غاية ما في الباب أنه يلزم أن تكون معدومة وأن لا تكون معدومة، فيكون زيادة الوجود لاستلزامه للنقيضين باطلاً على أن عدم ظهور ترتبه عليه ممنوع، لجواز أن يكون باعتبار كون العدم جزءاً له أو نفسه . قوله (الحل) أي منع مقدمة معينة وهي قوله : فكانت أي الماهية من حيث هي معدومة يمنع لزوم الواسطة فإنا نعني بقولنا : الماهية من حيث هي ليست بموجودة ولا معدومة أنه ليس شيء منهما في مرتبة الماهية في الملاحظة العقلية لعدم كونهما نفس الماهية أو داخلاً فيها، ففيه ارتفاع النقيضين في الملاحظة ولا استحالة فيه ولا نعني به أنها منفكة عنهما حتى يلزم ثبوت الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم .
One of them is for the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila: that it is the essence itself in all, i.e., the Necessary and all contingent things, based on three proofs. The first: if existence were additional to essence, then the essence considered in itself would be non-existent, i.e., if the essence is considered in its very self disregarding everything external to it, it would not be existent, so it would be non-existent, since there is no intermediary between them. Then it would follow from the annexation of existence to it and its subsistence in it, that the non-existent (which is the essence) is described by existence, and that is a contradiction, because then the essence would be non-existent and existent simultaneously. The answer is in two ways: first, the counterexample by the other accidental attributes added to their subjects without ambiguity, e.g., if blackness were additional to the body, the body considered in itself would not be black; then if blackness attaches to it, it would follow that the body which is not black is described by blackness, so that body would be black and not black simultaneously, which is a contradiction. Second, the solution: that the essence in itself is neither existent nor non-existent, as will come in the second observatory. And each of them, i.e., existence and non-existence, is something additional to it that attaches to it. So our statement that the essence in itself is neither existent nor non-existent means that it is neither identical to existence nor to non-existence, and that neither of them is internal to it, but each is additional to it. So if existence is considered with it, it is existent; if non-existence is considered with it, it is non-existent; and if neither is considered with it, it cannot be judged as existent or non-existent. We do not mean by it that the essence is separated from both together, which would necessitate an intermediate state. And its summary is that regarding it, considering the absence of an intermediary, the most that can be said is that it would be non-existent and not non-existent, so the addition of existence (because it necessitates opposites) is invalid. But the point that its consequence does not appear is not admitted, because it is possible that it is due to considering non-existence as a part or as identical to it. His statement: 'the solution' i.e., refuting a specific premise, namely his statement: so it i.e., the essence in itself is non-existent. He rejects the implication of an intermediary. For we mean by our statement that the essence in itself is neither existent nor non-existent, that neither of them is at the level of the essence in mental observation because they are not identical to the essence nor included in it. So in this there is a removal of the two opposites in observation, and there is no impossibility in it. And we do not mean by it that it is separated from them such that an intermediate between the existent and non-existent would be required.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the school that holds existence is identical to essence (the school of Ash'ari and Abu Husayn al-Basri). The proof is an argument from contradiction: if existence were additional to essence, then essence in itself would be non-existent (since there is no middle ground between existence and non-existence). Then when existence is attached to this non-existent essence, the non-existent would become qualified by existence, resulting in a contradiction of being both non-existent and existent simultaneously. The passage then gives a two-part answer to this proof. The first answer is a counterexample (naqd): other accidental attributes like blackness are additional to their subjects, and applying the same reasoning would lead to a similar contradiction, but these attributes are accepted as additional, so the proof fails. The second answer is the solution (hall): the essence in itself is neither existent nor non-existent; rather, existence and non-existence are both external to it. When neither is considered, the essence is not judged as either. This avoids the contradiction because the essence is not non-existent before the addition of existence; it simply has no determination. The commentary further explains that the claim of an intermediary is rejected because the state of being neither existent nor non-existent is a mental consideration, not a real intermediate state.

الماهية (al-mahiyyah)
الزيادة (al-ziyadah)
النقض (al-naqd)
الحل (al-hall)
الموجود (al-mawjud)
المعدوم (al-ma'dum)
التناقض (al-tanaqud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the 'neither existent nor non-existent' state is a real third state intermediate between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The text clarifies that this is only a mental consideration (considering essence independent of attributes), not a real ontological state. There is no third reality.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof given by the Ash'ari school for existence being identical to essence?
A) If existence were additional, then essence would be both existent and non-existent, a contradiction.
B) If existence were additional, then essence would be independent of its creator.
C) If existence were additional, then it would be impossible to predicate existence of God.
D) If existence were additional, then it would be an accident.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof that existence equals essence is refuted by showing that essence in itself is neither existent nor non-existent, thus avoiding the contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is not part of your essence; it is a gift from Allah every moment. Do you live as if your existence is a borrowed gift, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you truly believed that every breath is a new act of creation?
?
If your 'self' is neutral in itself—neither existent nor non-existent—then your identity is wholly dependent on Allah's will. Are you comfortable with that complete dependence, or do you try to build a self-sufficient identity through achievements, possessions, or relationships?
?
The proof's refutation shows that without Allah's granting existence, you are nothing. Does your daily life reflect that reality in your prayers, your gratitude, and your reliance on Him? What one habit could you change to make that dependence more conscious?

Objection and Answer on Existence as Superadded
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and two-part answer on existence as superadded to quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection that superadded existence leads to a contradiction, and how al-Jurjani responds using the methods of naqḍ (counterexample) and ḥall (solution) to defend the superaddition view.

Core Idea
Existence is not contradictory to quiddity because quiddity is inherently neutral.

This passage tackles a critical objection to the Ashʿarī view that existence is superadded to quiddity. The objection claims that this leads to a contradiction: the quiddity would be both existent and non-existent simultaneously. Al-Jurjani defends the view with a two-part answer—first a counterexample (naqḍ) showing the objection proves too much, and then a solution (ḥall) clarifying the neutral status of quiddity. Understanding this defense is essential for grasping the Ashʿarī ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the reasoning: if quiddity in itself is non-existent (since no intermediate), then superadding existence would make it both existent and non-existent—a contradiction.
Notice the assumption that there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence.
2
Study the first answer (naqḍ)
Al-Jurjani uses a counterexample: blackness superadded to a body. The same reasoning would apply, so the objection proves too much and is invalid.
This shows the objection would refute all superadded accidents, not just existence.
3
Study the second answer (ḥall)
The quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent; both are superadded. No contradiction arises because we do not consider quiddity as non-existent before attachment.
Neutrality does not mean a third state; it means neither attribute is intrinsic.
4
Review the implications
Reflect on how this preserves the superaddition view without logical inconsistency, and note the distinction between intellectual consideration and external reality.
The attachment of existence is at the time of existence, not before—avoiding 'attainment of the attained'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الحاصل أو حال كونها معدومة، فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين.
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is the quiddity itself or a part of it. 'Existent' meaning if the quiddity is considered in itself, disregarding everything external to it, it is not existent; thus it is non-existent, since there is no intermediate between them. Then it would follow from the attachment of existence to it and its subsistence by it that the non-existent (which is the quiddity) is described by existence, and that is a contradiction, since the quiddity would then be both non-existent and existent together. The answer is from two aspects: First, the counterexample (naqḍ) by other superadded accidents upon their subjects without confusion. For example, if blackness were superadded to body, the body in itself would be not black; then when blackness attaches to it, it would be necessary that the body which is not black be described by blackness, so that body would be both black and not black together, and that is a contradiction. Second, the solution (ḥall): that the quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent, as will come in the Second Observatory. Each of them (existence and non-existence) is a matter superadded to it, attaching to it. So our statement 'the quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent' means by it that it is not identical to existence nor to non-existence, and that neither of them is included in it, but each is superadded to it. So if existence is considered with it, it is existent; if non-existence is considered with it, it is non-existent; and if neither is considered with it, it is not possible to judge it as existent or non-existent. We do not mean by it that the quiddity is separate from both, such that an intermediate would be necessary. Summary: Regarding the denial of an intermediate, the utmost in this matter is that it would be necessary that it be non-existent and that it not be non-existent, so the superaddition of existence would be invalid because it entails a contradiction, unless it is said that the non-appearance of its entailment is not conceded, because it is possible that it is by considering non-existence as a part of it or itself. His statement (the solution) i.e., preventing a specific premise, namely his statement 'thus it is non-existent'—i.e., the quiddity in itself is non-existent—he prevents the necessity of an intermediate. For we mean by our statement 'the quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent' that neither of them is in the rank of the quiddity in intellectual consideration, because they are not the quiddity itself nor included in it. So in it there is a removal of the two contradictories in consideration, and there is no impossibility in it, and we do not mean by it that it is separate from both such that the existence of an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent would be necessary. His statement 'it is not possible to judge it, etc.' not that it is not qualified by either. His statement 'it attaches to the quiddity' and this attachment is only in the intellect, meaning that the intellect when considering... His statement 'disregarding everything external to it' there is discussion in it, because if the qualification of 'in itself' is interpreted by this, his statement 'thus it is non-existent' does not appear, because non-existence is external to it like existence. So if one disregards everything external to it, its being qualified by non-existence is not more likely than its being qualified by existence. So it is better to say in its interpretation: disregarding the attachment of existence to it. And it can be defended by saying that the mentioned interpretation is based on what is understood from his statement 'in itself', and as for his statement 'thus it is non-existent', it is argumentative and based on the denial of an intermediate, and many such examples exist, and what I have mentioned is the gist of the answer he mentioned. Existence attaches to the quiddity alone, not to the quiddity taken with non-existence, so that a contradiction would be necessary, nor to the quiddity taken with existence, so that it would be necessary that it be existent before its existence. In other words, it attaches to it not conditionally that it is existent nor conditionally that it is non-existent, but at the time of its being existent with this existence, not with another existence, all of this analogously to the attachment of accidents to their locus. His statement 'but at the time of its being existent, etc.' is a turning away from the content of the expression, and in it is an indication of repelling what was said: that the attachment of existence, if it is at the time of existence, necessitates the attainment of the attained, and if it is at the time of non-existence, necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories. The gist of the repelling is choosing the first part and denying the impossibility of attaining the attained, because it is attaining the attained by this attainment, and that is not impossible; only what is attained before this attainment is impossible. His statement 'but at the time of its being existent with this existence' the turning away pertains to both of those expressions together, not only the second, and in it is an indication of the answer to what the arguer returns to and says: its occurrence to the quiddity is either when it is existent, so it necessitates attaining the attained, or when it is non-existent, so it necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the view that existence is superadded to quiddity, followed by a two-part answer. The objection argues that if the quiddity in itself is considered without any external considerations, it is non-existent (since there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence). Then if existence attaches to it, the non-existent quiddity would become qualified by existence, which is a contradiction because it would be both non-existent and existent simultaneously. The answer is from two aspects. First, the counterexample (naqḍ): if blackness were superadded to a body, the body in itself is not black; then blackness attaching to it would make the non-black body black, leading to a similar contradiction. This shows that the same reasoning would apply to all accidents, so the objection proves too much. Second, the solution (ḥall): the quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent; it is neutral. Existence and non-existence are both superadded to it. When existence is considered, it is existent; when non-existence, it is non-existent; when neither is considered, no judgment is made. This does not imply a third state between existence and non-existence, because the quiddity is not separate from both; rather, it is simply not intrinsically qualified. The commentary then explains the solution in detail, addressing potential misunderstandings: the quiddity's neutrality is in intellectual consideration, not in external reality. The attachment of existence is not to the quiddity as non-existent or existent beforehand, but to the quiddity without any condition. The discussion of timing (whether existence attaches when the quiddity is existent or non-existent) is resolved by choosing the first option (when it is existent with this existence) and denying that this is a case of attaining the attained, because it is a new attainment through this particular existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'quiddity is neither existent nor non-existent' implies a third state between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The author clarifies that it only means neither existence nor non-existence is intrinsic to quiddity; there is no third state.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the 'ḥall' (solution) in response to the objection that superadded existence would make the quiddity both existent and non-existent?
A) The quiddity is essentially existent
B) The quiddity is essentially non-existent
C) The quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent
D) The quiddity is both existent and non-existent simultaneously

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani defends the superaddition of existence by showing that quiddity is intrinsically neutral, so attaching existence does not create a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence is nothing without Allah's gift of existence, why do you still feel proud of your achievements as if you were their source? How will you remind yourself today that your next breath is a superadded gift?
?
The objection assumes quiddity is non-existent, but the truth is it is neutral. Are there areas of your life where you view yourself as either utterly worthless or completely self-sufficient, instead of recognizing that your value and existence come entirely from Allah? What specific step can you take to live in that dependence?
?
The naqḍ argument shows that the same problem would apply to all attributes. If all your good qualities are given by Allah, is there any attribute you secretly claim as your own? How can you practice attributing every good thing to its true Giver today?

The Attribute of Priority in Nonexistent Causes
Sanusi argues that priority of the part can be conceptual, not existential, and defends this against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of priority can be attached to a cause even when it is nonexistent, thereby showing that causal priority need not be existential.

Core Idea
Priority is a relational attribute that can apply even to nonexistent causes – existence is not required.

This lesson examines a subtle argument about causal priority. The question is: must a cause be prior in existence to its effect? The author argues that the attribute of priority can be attached to a cause even when the cause does not yet exist, thereby showing that priority is not necessarily existential. This has important implications for understanding causation in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author uses the example of the constituent part (al-muqawwim) to make his point.
2
Identify the central argument
Find the claim that priority attaches to the part not in consideration of existence, and that this is sufficient for the denial (al-manʿ) of the opponent's claim. Understand why: because a cause can be described with priority while nonexistent, so its priority is not existential.
This is the key logical move.
3
Consider the objection and refutation
Analyze the objection that the priority aspect is an accident of the quiddity and thus the quiddity is prior. The refutation points out that this aspect does not exist externally, so does not need an external cause.
The distinction between external existence and mental conception is crucial.
4
Understand the philosophers' answer
Read the philosophers' answer that the intended cause is the efficient cause, whose priority in existence is self-evident. Note the objection that if a cause gives existence to itself, priority would be impossible.
This final part connects to broader causal theory.
