It attaches to the constituent (not in consideration of existence) because it is established for the constituent before it exists, except that we do not conceive it except in consideration of existence. (And that is) i.e. this which we have mentioned of the description of the constituent with priority over the effect in the state of its nonexistence is sufficient for us in (the ground of) the denial, since it has then been established that one of the causes has been described with priority over the effect in the state of its being nonexistent, so its priority over it is not in terms of existence, and it becomes permissible that the state of the efficient cause be likewise. And what is said that he intended that this aspect is established for the part in the state of its nonexistence, and it is one of its accidents and an effect of its quiddity, so its quiddity is prior to this aspect not in consideration of existence, and this amount suffices for us in the denial—it is nothing, because this aspect does not exist externally so that it needs an external cause, and our discussion is about it. His statement: (Because it is established etc.) There is an investigation in it, because if he intends that it is established for it before it exists externally and in the mind, then it is false, because the absolute nonexistent has nothing established for it; and if he intends before it exists externally, then it is granted, because priority is a relational attribute by which things are described in the mind, but it does not avail what is sought, namely its priority not in terms of existence absolutely. So the correct thing is to say instead: and if it is established for it in existence, and to distinguish between attachment in consideration of existence i.e. by its condition and between attachment in existence by making existence a container for it. For in the first there is an entry into existence unlike the second. And you may say: the commentator intends by his statement: before it exists, before existence is considered with it, so it returns to what we said, except that his statement 'in the state of its nonexistence' prevents that. His statement: (We do not conceive it except in consideration of existence) because it is an expression of the priority of the part whenever they exist, and this is like possibility established for the quiddity before existence, although it is not conceived except in relation to existence. His statement: (in the state of its nonexistence) you have known what is in it. His statement: (sufficient in the denial) i.e. we have no need to prove that it is not conceived in relation to existence. His statement: (And what is said) i.e. in directing the answer. His statement: (that this aspect is established etc.) So the meaning of his statement 'it is priority' is that it is prior to the existence of the part. His statement: (and an effect of its quiddity) because every accident needs its subject. His statement: (And this amount suffices for us etc.) and does not need to prove the priority of the part as such over the [whole]. His statement: (to an external cause) i.e. existing externally. His statement: (and our discussion is about her) i.e. about the cause existing externally, because the arguer said: everything that is a cause for the existence of a thing externally must be prior in existence, and the objector denied that it be prior in existence. His statement: (and our discussion is about her) i.e. about the external cause, because external existence, even if it is not an external existent, yet the description of the quiddity with it in reality and its thereby becoming existent externally, needs the external maker absolutely, unlike the description with the mentioned aspect. So the difference between them became clear, even though each of them shares in not being an external existent. And the commentator confined himself in explaining the non-need of the aspect for the external cause to denying its existence externally, while it needs to explain that the description with it also does not need that cause, because the speaker made the aspect itself an effect of the part, which is necessitated by its being one of the accidents of the part, as is not hidden, so reflect. His statement: (This directing is not suitable because the aspect according to this directing is not identical to priority, as indicated by the author's statement 'so this aspect is priority'—how, and its being identical to posteriority is closer in this directing than its being identical to priority, as is not hidden. His statement: (The philosophers answered etc.) The summary of the answer is that what is meant by our statement 'the cause is prior etc.' is the efficient cause, and the priority of the efficient cause over its effect in existence is known by necessity and does not admit of denial, because reason judges by intuition that the rank of bringing into existence is after the rank of existence; nay, even dumb animals are certain of it, and for this, when they hear a sound they flee from it based on the fact that its existence requires an existing cause, perhaps that may harm us. His statement: (The philosophers answered etc.) Reference has already been made to what was said against it: that we do not concede that what gives existence to itself necessitates its priority over it in existence, for there is no meaning of 'giving' here except that that quiddity necessitates existence by its essence, and its priority in existence over it is impossible due to the impossibility of attaining the attained, as in the case of the recipient exactly.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument concerning whether the attribute of priority (al-taqaddum) can be predicated of a constituent part (al-muqawwim) when it is nonexistent, and whether this suffices to deny that the cause must be prior in existence. The author first asserts that the attribute of priority attaches to the constituent not in consideration of existence (la bi-iʿtibār al-wujūd), because it is established for it before it exists, even though we only conceive it in relation to existence. This is claimed to be sufficient grounds for denial (al-manʿ) of the opponent’s claim that every cause must be prior in existence. The reasoning is: if a cause can be described with priority when nonexistent, then its priority is not necessarily in terms of existence, so the same could hold for the efficient cause. An objection is then raised: this aspect (al-haythiyya) of priority is an accident of the part and an effect of its quiddity, so the quiddity would be prior to this aspect, but not in terms of existence—and this itself suffices for the denial. The author refutes this objection: the aspect in question does not exist externally, so it does not require an external cause; the discussion is about external causes. The passage then presents a detailed analysis of the commentator’s statements, distinguishing between priority in the mind and in external existence, and clarifying that the attribute of priority is a relational (iʿtibāriyya) description. The crucial point is that the needed priority is not absolute priority in existence, but a conceptual priority. Then the philosophers’ answer is given: the intended cause is the efficient cause (al-ʿilla al-fāʿiliyya), whose priority in existence over its effect is self-evident; even animals know it instinctively. An objection to this answer is noted: if the cause gives existence to itself, then priority is impossible because it would entail attaining what is already attained. The passage concludes with a reference to this objection, implying it requires further consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any attribute of priority must be in terms of existence; the passage clarifies that priority can be a non-existential relational attribute.
Correction: Priority is a relational (iʿtibāriyya) attribute that can be conceived even for a nonexistent, as in the case of the part's priority over the whole when both are nonexistent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it sufficient for the denial that the constituent part is described with priority even when nonexistent?
A) Because priority is always a real attribute in external existence regardless.
B) Because a cause can be described with priority when nonexistent, so priority need not be in terms of existence.
C) Because the constituent part is identical to existence, so priority is meaningless.
D) Because the philosophers proved that every cause must be prior in existence.

Key Takeaway
Priority is not inherently tied to existence; it can be a conceptual attribute, thus the causal priority of an efficient cause is a different matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own reliance on secondary causes (e.g., means, people, circumstances), do you subconsciously assign them existential priority, forgetting that all priority is merely conceptual and dependent on the Creator? How will you shift your trust today from causes to the sole Cause?
?
If the attribute of priority can exist in the mind without external existence, then what does that imply about your own mental constructs—like your assumptions about God's actions? Are you ready to let go of the false necessity of existential priority in your understanding of divine causality, and surrender to a God whose ways are not bound by your mental categories?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

The Proof that Existence is Not Identical to Quiddity
A proof that existence is an attribute added to quiddity, using a hypothetical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that existence (wujud) is neither identical to quiddity (mahiyya) nor a part of it, using a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be quiddity because quiddity accepts non-existence but existence does not.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic metaphysics. The author uses a conditional syllogism to show that existence must be an attribute added to quiddity. The proof hinges on the observation that quiddity considered by itself can be conceived as non-existent, whereas a quiddity that actually exists cannot. This lesson will unpack the logical structure of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional premise
First, note the conditional: if quiddity did not accept non-existence, it would be necessary in itself (wajib dhati). This sets up the background. Then observe that quiddity taken with existence repels non-existence, otherwise a thing could be existent and non-existent simultaneously.
This observation is key: it shows that quiddity with existence does not accept non-existence.
2
Follow the hypothetical syllogism
The core proof is a conditional syllogism: if existence were the essence or part of quiddity, then quiddity from its very self would not differ from quiddity taken with existence regarding acceptance of non-existence—both would reject it. But in reality, quiddity from itself accepts non-existence. Therefore, the antecedent is false.
The syllogism has two branches: identity and part. Both lead to the same contradiction.
3
Grasp the two assumptions
The author explains two cases: (1) If existence is identical to quiddity, then existence cannot accept its opposite (non-existence). (2) If existence is a part, then the composite (quiddity including existence) also rejects non-existence. Both contradict the fact that quiddity alone accepts non-existence.
Understanding these two assumptions clarifies why existence cannot be the very quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : ( لم تكن كذلك أي لم تكن الماهية من حيث هي مخالفة للمأخوذة مع الوجود بل كانت من حيث هي تأبى العدم أيضاً أي كما أن المأخوذة مع الوجود تأبى عنه، الوجود تأبى عنه، فصح الإضراب، وظهر معنى كلمة أيضاً بلا تكلف وليس قوله كذلك إشارة إلى قوله تقبل العدم حتى لا الإضراب لأن معنى لم تقبل العدم ومعنى تأبى العدم واحد ولا يصح قوله أيضاً لأن معناه حينئذ أنها لم تقبل العدم كما أنها لا تقبل شيئاً آخر، وحاصل الاستدلال قياس استثنائي صورته أنه لو كان الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزءها لم تكن الماهية من حيث هي هي مخالفة للماهية المأخوذة مع الوجود في قبول العدم، وعدم قبوله بل كانت متحدة معها في عدم القبول، والثاني باطل أما الملازمة فلما ذكره الشارح وأما بطلان التالي فلما ذكره المصنف من أن الماهية من حيث هي تقبل العدم والماهية المأخوذة لا تقبله فافهم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام . قوله : ( فلأن الوجود يأبى إلخ) كيف لا والماهية المعروضة له لا تقبله فكيف يقبل نفسه. قوله : ( مأخوذ مع الوجود) من حيث إنه موجود . قوله : ( لما مر من لزوم جواز كونها موجودة ومعدومة معاً.
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is the essence of quiddity or a part of it. And if [quiddity] does not accept non-existence, possibility (al-imkan) is removed from it and it becomes characterized by essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati). And there is no doubt that the possible quiddity, when taken with existence, repels it; otherwise it would be possible for it to be existent and non-existent simultaneously. And if existence were the essence of the possible quiddity or a part of it, it would not be so, but rather it would repel non-existence from its very self as well. For on the assumption that existence is identical to it, existence repels the acceptance of its opposite; and on the assumption that it is a part of it, then the quiddity would then be taken from its very self with existence, so it would not accept non-existence due to what has passed. His saying: 'repels it' means that the quiddity from its very self is different from the quiddity taken with existence in acceptance and non-acceptance. His saying: 'it would not be so' means that the quiddity from its very self is not different from that taken with existence, but rather it repels non-existence from its very self as well, just as that taken with existence repels it, so the diversion is correct, and the meaning of the word 'also' becomes clear without difficulty. And his saying 'so' does not refer to his saying 'it accepts non-existence' so that there is no diversion, because the meaning of 'it does not accept non-existence' and the meaning of 'it repels non-existence' are one, and his saying 'also' is not valid because its meaning then would be that it does not accept non-existence just as it does not accept something else. And the summary of the proof is a hypothetical syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) whose form is: if existence were the essence of quiddity or a part of it, then the quiddity from its very self would not be different from the quiddity taken with existence in accepting or not accepting non-existence, but rather it would be united with it in not accepting; and the second is false. As for the entailment, it is due to what the commentator mentioned; and as for the falsity of the consequent, it is due to what the author mentioned: that quiddity from its very self accepts non-existence while the taken quiddity does not accept it, so understand, for indeed feet have slipped in it. His saying: 'for existence repels, etc.' How not? And the quiddity that is its subject does not accept it, so how can it accept itself? His saying: 'taken with existence' meaning from the aspect that it is existent. His saying: 'due to what has passed of the necessity of the possibility of it being existent and non-existent simultaneously.'
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This is a kalam proof (istidlal) that existence (al-wujud) is neither identical to quiddity (al-mahiyya) nor a part of it. The argument begins by stating a conditional: if quiddity did not accept non-existence, it would become necessary in itself (wajib dhati). Then it affirms a fact: the possible quiddity when taken with existence repels non-existence, because otherwise it could be both existent and non-existent at once. The core of the proof is the hypothetical syllogism: if existence were the essence of quiddity or a part of it, then quiddity from its very self (min haythu hiya hiya) would not differ from quiddity taken with existence regarding acceptance of non-existence—both would reject it. But in reality, quiddity from its very self accepts non-existence, while quiddity taken with existence does not. Therefore, the conditional is false, so existence is not identical to nor a part of quiddity. The commentary explains the reasoning: on the first assumption (existence = quiddity), existence itself cannot accept its opposite; on the second (existence as part), the composite includes existence and thus rejects non-existence. The commentator then elaborates on the logical form, noting that the proof is a qiyas istithna'i (conditional syllogism) and warns against misunderstanding.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
إمكان (imkan)
وجوب ذاتي (wujub dhati)
قبول العدم (qubul al-'adam)
قياس استثنائي (qiyas istithna'i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that quiddity has real existence in the state of non-existence.
Correction: The proof only asserts that quiddity can be conceived (mentally) without existence; it does not claim extra-mental existence for quiddity in that state.

Checkpoint
What is the logical form of the proof that existence is not identical to quiddity?
A) A categorical syllogism
B) A hypothetical syllogism (qiyas istithna'i)
C) An inductive argument
D) A reductio ad absurdum

Key Takeaway
Quiddity's conceptual acceptance of non-existence proves that existence is an added attribute, not the essence or a part of quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of yourself, do you treat your existence as something you own intrinsically, or as a gift that could be taken away? How does that belief affect your daily choices?
?
If your quiddity (your 'what you are') can be conceived without existence, then everything you are depends entirely on the One who gives existence. Does this truth make you feel vulnerable or secure? Why?
?
The proof shows that existence is not part of your essence. So your existence is not self-sufficient. What would it look like today to truly live as if your existence is a constant, moment-by-moment gift from God? Name one concrete action you can take this hour to acknowledge that dependence.

The Two Meanings of Acceptance (Qubul)
The commentator distinguishes two meanings of 'acceptance' to refute a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between acceptance as real co-existence and acceptance as mere occurrence or removal, and how this refutes the proof that quiddity must be distinct from existence.

Core Idea
Acceptance of non-existence does not imply quiddity exists apart from existence.

The proof that quiddity must be distinct from existence because it 'accepts' non-existence depends on what 'acceptance' means. The commentator clarifies that there are two meanings: real attribute-requiring acceptance, and mere occurrence or removal. This distinction dissolves the argument and protects the doctrine that possible existents are identical to their existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
This is a response (ujiba) to a previous proof, so follow the logical flow carefully.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'acceptance'
Find where the commentator distinguishes between the real meaning of acceptance (requiring co-existence of acceptor and accepted) and acceptance as mere occurrence (turyan) or complete removal (irtifa').
The first meaning is dismissed because it would require affirming the non-existent, which is rejected.
3
Understand the refutation of the first meaning
See why the first meaning leads to a concession that the proof's conclusion is false: quiddity accepting non-existence in the real sense would require the non-existent to have some reality, which is not accepted.
4
Analyze the second meaning and its implication
For the second meaning, acceptance as occurrence or removal, even if quiddity were identical to existence, removal is still possible because existence itself can be removed. This shows the proof does not prove a real distinction.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how this distinction protects the view that quiddity and existence are identical in possible beings, and why the objection fails.
This connects to the broader debate about the reality of quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وأجيب إلخ) حاصله أنه إن أريد بالقبول معناه الحقيقي أعني الاتصاف الذي يقتضي مجامعة القابل والمقبول فلا نسلم بطلان التالي بمنع أن الماهية من حيث لأنه فرع القول بثبوت المعدوم ولا ثبوت له عندنا وإن أريد به الطريان سواء اجتمع معه أو لا، فلا قوله: (بل كانت تأبى العدم من حيث هي هي أيضاً أي مثل الماهية الواجبية أو مثل المأخوذ مع الوجود . هي نفي صرف ( وإن أردت ) بقبولها العدم ( ارتفاعها ) بالكلية، فلا نسلم أنها لو كانت نفس الوجود أو كان الوجود جزءها ( لما قبلته أي لما قبلت الماهية من حيث هي هي العدم وذلك لأن الوجود نفسه يرتفع بالكلية ( لأنه إذا ارتفع الماهية ) الممكنة ) فقد ارتفع وجودها قطعاً ) إذ لا يجوز قيام ذلك الوجود بذاته ولا بغير تلك الماهية ولو قام بها لم تكن مرتفعة بل ،موجودة وإذا جاز ارتفاع الوجود بالكلية واتصافه اشتقاقاً بنقيضه الذي هو العدم جاز ذلك في الماهية على تقدير كون الوجود نفسها .
His saying: 'And it was answered, etc.' Its summary is that if by 'acceptance' is meant its real meaning, namely the attribute that requires the co-existence of the acceptor and the accepted, then we do not concede the falsity of the consequent by denying that quiddity from its very self [accepts non-existence] because that depends on affirming the existence of the non-existent, and there is no affirmation of it according to us. And if by it is meant 'occurrence' (al-turyan) whether it coincides with it or not, then [we say] his saying: 'But rather it repels non-existence from its very self as well' i.e. like the necessary quiddity or like quiddity taken with existence. It is pure negation. And if you mean by its acceptance of non-existence its complete removal (irtifa'), then we do not concede that if it were identical to existence or existence were a part of it, it would not accept it, i.e., quiddity from its very self would not accept non-existence. That is because existence itself is completely removed because when the possible quiddity is removed, its existence is certainly removed, since it is not possible for that existence to subsist by itself or by something other than that quiddity; and if it did subsist by it, the quiddity would not be removed but would be existent. And if the complete removal of existence is permissible and its derivative attribution to its opposite which is non-existence is permissible, then that is permissible in the quiddity on the assumption that existence is identical to it.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This is a response (ujiba) to the previous proof. The commentator clarifies that the argument depends on the meaning of 'acceptance of non-existence' (qubul al-'adam). He distinguishes two senses: (1) real acceptance, which requires the subject to exist and receive an attribute—this cannot apply to non-existence because the non-existent has no reality; (2) acceptance as mere 'occurrence' (turyan), where non-existence simply replaces existence. On the first sense, the proof fails because we do not affirm any reality for the non-existent. On the second sense, the quiddity's rejection of non-existence when taken with existence is like the necessary quiddity's rejection. The commentator then addresses the second possible interpretation: if by 'accepting non-existence' we mean complete removal (irtifa'), then even existence itself can be removed, so the quiddity could be removed regardless. This shows that the quiddity's ability to be removed does not prove existence is not identical to it.

Key Terms
قبول (qubul)
اتصاف (ittisaf)
طريان (turyan)
ارتفاع (irtifa')
ثبوت المعدوم (thubut al-ma'dum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows quiddity must be distinct from existence because quiddity accepts non-existence.
Correction: The response clarifies that 'acceptance' does not mean real subsistence without existence; it can mean mere occurrence or removal, which does not require a real subject.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in the response to the proof?
A) Between essential and accidental properties
B) Between 'acceptance' as real subsistence and as mere occurrence or removal
C) Between necessary and possible existence
D) Between mental and external existence

Key Takeaway
The response shows that quiddity's acceptance of non-existence is not a real attribute but simply the possibility of removal, preserving the identity of quiddity and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your own existence. Do you truly believe that your very self is nothing without God's sustaining existence each moment? Or do you live as if you have an independent core that persists on its own? What would change in your daily reliance on God if you internalized this lesson?
?
The lesson contrasts a real attribute of acceptance that requires coexistence with a mere occurrence. Consider your claim of faith: is it a real attribute that coexists with your actions, or just a verbal occurrence that doesn't affect your life? What specific step can you take today to make your faith a true attribute that is always present with your deeds?

Second Argument: Conceiving Quiddity with Doubt of Existence
Sanusi presents the second argument: conceiving quiddity with doubt of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the superaddition of existence to quiddity: that we can conceive a quiddity while doubting its existence, and how the author defends this argument against the objection concerning mental existence.

Core Idea
Doubt about existence after conceiving quiddity proves existence is not the same as quiddity.

This lesson presents another powerful argument against the identity of existence and quiddity. If existence were identical, then knowing the quiddity would immediately tell us whether it exists. But we can conceive a triangle and still doubt if it exists outside the mind. The argument faces a subtle objection about mental existence, which the author skillfully answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the core premise: we can conceive a possible quiddity while doubting its existence. This is the foundation of the proof.
Notice the example of a triangle is used to illustrate the point.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that doubt only applies to external existence, not mental existence, because mental existence is identical to the conception itself. Consider whether this objection weakens the argument.
The objector argues that the proof only shows external existence is not identical, but the claim is about absolute existence.
3
Follow the reply
The author replies by granting mental existence but still showing that the entailment holds: if existence were identical, quiddity would repel non-existence, but existence itself does not repel non-existence. This defends the argument.
The commentator adds clarifications about the meaning of 'acceptance' and the nature of mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني أنا نعقل الماهية الممكنة ( كالمثلث ) مثلاً ( مع الشك في وجودها فلا يكون الوجود نفسها ولا جزءها لما سيصرح به ( لا يقال الشك : إنما يتصور في وجودها الخارجي دون الوجود ( الذهني فإنه ) أي الوجود الذهني (نفس التعقل والتصور، فإذا تعقلت الماهية كانت موجودة في الذهن فكيف يشك بعد تعقلها في وجودها الذهني؟ فاللازم مما ذكرتم أن الوجود الخارجي ليس نفس الماهية ولا جزءها ) والكلام في الوجود المطلق وأنه زائد على الماهية سواء كان وجوداً خارجياً أو ذهنياً فالدليل قاصر عن المدعى ( لأنا نقول: ) على تقدير تسليم الوجود الذهني لا قصور فيه إذ ) تحقق الوجود (الذهني حال كون الماهية معقولة
نسلم الملازمة المدلول عليها بقوله لو كان نفس الماهية أو جزءها كانت الماهية من حيث هي تأبى العدم كالموجودة لأن الوجود في نفسه لا يأبى طريان العدم، بأن يرتفع بالكلية فكيف تأبى عنه الماهية بواسطة اتحاد الوجود بها أو جزئيته لها؟ وإنما قلنا إن الوجود يرتفع بالكلية لأن الماهية الممكنة الموجودة إذا ارتفعت بطريان العدم سواء ارتفع نفسها أو لا يرتفع الوجود بالكلية كما ذكره الشارح .
قوله لما سيصرح به من امتناع الشك في ثبوت الشيء لنفسه وثبوت جزئيته له بعد تعقله بالكنه .
قوله : (نفس التعقل والتصور بمعنى حصول صورة الشيء لا بمعنى الصورة الحاصلة فإن التعقل حينئذ موجود لا وجود .
قوله : ( على تقدير تسليم إلخ) أي لا نسلم أن للوجود فرداً سوى الوجود الخارجي، فالدليل غير قاصر ولو سلم ذلك فلا تصور أيضاً .
قوله : فلا نسلم أنها لو كانت نفس الوجود لما قبلته خلاصة الجواب أن ليس المراد بالقبول ها هنا القبول الحقيقي الذي يقتضي اجتماع القابل مع المقبول بل المجازي.
قوله : ( فإنه نفس التعقل والتصور المراد بالتعقل والتصورها هنا نفس حصول صورة الشيء في العقل ولو مسامحة لما صرح المحققون بأنه الصورة الحاصلة، فلا يرد أن التصور والتعقل موجود ذهني لا وجود ذهني .
The second argument: that we conceive the possible quiddity (like a triangle) for example while doubting its existence, so existence is not its essence nor a part of it, as will be declared. It should not be said that doubt only occurs about its external existence, not about mental existence, for mental existence itself is the act of intellection and conception; so when you conceive the quiddity, it exists in the mind, so how can you doubt after conceiving it regarding its mental existence? Therefore, what you have deduced is that external existence is not identical to quiddity nor a part of it, but the discussion is about absolute existence and that it is superadded to quiddity, whether external or mental, so the proof is insufficient for the claim. For we say: on the assumption of granting mental existence, there is no insufficiency in it, because mental existence is realized while the quiddity is conceived. We concede the entailment indicated by his saying: if it were identical to quiddity or a part, then quiddity from its very self would repel non-existence like the existent, because existence itself does not repel the occurrence of non-existence, by being completely removed; so how would quiddity repel it through the union of existence with it or its being a part of it? For we have said that existence is completely removed because the existing possible quiddity when it is removed by the occurrence of non-existence, whether its essence is removed or not, existence is completely removed, as the commentator mentioned. His saying: 'as will be declared' refers to the impossibility of doubt about a thing's being itself or its being a part of itself after conceptualization in essence. His saying: 'the act of intellection and conception' means the obtaining of the form of the thing, not the obtained form, for then intellection would be an existent, not existence. His saying: 'on the assumption of granting, etc.' means we do not concede that existence has an individual other than external existence, so the proof is not insufficient; and even if that is granted, there is no conception either. His saying: 'so we do not concede that if it were identical to existence, it would not accept it' is the summary of the answer: that the intended meaning here is not the real acceptance that requires the conjunction of acceptor and accepted, but rather figurative acceptance. His saying: 'for it is itself the act of intellection and conception' means that the intended meaning of intellection and conception here is the obtaining of the form of the thing in the mind, even if loosely, as the verifiers have declared that it is the obtained form; so it is not objected that conception and intellection are mental existents, not mental existence.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This is a second independent proof that existence is not identical to quiddity nor a part of it. The argument is: we can conceive of a possible quiddity (e.g., a triangle) while being in doubt about its existence. If existence were the same as quiddity or a part of it, then in conceiving the quiddity we would ipso facto conceive its existence, and doubt would be impossible. Since doubt is possible, existence must be distinct. The objection (la yuqalu) raises that doubt only concerns external existence, not mental existence, because mental existence is exactly the conception itself. So the doubt would not extend to mental existence, and the proof only shows that external existence is not identical, but the claim is about absolute existence. The reply (ana naqulu) concedes mental existence but argues that the proof still works if we grant mental existence: the entailment is that if existence were identical, quiddity would repel non-existence like an existent, but existence itself does not repel non-existence (it can be removed), so quiddity would not repel it either. The commentary further explains that the proof does not require mental existence to be a separate individual; even if one denies mental existence, the proof stands. The final clarification states that 'acceptance' here is figurative, not real.

Key Terms
تعقل (ta'aqqul)
شك (shakk)
وجود خارجي (wujud khariji)
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni)
الوجود المطلق (al-wujud al-mutlaq)
تصور (tasawwur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that doubt only applies to external existence invalidates the argument that existence is distinct from quiddity.
Correction: The reply shows that even if mental existence is granted, the argument still holds because existence itself does not require quiddity to be necessary; quiddity can be conceived without existence.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the second argument that existence is not identical to quiddity?
A) Quiddity must be necessary if existence is identical
B) We can conceive a quiddity while doubting its existence
C) Existence always accompanies quiddity in the mind
D) The possible quiddity cannot be conceived without existence

Key Takeaway
We can conceive quiddity while doubting its existence, proving existence is not identical to quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine something you desire—like a perfect spouse or a successful future—do you automatically assume it must exist? How does this lesson's proof that quiddity can be conceived without existence challenge the way you attach your hopes to mere concepts? What would it mean for you to hold those concepts loosely, knowing they are not existence itself?
?
You often find yourself doubting the reality of unseen things—like the afterlife or the angels—while still conceiving them clearly. This lesson says that doubt itself proves existence is not part of the concept. Does your doubt ever become an excuse for inaction? How can you use this distinction to move from conceptual belief to actual conviction?
?
Reflect on a time when you confused your mental image of something with its actual reality—perhaps an idol in your heart or a false expectation. The lesson shows that quiddity (what you conceive) is separate from existence (what is real). Are you willing to examine your concepts and let them be tested against truth, even if it means admitting they do not exist as you imagined? What concrete step can you take today to align your concepts with what truly exists?

First Argument: Mental Existence is Not Identical to Essence
The commentator presents the first argument for the separation of mental existence from essence, with an objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that mental existence is separate from the essence, including the objection and its refutation.

Core Idea
Mental existence is not the essence itself; the argument shows it is separate.

In this passage, the commentator presents the first argument demonstrating that mental existence is not identical to the essence. He then anticipates an objection and provides a concise refutation. This argument is foundational for understanding the distinction between mental and external existence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the overall argument. Don't analyze yet; just absorb the flow.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the main claim: mental existence is not the essence because the external essence is devoid of mental existence. Notice how the commentator concludes that mental existence is neither the essence nor a part of it.
The argument relies on the premise that the external essence lacks mental existence.
3
Examine the objection
The objection attempts to apply the same logic to external existence: since the mental essence lacks external existence, external existence would also be additional. Consider why this might seem plausible.
4
Follow the refutation
The commentator denies the premise that the essence itself is present in the mind. Only some aspects of the essence are present. Therefore, the objection does not hold.
This is a key point: the mental representation is not the essence itself.
5
Grasp the dispute over the condition
The passage discusses a condition about the essence being unconceived, but argues that this condition is impossible because essences are always conceived by some intellect. This reinforces the argument.
This part deals with a potential counter-argument.

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the first argument that mental existence is not identical to the essence, followed by an objection and its answer. The argument states that the external essence is devoid of mental existence, so mental existence cannot be the essence nor a part of it. Then a potential objection is raised: the essence in the mind is devoid of external existence, so by the same reasoning external existence would also be additional. The commentator replies that this objection fails because we do not concede that the essence itself is present in the mind; only some of its aspects are present, and these aspects are not external existents. The passage also discusses the condition that the essence must be unconceived by any intellect for it to be devoid of mental existence, but this condition is problematic since essences are conceived by higher intellects. The commentator concludes that the objection is refuted.

Second Proof: Doubt About Existence Shows Existence Is Not the Essence
The commentator presents the second proof using doubt about existence and responds to objections about the middle term and knowledge of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam proof that existence is additional to essence, and the key objections that limit its scope.

Core Idea
If we can conceive an essence while doubting its existence, existence cannot be the essence or its part.

The commentator presents a second proof that existence is not identical to the essence. The proof relies on the fact that we can conceive an essence (like a triangle) while doubting its actual existence. This intuitive distinction suggests that existence is not the essence itself nor a part of it. However, the proof faces important objections concerning the completeness of our knowledge of essences and the generality of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second proof's structure
Read the passage to see how the proof is formulated: we conceive the essence but do not assent to its existence. Note that the commentator clarifies the correct middle term.
2
Identify the commentator's clarification
The proof is not about conceiving vs. assenting, but about doubting the subsistence of existence for the essence. See why this is necessary.
3
Analyze the first objection
The objection says the proof only works if essences are known in their essence (bi-al-kunh). We do not concede that any essence is known that way.
4
Consider the second objection
A particular example (triangle) does not establish a universal rule. So the proof only refutes the identity claim, not proves that existence is always additional.
This is a crucial limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض (الفضلاء يعني القاضي الأرموي (حاصل) (الدليل الذي هو الوجه الثاني ( أنا نعلمه أي الممكن كالمثلث مثلاً (تصوراً ) فإن هذا معنى كون الماهية الممكنة معقولة ولا نعلمه أي وجود الممكن ( تصديقاً) لأن الشك في الوجود ينافي التصديق به لا تصوره، فيصير الدليل هكذا نعلم الماهية تصوراً، ولا نعلم وجودها تصديقاً ( فلا ينتج إذ الوسط غير مكرر ، وليس له ورود إذ الاستدلال ) ليس بما توهمه هذا الفاضل بل ( بأنا نشك في ثبوته ) أي ثبوت الوجود (للماهية ) المعقولة ( ولا شيء من الماهية وجزئها مما يشك في ثبوته (للماهية لامتناع الشك في ثبوت الشيء لنفسه، وفي ثبوت ذاتي له فلا يكون الوجود نفس الماهية، ولا جزءها لكن يرد على هذا أنه إنما لا يجوز الشك في الجزء إذا كانت الماهية معقولة بالكنه، ولا نسلم أن شيئاً من الماهيات معقول كذلك، وأيضاً المثال الجزئي لا يصحح كلية، فيجوز أن يكون بعض ما لم نتعقلها من الماهيات بحيث لو تعقلت بخصوصها لم يشك في وجودها فما ذكرتموه إنما يصح لإبطال قول من ادعى أن كل وجود نفس الماهية لا لإثبات أن كل وجود زائد عليها.
Some of the virtuous (meaning al-Qāḍī al-Urmawī) (summarized) (the proof which is the second aspect: that we know it, i.e., the contingent, e.g., the triangle, (by conception), for this is the meaning of the contingent essence being conceivable; and we do not know it, i.e., the existence of the contingent, (by assent), because doubt about existence negates assent to it, not its conception. So the proof becomes: we know the essence by conception, and we do not know its existence by assent. (So it does not yield a conclusion, because the middle term is not repeated, and it has no force, because the inference) is not what this virtuous one imagined, but rather (by our doubting its subsistence), i.e., the subsistence of existence (for the conceived essence), and nothing of the essence and its part is such that doubt arises about its subsistence (for the essence, because it is impossible to doubt the subsistence of a thing for itself, or of an essential attribute for it. So existence is neither the essence nor a part of it. But it is objected to this that doubt about the part is not allowed only when the essence is known in its essence, and we do not concede that any of the essences is known in its essence. Also, a particular example does not establish a universal. So it is possible that some essences we have not conceived are such that if they were conceived in their particularity, we would not doubt their existence. So what you have mentioned only serves to refute the claim of one who asserts that every existence is identical to the essence, not to prove that every existence is additional to it.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that existence is not identical to the essence, based on the fact that we can conceive an essence (like a triangle) while doubting its existence. The proof is: we conceive the essence but do not assent to its existence, so existence is not the essence nor a part of it. The commentator then addresses two objections. First, that the proof is invalid because the middle term is not repeated; the commentator clarifies that the correct formulation is that we doubt the subsistence of existence for the essence, and no essence or its part can be doubted for itself. Second, that the proof only works if the essence is known in its entirety (bi-al-kunh), which is not the case, and that a particular example does not prove universality. The objection argues that the proof only disproves the claim that all existence is identical to essence, not that all existence is additional.

Correction: The proof only refutes the claim that existence is identical to essence for some essences; it does not prove that existence is always additional.

C) The proof uses a particular example that is not valid.
D) The proof confuses conception and assent.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that existence is not the essence based on doubt, but it only refutes identity, not proves universal addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take for you to honestly admit that some of your most cherished beliefs might be based on mere conception rather than proof? Commit to examining one such belief this week.
?
The proof’s objector says no essence is known in its full reality. You do not know yourself entirely, nor the people you judge, nor the doctrines you defend. Yet you act as if you do. Where in your life are you pretending to have complete knowledge—of Allah, of others, or of yourself? What is the first step you will take to replace that pretense with humble inquiry?

Third Proof: Predication Would Be Meaningless If Existence Identical to Essence
Commentary on the third proof against identity of existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third proof argues that if existence were identical to essence, predicating existence would be a meaningless tautology, and how the commentator refines this argument.

Core Idea
Identifying existence with essence makes 'exists' just saying 'same thing' – no information.

This passage presents the third proof against the identity of existence and essence. The key insight: if they were identical, any statement like 'the triangle exists' would be as empty as saying 'the triangle is a triangle'. The commentator explores this and addresses potential objections, distinguishing between meaningful and merely verbal benefit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's core claim
Read the opening line: 'If existence were identical to essence, then predicating existence upon essence would yield no meaningful benefit.' Grasp why this is a problem.
Think of tautology – saying x is x gives no new info.
2
Examine the response about derivative predication
The commentator considers that even if the predication is derivative (like 'lion is an animal'), it still collapses into self-predication because the essence would be existence itself.
Derivative doesn't escape the problem.
3
Distinguish meaningful vs verbal benefit
The author notes that some statements only have verbal benefit (like metaphors). Here, the predication would lack even meaningful benefit.
Verbal benefit is not enough to rescue the claim.
4
Note the limitation: compulsion vs certainty
The commentator concludes that this argument only compels the opponent, but does not yield logical certainty.
The issue of existence vs essence requires certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that existence cannot be identical to the essence because if it were, predicating existence would be meaningless (like saying 'a thing is itself'). The commentator then discusses various responses and refinements. He explains that the proof's formulation is valid if the major premise is understood implicitly. He also addresses the objection about the middle term not being repeated, suggesting that replacing 'doubt' with 'heedlessness' fixes it. The commentator also clarifies that predicating existence of the essence, even if derivative, would still be self-predication if existence were identical. He distinguishes between meaningful (ma'nawī) and verbal (lafẓī) benefit. Finally, he notes that the argument only compels the opponent but does not yield certainty, and that the issue ultimately requires certainty. The passage ends with an incomplete remark about the meaning of blackness.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that identifying existence with essence leads to tautology in predication, but it only compels the opponent and does not yield certainty.

The Second Argument: Existence Is Not Identical to Quiddity
Presents the second argument with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for distinguishing existence from quiddity, including its logical structure and the main objection against it.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be identical to quiddity because we can conceive a thing while doubting its existence.

This passage presents the second of several arguments for the claim that existence is additional to quiddity. The argument relies on the fact that we can conceive a quiddity (like a triangle) without knowing whether it actually exists. This gap between conception and affirmation suggests that existence is not part of the quiddity itself. However, the argument faces a serious objection that questions its scope and validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from argument to objection to answer.
2
Identify the core logical structure
The argument uses a syllogism: we conceive the quiddity (e.g., triangle), but we doubt its existence. Since a thing's essence cannot be doubted for itself, existence must be separate.
The text refines the initial formulation to avoid a logical error.
3
Understand the objection
The objection argues that the condition of the syllogism (no doubt about the part) holds only if the quiddity is known in its essence, which is not conceded. Also, a particular example does not prove a universal rule.
4
Consider the answer and clarifications
The answer notes that if the purpose is to draw attention to a self-evident rule, the objection does not apply. Two glosses further clarify the logical points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض (الفضلاء يعني القاضي الأرموي (حاصل) (الدليل الذي هو الوجه الثاني ( أنا نعلمه أي الممكن كالمثلث مثلاً (تصوراً ) فإن هذا معنى كون الماهية الممكنة معقولة ولا نعلمه أي وجود الممكن ( تصديقاً) لأن الشك في الوجود ينافي التصديق به لا تصوره، فيصير الدليل هكذا نعلم الماهية تصوراً، ولا نعلم وجودها تصديقاً ( فلا ينتج إذ الوسط غير مكرر ، وليس له ورود إذ الاستدلال ) ليس بما توهمه هذا الفاضل بل ( بأنا نشك في ثبوته ) أي ثبوت الوجود (للماهية ) المعقولة ( ولا شيء من الماهية وجزئها مما يشك في ثبوته (للماهية لامتناع الشك في ثبوت الشيء لنفسه، وفي ثبوت ذاتي له فلا يكون الوجود نفس الماهية، ولا جزءها لكن يرد على هذا أنه إنما لا يجوز الشك في الجزء إذا كانت الماهية معقولة بالكنه، ولا نسلم أن شيئاً من الماهيات معقول كذلك، وأيضاً المثال الجزئي لا يصحح كلية، فيجوز أن يكون بعض ما لم نتعقلها من الماهيات بحيث لو تعقلت بخصوصها لم يشك في وجودها فما ذكرتموه إنما يصح لإبطال قول من ادعى أن كل وجود نفس الماهية لا لإثبات أن كل وجود زائد عليها . الوجه ( الثالث لو كان الوجود نفس الماهية لما أفاد حمله عليها ( فائدة معنوية أصلا بل كان يعد هدراً ( وكان قولنا :

قاعدة
في وجوده تمام الدليل كأنه قيل : المثلث معقول والوجود مشكوك فيه وحاصل الجواب : أنه صغرى الدليل والكبرى مطوية .
قوله : ( إذ الوسط غير مكرر حتى لو أبدل قوله مع الشك في وجوده بقولنا : مع الذهول عن وجوده تم الدليل واندفع المناقشة.
قوله : (المثال الجزئي إلخ هذا إذا كان المقصود الإثبات وأما إذا كان التنبيه على تلك القاعدة البديهية فلا يرد .
Some of the virtuous (meaning al-Qadi al-Urmawi) obtained the proof which is the second aspect: that we know it, i.e., the possible (like a triangle for example) conceptually, for this is the meaning of the quiddity of the possible being intelligible, and we do not know it, i.e., the existence of the possible, affirmatively, because doubt about existence negates affirmation of it, not its conception. So the proof becomes thus: we know the quiddity conceptually, and we do not know its existence affirmatively. (Then it does not yield a conclusion because the middle term is not repeated, and it is not a valid inference because the reasoning) is not what this virtuous one imagined, but rather (by our doubting its subsistence) i.e., the subsistence of existence (for the quiddity) that is intelligible, (and nothing of the quiddity and its part is such that its subsistence is doubted for the quiddity, because it is impossible to doubt the subsistence of a thing for itself, and for its essential attribute for it. So existence is not the quiddity itself, nor a part of it. But an objection is made against this: doubt is not allowed about a part only when the quiddity is known in essence, and we do not concede that any quiddity is known in essence. Also, a particular example does not validate a universal; it is possible that some quiddities we have not conceived are such that if we conceived them specifically, we would not doubt their existence. So what you mentioned is only valid to refute the claim that every existence is identical to the quiddity, not to prove that every existence is additional to it. The third aspect: if existence were identical to the quiddity, then predicating it upon it would not provide a meaningful benefit at all, but would be considered useless. And our saying: 

Rule
In its existence is the complete proof, as if it were said: The triangle is intelligible and existence is doubted. The sum of the answer: it is the minor premise of the proof and the major premise is implied.
His saying: ('the middle term is not repeated') such that if he replaced his statement 'with doubt about its existence' with our statement: 'with heedlessness of its existence', the proof would be complete and the objection would be repelled.
His saying: ('the particular example' etc.) This is if the intent is proof; but if it is to draw attention to that self-evident rule, then the objection does not apply.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) regarding whether existence is identical to quiddity or a part of it. It begins by stating the argument: we conceive a quiddity, like a triangle, but we doubt its existence. Since we have conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) of the quiddity but not affirmative knowledge (taṣdīq) of its existence, this shows that existence is not the same as the quiddity, nor a part of it. The argument is reframed to avoid a logical flaw: the middle term is repeated if we simply say we know quiddity but not its existence. Instead, the correct reasoning is: we doubt the subsistence of existence for the quiddity, and nothing of the quiddity or its part is such that its subsistence is doubted (because a thing cannot be doubted for itself). Therefore existence is neither identical nor a part. The text then introduces an objection: doubt about a part is only impossible if the quiddity is known in its essence (bi-l-kunh), which we do not concede; also, the example of a triangle is particular and does not prove a universal rule. The objection claims that the argument only refutes the view that every existence is identical to quiddity, not that every existence is additional. The passage then includes a 'rule' (qāʿida) that clarifies the logical form. Finally, two glosses explain the reasoning: the first shows how replacing 'doubt' with 'heedlessness' would complete the proof; the second answers the objection by noting that if the purpose is merely to draw attention to a self-evident rule, the objection does not apply.

Key Terms

التصور

المثال الجزئي
القاعدة البديهية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument proves that existence is always additional to quiddity for all beings.
Correction: The argument only refutes the claim that existence is identical to quiddity. It does not prove that existence is additional in every case, because the objection limits its scope.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection argue that the second argument fails to prove that every existence is additional to quiddity?
A) Because the middle term is not repeated.
B) Because the quiddity of a triangle is not known in essence, so the argument's condition is not met.
C) Because doubt about existence does not negate conceptual knowledge.
D) Because the example is universal, not particular.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that existence is not identical to quiddity by appealing to the distinction between conceptual and affirmative knowledge, but its scope is limited by the objection that quiddities are not known in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you ever doubt it? This lesson shows that existence and essence are distinct. How does this truth affect your understanding of your own contingency and need for a Necessary Being?
?
The objection states that we do not know quiddities in their essence. When you reflect on Allah's names and attributes, do you recognize that you do not know His essence? How should this humility shape your worship and reliance on revelation?
?
If predicting existence were useless when identical to quiddity, then every predication of existence is meaningful. When you say 'Allah exists', is that statement meaningful to you? Does it carry weight in your daily life, or has it become an empty phrase?

The Third Argument: Predicating Existence Would Be Useless
Explains the third argument: predication of existence would be useless if existence were identical to quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why predicating existence would be useless if existence were identical to quiddity, and how commentators address objections and alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
If existence equalled quiddity, saying 'it exists' would be like saying 'it is itself'—no new information.

This passage presents the third argument against the identity of existence and quiddity. The core claim is that if existence were the same as quiddity, then predicating existence of anything would be entirely redundant. The commentary explores the nuances of this argument, including objections about verbal benefit and the role of mental existence, refining why the predication remains informative in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the formal tone and the chain of glosses.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the reasoning that predicating existence would be predication of a thing of itself (ḥaml al-shay' ʿalā nafsihi). Understand why this is considered useless.
3
Examine the objection and response
Notice the glosses discussing verbal benefit (al-fā'idah al-lafẓiyyah) and mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). See how the commentator refines the argument.
4
Consider the alternative expression
The passage offers an alternative: saying 'blackness exists' is like 'blackness possesses blackness'. Why is this not beneficial? Contrast with the case where existence is a part.
This shows the subtlety of the argument.

Commentary
This passage provides the commentary on the third argument (al-wajh al-thālith) that if existence were identical to quiddity, then predicating existence would be useless. The commentary begins by explaining why: it would be predication of a thing of itself, whether by equivalence (muwāṭa'a) or derivation (ishtiqāq). If it is by derivation, then the quiddity exists by itself, not by a separate quality, so saying 'it exists' simply means 'it is existence'. The gloss then notes that even if no meaningful benefit results, there might be a verbal benefit, like saying 'the lion is a lion'. The next gloss discusses that if the existence were mental, the denial of benefit might not apply. Then the passage examines the claim that the argument only proves that existence is additional in a specific sense, not universally. The commentator offers an alternative expression: saying 'blackness exists' is like saying 'blackness possesses blackness' or 'existence possesses existence', which is not beneficial. However, if existence were a part, the predication would be like 'blackness is color', which is beneficial only when the quiddity is known in essence (bi-l-kunh). Since we do not know quiddities in essence, the predication of existence remains informative. The passage ends by contrasting the benefit of predicating existence with the lack of benefit if existence were a part and the quiddity were known in essence.

Key Takeaway
Predicating existence is informative only because existence is additional to quiddity and we do not know quiddities in their essence; if existence were identical to quiddity, the predication would be tautological.

?

?

Where today can you trade a tautological belief for a meaningful one that reshapes your action?
?
The commentator urges you to 'reflect' (فتدبر) on the subtlety of mental existence. Are you content to know God only through secondhand concepts—attributes and names—without striving to know Him in His essence as much as a creature can? What would change in your relationship with Him if you were to take one step from knowing about Him to knowing Him? What will you sacrifice this week to make that step?

The Fourth Argument: Proving the Addition of Absolute Existence
Commentary on the fourth argument for existence addition, focusing on absolute existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument specifically demonstrates that absolute existence is additional to quiddity, and why it does not cover specific existence.

Core Idea
The fourth argument proves absolute existence is additional, not specific existence.

The commentator now turns to the fourth argument for the addition of existence to quiddity. This argument is unique in that it only establishes the addition of absolute existence, unlike the previous arguments which covered both absolute and specific. The passage also addresses the subtle issue of whether a thing can be predicated of itself in a beneficial way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of the fourth argument
Read the first sentence carefully: 'This aspect indicates the addition of absolute existence, unlike the previous aspects...' Note that the argument is limited in scope.
This sets the context for the entire passage.
2
Understand the consequence if existence were identical
The commentary mentions that if existence were not additional, the non-existence of quiddity would mean its complete removal. Reflect on why this would be a problem.
This consequence is part of the argument's reasoning.
3
Examine the discussion on self-predication
The passage critiques the claim that a thing can be predicated of itself derivatively with benefit. Look for the term 'relational distinction' (al-mughāyara al-iʿtibāriyya) and see how it is used.
This is a nuanced point about the logic of predication.
4
Note the limitation of the argument
The final gloss states that if the argument were complete, it would only prove absolute existence addition. Confirm this by reading the last sentence.
This contrasts with the previous arguments.

قوله : (الوجه الرابع هذا الوجه يدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق بخلاف الوجوه السابقة فإنها دالة على زيادة المطلق والخاص.

موجود حينئذ أنه ليس بمرتفع على ما مر أن معنى عدم الماهية على تقدير عينية الوجود لها ارتفاعها بالكلية، وأما القول بأن نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه بالاشتقاق مفيد، بل هو مبحث للعقلاء، فإن النسبة بين الوجود ونفسه اشتقاقا معركة للآراء، فقد ذكرنا في مباحث شبه القادحين في البديهيات اندفاعه وكيف لا والمغايرة الاعتبارية أن كفى في نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه بلفظ ذو وكان صحة الحمل مبنياً عليها كان إنكار عدم الإفادة مكابرة إذ لا فائدة في اعتبار المغايرة بين الشيء ونفسه ، وحمله على نفسه بواسطة ذو وإن لم يكف كما هو الظاهر إذ التغاير الاعتباري لا يكفي في كل نسبة كما في كون شيء فوق شيء وأمثاله والنسبة بين الشيء ونفسه بالصاحبية والاتصاف من هذا القبيل فهذا الحمل ليس بصحيح فضلاً عن الإفادة . قوله : ( الرابع إلخ ) لو تم لدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق دون الخاص.
His saying: ('the fourth aspect') This aspect indicates the addition of absolute existence, unlike the previous aspects, for they indicate the addition of both absolute and specific.

So it exists then, that it is not removed, as we have passed that the meaning of non-existence of the quiddity on the supposition that existence is identical to it is its complete removal. As for the saying that the relation of a thing to itself by derivation is beneficial, it is rather a subject of discussion among the intelligent. For the relation between existence and itself by derivation is a battlefield of opinions. We have mentioned in the discussions of the semblances of the impugners of self-evident truths its refutation. And why not? For the relational distinction (al-mughāyara al-iʿtibāriyya) is sufficient for the relation of a thing to itself with the word 'possessor' (dhū), and if the correctness of predication is built upon it, then the denial of lack of benefit is stubbornness, since there is no benefit in considering the distinction between a thing and itself, and predicating it of itself through 'possessor'. And if it is not sufficient, as is apparent – because relational distinction is not sufficient for every relation, as in the case of something being above something and its like – then the relation between a thing and itself by companionship and qualification is of this kind, so this predication is not sound, let alone beneficial. His saying: ('the fourth' etc.) If it were complete, it would indicate the addition of absolute existence, not specific.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the gloss on the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rābiʿ). It first notes that this argument specifically proves the addition of absolute existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq), while the previous arguments covered both absolute and specific existence. The commentary then discusses the consequence if existence were not additional: the non-existence of the quiddity would mean its complete removal. Next, it critiques the claim that the derivative relation of a thing to itself is beneficial. The commentator argues that such a relation is a matter of debate, and that relational distinction (al-mughāyara al-iʿtibāriyya) might allow the predication of 'possessor' but it yields no benefit because it is merely a distinction in conception. Moreover, for relations like 'above' or companionship, this relational distinction is insufficient, so the predication is not sound. The final gloss states that if the fourth argument were complete, it would only prove the addition of absolute existence, not specific existence.

Why Existence Cannot Be a Part of Essence
Al-Jurjani refutes the view that existence is a part of essence by eliminating both identity and composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be a part of essence, through the argument that both identity and composition lead to absurdities, including an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Existence is neither essence nor its part; both options lead to contradiction.

Al-Jurjani presents a decisive proof that existence is separate from essence. He considers two possibilities—identity and composition—and shows that both are impossible. This argument establishes a foundational distinction in Islamic metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a sense of the logical flow.
The passage is dense; focus on the two main possibilities.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Notice that the passage examines two ways existence could be a part: identity (existence = essence) or composition (existence as a constituent part).
3
Understand refutation of identity
The first possibility is rejected because existence is common across all existents, while essences are distinct. They cannot be identical.
The passage also dismisses a monistic objection.
4
Understand refutation of composition
If existence were a part, it would be the most common essential attribute, i.e., a genus. But then differentiae would also be existent, leading to a chain of genera and infinite regress, which is impossible.
Infinite regress in parts of an essence is absurd because composites must terminate in simples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها لكن لو كان الوجود جزءاً للماهية لكان إما نفسها أو جزءها والأول باطل لأنه ( أي الوجود ) مشترك ) لما مر ( دونها ) أي دون الماهية لأن حقائق الموجودات متخالفة بالضرورة وما يقال من أن الكل ذات واحدة تتعدد بحسب الأوصاف لا غير فالمتقيدون بطور العقل يعدونه مكابرة لا يلتفت إليها ( وكذا الثاني) باطل إذ لو كان الوجود ( جزءاً ) للماهيات لكان أعم الذاتيات المشتركة بين الموجودات إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه ( فكان جنسا لها ) إن كان محمولاً عليها وإلا كان جزءاً مشتركاً مثل الجنس ) وتتمايز أنواعه ) المندرجة تحته ( بفصول) أو بأجزاء مختصة مثل الفصول ( هي أيضاً موجودة) لكونها مقومة وأجزاء للماهيات الموجودة ( فيكون ( الوجود ( جنساً لها ) أي لتلك الفصول أيضاً إذ الفرض أنه جنس للموجودات ( فلها ) أي فللفصول (فصول) أخر ( كذلك ) أي موجودة أيضاً ) ويلزم التسلسل وترتب أجزاء الماهية الواحدة إلى غير النهاية ( وأنه محال إذ المركب لا بد له من الانتهاء إلى البسيط )
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is the essence itself or a part of it. But if existence were a part of the essence, it would either be the essence itself or a part of it. The first is false because it (i.e., existence) is common (as previously mentioned) while they (i.e., the essences) are not, because the realities of existents are necessarily different. And what is said—that all is one entity that becomes multiple according to attributes only—those bound by the mode of the intellect consider it absurd and not to be heeded. (And likewise the second) is false, for if existence were a part of the essences, it would be the most common of the essential attributes common among existents, since there is no essential attribute more common than it. So it would be a genus for them if it were predicated of them; otherwise it would be a common part like the genus. And its types (subsumed under it) are differentiated by differentiae or by specific parts like the differentiae, which are also existent because they are constitutive and parts of the existing essences. Thus existence would be a genus for them, i.e., for those differentiae as well, since it is assumed to be a genus for existents. So the differentiae would have other differentiae likewise, i.e., also existent, and this entails an infinite regress and the ordering of the parts of a single essence ad infinitum. And that is impossible, because a composite must necessarily terminate in a simple.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that existence cannot be a part of essence. The argument proceeds by considering two possibilities: either existence is identical to essence, or it is a part of it. The first is false because existence is common (مشترك) across all existents, whereas essences are distinct. The second is also false because if existence were a part, it would be the most common essential attribute (أعم الذاتيات المشتركة) and would serve as a genus for all essences. Consequently, the differentiae (فصول) of those essences would also have to be existent, leading to an infinite regress of parts for a single essence, which is impossible because every composite must ultimately be composed of simples.

الماهية
الجزء
التسلسل
البسيط
الذاتيات
الجنس
الفصول

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that existence being a part is impossible only due to infinite regress.
Correction: The argument also refutes identity based on commonality, so both options are eliminated.

Checkpoint

A) Because existence is a part, not the whole
B) Because existence is common while essences are distinct
C) Because existence is a genus
D) Because of infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Existence is neither identical to essence nor a part of it; both possibilities lead to logical impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the reality of your own existence, do you ever mistakenly think that your existence is just an added attribute to your essence, rather than the very fact that you are? How does this argument help you see that your existence is not a part of you but something given by the Creator?
?
Al-Jurjani calls those who think all reality is one entity 'absurd'. In your daily life, do you fall into the trap of treating different things as essentially the same, ignoring the distinct realities that God has created? How can this lesson sharpen your discernment?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible, requiring a simple ultimate cause. Have you ever felt that your own reasoning can go on forever without reaching a foundation? How does this argument push you to rely on the Necessary Being, who is absolutely simple, as the ground of all existence?

The Substance/Accident Objection and the Response
Al-Jurjani explains and responds to the objection that existence cannot be part of both substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's defense that existence can be an accidental part of substances and other accidents, and how he distinguishes carried from non-carried parts to rebut the objection.

Core Idea
Existence can be an accidental part of substance without predication.

Al-Jurjani here addresses a key objection to the view that existence is an accident: how can one accident (existence) be part of both substances and other accidents? He distinguishes two types of parts and shows that the objection only applies to one. This passage sharpens our understanding of the ontological categories used in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Jurjani uses a concessive structure: he concedes one point but denies another.
2
Identify the distinction between carried and non-carried parts
Find where Al-Jurjani distinguishes 'carried parts' (الأجزاء المحمولة) from 'non-carried parts' (غير المحمولة). Understand why this distinction matters for the objection.
Carried parts are those that are predicated of the whole; non-carried parts are not.
3
Examine the example of the bed's structure
The commentator uses the example of a bed's structure (الهيئة السريرية) to show how an accident can be a part of a substance without being predicated. See how this makes the objection fail for non-carried parts.
This example is central to understanding the response.
4
Follow the rebuttal about genus and common accident
Al-Jurjani then responds to the claim that existence is not a genus for differentiae by stating it is a 'common accident'. Trace his reasoning about why this is permissible and how it refutes the opponent's proof.
He argues that the opponent's own assumption of existence being a genus leads to impossibility.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Reflect on how Al-Jurjani defends the view that existence is an accident that can be part of both substances and accidents. The key is the distinction of part-types and the possibility of composition.
This lesson strengthens the broader kalam argument for God's existence by removing an objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فالوجود إما جوهر إلخ ) هذا في الأجزاء المحمولة مسلم لأنه يستلزم حمل الجوهر كالهيئة أو العرض على الجوهر مواطأة، وأما في غير المحمولة فيجوز أن يكون العرض جزء الجوهر كالهيئة السريرية للسرير قوله : ( بأن يقال : إلخ) أي يمنع قوله فلا يكون الوجود جنساً للفصول . قوله : (بل) كل جنس أي في الماهيات الحقيقية . قوله : ( عرض عام (له كيلا يتكرر الذاتي في الماهيات الحقيقية. قوله : ( وإنما جاز ذلك ) أي كونه عرضاً عاماً للفصول وحاصله أن منع كونه جنساً للفصول مقدمة دليله أعني قوله : إذ المفروض أنه جنس للموجودات، وذلك لأن مدعى من راجع إلى منع قوله : ( لا بد له من الانتهاء إلى البسيط فإن قلت كيف الانتهاء إليه والحال أن الفرض جنسية الوجود للموجودات ؟ قلت : المراد أن هذا الفرض يستلزم عدمه وأنه أشد استحالة. قوله : ( فلا يكون جزءاً للجوهر) قد يمنع ذلك بتجويز كون الجوهر مركباً من جوهر وعرض كما في السرير على أن اللازم هو الزيادة في البعض والمدعى أنه زائد في الكل.
His saying: ('Thus existence is either substance, etc.') This is conceded regarding carried parts, because it necessitates the predication of the substance, like the form, or the accident, upon the substance identically. As for non-carried parts, it is permissible that the accident be a part of the substance, like the structure of the bed for the bed. His saying: ('By saying, etc.') i.e., he prevents his saying: thus existence is not a genus for the differentiae. His saying: ('Rather, every genus') i.e., in real essences. His saying: ('A common accident for it') so that the essential is not repeated in real essences. His saying: ('And that is permissible') i.e., its being a common accident for the differentiae. Its summary is that preventing it from being a genus for the differentiae is a premise of his proof, namely his saying: 'since it is assumed that it is a genus for existents.' And that is because the claim of whoever refers back to preventing his saying: ('It must terminate in the simple')—if you say: how is termination to it when the assumption is that existence is a genus for existents? I say: the intended meaning is that this assumption necessitates its non-existence and that is more impossible. His saying: ('Thus it is not a part of substance') may be prevented by allowing that substance is composed of substance and accident, as in the bed, given that the necessary consequence is increase in some, while the claim is that it is increased in all.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that existence cannot be a part of both substances and accidents. The commentator explains that this holds for 'carried parts' (الأجزاء المحمولة) because it would entail that the accident is predicated of the substance. However, for non-carried parts (غير المحمولة), such as the structure of a bed being an accident that is part of the bed, it is possible. The commentator then responds to the claim that existence is a genus for the differentiae by pointing out that existence can be a common accident (عرض عام) for them, and this is permissible because the claim is that existence is additional. The passage further discusses that preventing existence from being a genus for the differentiae is a premise of the proof, and that the assumption of existence being a genus leads to impossibility, so the response refutes that.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
الأجزاء المحمولة
غير المحمولة
العرض العام
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence cannot be an accident for any essence, as it would make predication impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that existence can be an accidental part of substance in non-carried parts, as with the bed's structure.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can an accident be a part of a substance?
A) When the parts are carried parts
B) When the parts are non-carried parts
C) When the existence is a genus
D) When the differentiae are not existent

Key Takeaway
Existence is an accident that can be part of both substances and other accidents without contradiction, as shown by the distinction between carried and non-carried parts.
