The Stricture from the Sharedness of Existence
Philosophers argue existence is a specific nature; response denies it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that existence is a specific nature with uniform concomitants, and the response that denies this premise.

Core Idea
If existence were a specific nature, it would be additional to God's essence, but it is not.

The philosophers claim that existence in the Necessary Being is identical to essence. The theologian counters with a stricture: the philosophers themselves agree that a specific nature has uniform necessary concomitants. Since existence in contingent beings is additional to essence, it must also be additional in the Necessary Being. The response denies that existence is a specific nature at all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' position
Recall that the philosophers hold existence is identical to essence in the Necessary Being. This is the target of the argument.
The passage calls this an 'ilzam' (stricture) because it uses the philosophers' own premise against them.
2
Understand the premise about specific nature
The philosophers agree that a specific nature (ṭabī‘a naw‘iyya) has uniform necessary concomitants across all its individuals.
This is the key premise: what holds for one individual of a nature holds for all.
3
Follow the argument's logic
Existence is claimed to be a specific nature common to all existents, so its necessary concomitants do not differ. Since existence is established as additional to essences in contingent beings, it must also be additional in the Necessary Being.
Thus the philosophers' own view would contradict their identity thesis.
4
Study the response
The answer denies that existence is a specific nature. Instead, it is an accident that attaches to individuals with differing realities.
This blocks the argument because the premise only applies to genuine specific natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتفرع على ذواتها بخلاف الوجود الذي هو في الواجب فإنه مستغن عما عداه بالكلية ( الزام للحكماء ) القائلين بأن وجود الواجب عين ذاته، وهو الوجه الرابع من تلك الوجوه إلا أنه إلزامي فإن الحكماء اتفقوا على أن الطبيعة النوعية، يصح على كل فرد منها ما يصح على الآخر فنقول : ( الوجود طبيعة نوعية ) مشتركة بين الوجودات ( فلا تختلف لوازمه فلما ثبت كونه زائداً على ماهيات الممكنات عارضاً لها وجب أن يكون في الواجب كذلك والجواب منع كونه أي الوجود طبيعة نوعية بل هو أمر عارض لأفراده المتخالفة الحقائق
Branching upon essences, unlike existence which is in the Necessary Being, for it is completely independent of what is other than it. (A stricture upon the philosophers) who say that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to His essence. This is the fourth of those aspects, except that it is a stricture: for the philosophers agreed that the specific nature (al-ṭabī‘a al-naw‘iyya) is such that what is valid for every individual of it is valid for the other. So we say: Existence is a specific nature common among existents, so its necessary concomitants do not differ. Since it has been established that it is additional to the essences of contingent things and accidental to them, it must be likewise in the Necessary Being. And the answer is to deny that it is a specific nature; rather, it is something accidental to its individuals that have differing realities.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents an argument (ilzām) against the philosophers who claim that existence is identical to essence in the Necessary Being. The argument employs a premise agreed upon by the philosophers: a specific nature (ṭabī‘a naw‘iyya) has uniform necessary concomitants—what holds for one individual holds for all. Existence is then claimed to be such a nature, common to all existents. Since existence is known to be additional to the essences of contingent beings (mumkināt), by the premise it must also be additional to the essence of the Necessary Being. The response counters by denying the premise: existence is not a specific nature but an accident that attaches to individuals with distinct realities, so the argument fails.

Key Terms
طبيعة نوعية
وجود
ماهية
الزام
حكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes existence is a genus (jins), not a specific nature (naw'iyya).
Correction: The argument explicitly calls existence a specific nature (ṭabī‘a naw‘iyya), which has stricter uniformity than a genus.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' premise that the argument uses against them?
A) Existence is a genus.
B) A specific nature has uniform necessary concomitants across its individuals.
C) The Necessary Being has no essence.
D) Existence is accidental to all things.

Key Takeaway
The stricture fails because existence is not a specific nature with uniform concomitants, but an accident attaching to diverse realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is not a specific nature but accidental to you, then your existence is a gift that could have been otherwise. Do you live with the humble awareness that you do not own your existence, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you really internalised that your existence is contingent and dependent on God every moment?
?
The philosophers claim God's existence is identical to His essence—perfect, necessary, without addition. You, on the other hand, have an existence that is added to your essence, meaning you are a combination of what you are and that you are. Does this thought make you feel fragile, or does it fill you with gratitude? How often do you thank God not just for what you have, but for the very fact that you exist?
?
The response says existence attaches to individuals with differing realities—your existence is unique to you. Do you treat your existence as a generic commodity, or as a personalised gift from God meant for a specific purpose? Are you living that purpose, or are you wasting the accidental existence that could have been given to someone else?

The First Proof for Mental Existence
The author presents the first proof for mental existence with examples and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for mental existence: we conceive things without external reality, which demonstrates they exist in the mind.

Core Idea
Conceiving the impossible proves mental existence.

The philosophers argue that our ability to conceive impossible objects—like a square circle or the conjunction of opposites—shows that these things have a form of existence in the mind. This lesson explores their first proof and clarifies key terms to avoid misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the examples given: the absolutely impossible, conjunction of contradictories, and absolute non-existence.
The passage includes commentary markers like 'قوله' which indicate the commentator's explanations.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the core argument: we conceive X (which has no external reality) → therefore X has mental existence. This is a direct inference from our act of conception.
The proof relies on the fact that conception is a real mental act.
3
Examine the commentator's clarifications
The commentary explains key phrases: 'at all' means neither essentially nor derivatively; 'absolute' disregards individuality; 'non-existence opposed to existence' excludes things like 'not-blind' which actually exist. Understand how these refine the scope of mental existence.
These clarifications prevent misunderstandings about what counts as having no external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج مثبتوه وهم الحكماء بأمور الأول أنا نتصور ما لا وجود له في الخارج أصلاً (كالممتنع) مطلقاً (واجتماع النقيضين والضدين (والعدم المقابل للوجود) الخارجي (المطلق) أي من غير إضافة وتقييد بشيء مخصوص وحمل الإطلاق هاهنا ولا إلى أن المراد الخارجية بمعنى ما يكون في الذهن لا بمعنى ما يكون باعتبار الوجود الخارجي، فلا دور فإن جميعها مع كونه خروجاً عن ظاهر العبارة دعاوي لا دليل عليها، بل الدليل على خلافها فإنهم قالوا بأن المعقولات الثانية تعرض للمعقولات الأولى وإن العلة الغائية باعتبار الوجود الذهني علة لعلية الفاعل، وإن الحد التام موصل إلى كنه الشيء وإن الكيفيات النفسانية موجودة في الخارج يستلزم تعريف الشيء بما هو أخفى منه وأما ما قيل: إن معنى الوجود الخارجي بديهي وما ذكر تنبيه عليه فالمناقشة فيه غير مفيدة، ففيه أن مقصود المعترض أنه لا يحصل بهذا البيان الفرق بين الوجود الخارجي والذهني، الذي هو مناط تحرير محل النزاع على أن دعوى البداهة في محل النزاع غير مسموعة.، قوله : ( وعلى هذا إلخ فالقول بأن الحاصل في الذهن مثل الأشياء وأشباحها المخالفة لها في الحقيقة خروج عن محل النزاع. قوله : (عسير جدا منشأه توهم أن دليل المثبت يثبت وجود صور الأشياء في الذهن والدليل النافي ينفي وجود الهويات الخارجية . قوله : ( أصلاً) لا أصالة ولا تبعاً. قوله : ( مطلقاً) أي مع قطع النظر عن تحققه في فرد أي مفهوم الممتنع من حيث هو . قوله : ( والعدم المقابل للوجود احتراز عن المقابل للعدم كاللا أعمى فإنه موجود . قوله : ( المطلق) احتراز عن العدم المقابل للوجود المقيد كعدم وجود زيد، فإنه موجود بوجود عمرو قوله : (كالممتنع مطلقاً) أي الأعم من الذاتي والغيري أو أعم مما بعده أعني اجتماع النقيضين والضدين، ويمكن أن يكون معنى الإطلاق التمحض في الامتناع، فيكون المراد به الممتنع الذاتي وفيه احتمال آخر وهو أن يكون معنى الإطلاق تعميمه في أفراده، وعلى كل تقدير يكون ذلك اجتماع النقيضين بعده من قبيل ذكر الخاص بعد العام كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( والعدم المقابل للوجود (المطلق الظاهر أن تقييد العدم بالمقابل للوجود المطلق بناء على ما اشتهر من أن عدم العدم وجود فسلب العمى هو البصر بعينه، كما سيأتي في مباحث الوحدة والكثرة، فليس العدم مطلقا مما لا وجود له في الخارج، وأما تقييد الوجود بالمطلق فليس فيه كثير فائدة فليتأمل .
Its proponents, the philosophers, argued with several things: first, that we conceive what has no existence in the external world at all, such as the absolutely impossible, and the conjunction of two contradictories and opposites, and non-existence opposed to absolute external existence, i.e., without addition and restriction to a particular thing. The meaning of 'absolute' here is not that the intended externality is in the sense of what is in the mind, not in the sense of what is by consideration of external existence, so there is no circularity. For all these, besides being a departure from the apparent wording, are claims without proof, rather the proof is contrary to them. For they said that secondary intelligibles attach to primary intelligibles, and that the final cause by virtue of mental existence is the cause of the causality of the agent, and that the complete definition leads to the essence of the thing, and that psychic qualities existing externally entail defining a thing by what is more obscure than it. As for what was said: that the meaning of external existence is self-evident and what was mentioned is a reminder, then the discussion of it is useless. In response: the objector's intention is that this explanation does not produce the difference between external and mental existence, which is the criterion for settling the point of dispute; however, the claim of self-evidence in the point of dispute is not accepted. (Commentary): His words: 'And upon this, etc.' So the statement that what is obtained in the mind is like things and their forms that are different from them in reality is a departure from the point of dispute. His words: 'very difficult' – its origin is the misconception that the proof of the proponent establishes the existence of forms of things in the mind, and the proof of the opponent negates the existence of external identities. His words: 'at all' – not essentially nor derivatively. His words: 'absolutely' – i.e., disregarding its realization in an individual, i.e., the concept of the impossible as such. His words: 'and the non-existence opposed to existence' – a precaution against what is opposed to non-existence, like 'not-blind', for it exists. His words: 'the absolute' – a precaution against non-existence opposed to restricted existence, like the non-existence of Zayd, for it exists by the existence of Amr. His words: 'like the absolutely impossible' – i.e., the general (al-aʿamm) including the essential and the other, or more general than what follows, namely the conjunction of two contradictories and opposites. It is possible that the meaning of 'absolute' is pure impossibility, so the intended is the essentially impossible; and there is another probability: that the meaning of 'absolute' is its generality over its individuals. In any case, the conjunction of two contradictories after it is of the type of mentioning the specific after the general, as is not hidden. His words: 'and the non-existence opposed to existence (the absolute)' – apparently, restricting non-existence by 'opposed to absolute existence' is based on what is well-known: that the non-existence of non-existence is existence, so the negation of blindness is exactly sight, as will come in the discussions of unity and multiplicity. So non-existence is not absolutely something that has no existence externally. As for restricting existence by 'absolute', there is not much benefit in it, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the philosophers' proofs for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The proof is that we can conceive things that have no external existence whatsoever, such as the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ) in an absolute sense, the conjunction of two contradictories and opposites, and absolute non-existence (al-ʿadam al-muṭlaq). The very fact that we conceive them shows they have some mode of existence, namely mental existence. The author then clarifies that 'absolute' here does not imply a referent to external existence, thus avoiding circularity. He refutes objections that the explanation fails to distinguish external from mental existence, and rejects the claim that external existence is self-evident in this context. The commentary portion then explains each phrase: 'at all' means neither essentially nor derivatively; 'absolutely' means disregarding individuality; 'non-existence opposed to existence' excludes non-existence like 'not-blind' which actually exists; 'the absolute' restricts to non-existence opposed to absolute existence, not restricted non-existence like the non-existence of Zayd. The phrase 'like the absolutely impossible' is interpreted as broader than the subsequent examples, either as essential impossibility or as covering all individuals, and the conjunction of contradictories is a specific instance. Finally, it notes that restricting non-existence to that opposed to absolute existence is necessary because the non-existence of non-existence is existence, so not all non-existence lacks external existence. The passage thus carefully delimits the scope of the proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that conceiving the impossible implies its external existence.
Correction: The proof shows it only implies mental existence, not external reality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first proof for mental existence is that we conceive things with no external reality, such as the impossible and absolute non-existence, demonstrating that they exist in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you sometimes feel their presence in your doubts? How does this distinction between mental and external existence affect your certainty in tawhid?
?
The proof for mental existence shows that the mind has its own reality. Do you treat your thoughts as mere illusions, or do you recognize that your inner world is a real creation of Allah? How does this lesson change how you view your own ideas and intentions?

The Second Proof for Mental Existence: Positive Judgments on Non-Existent Things
Second proof for mental existence via positive judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how making true positive judgments about non-existent things proves they must exist in the mind.

Core Idea
True positive judgments require real subjects, even if only in the mind.

The second proof for mental existence turns our everyday positive statements about non-existent things into a philosophical argument. When we say 'the impossible is more general than the associate of God,' we are making a true judgment about something that has no outward existence. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why such judgments demand mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentary is densely interwoven with the main text.
2
Identify the key judgment
The passage gives examples of positive judgments about non-existent things, like 'the impossible is subject to common possibility.' List these examples and note that they are true in reality.
These judgments are not just verbal; they are true (sadiqa) in themselves.
3
Understand the logical requirement
The proof argues that for a judgment to be true, the subject must exist in some mode. Since these subjects have no external existence, they must exist mentally.
The key phrase: 'the existence of a thing for something else is a branch of its own existence.'
4
Explore the commentary distinctions
The commentary distinguishes between the act of judging (hukm) and the thing judged (mahkum bihi). It also clarifies that 'positive attributes' mean affirmative realities, not negative ones.
This clarifies potential misunderstandings about what exactly is being predicated.
5
Consider the objection
The passage ends with an objection: how can we judge the impossible if it is inconceivable? Though the text cuts off, think about how mental existence might answer this.
The objection challenges the very possibility of mental existence for impossibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحكم عليه أي على ما لا وجود له في الخارج بأحكام (ثبوتية صادقة ككونها محكوماً عليها بالإمكان العام وملزومة، أو لازمة لبعض الأشياء وكون الممتنع مثلاً أخص من المعدوم وأعم من شريك الباري وكونه متعقلاً، إلى غير ذلك من الأحكام الإيجابية الصادقة في نفس الأمر، سواء كانت صادقة على مفهوم الممتنع أو على ما صدق عليه (وإنه ) أي الحكم على تلك الأمور المتصورة بأحكام ثبوتية صادقة يستدعي ثبوتها إذ ثبوت الشيء لغيره) في نفس الأمر قوله : (لغو) إذ لا فائدة في التقييد ولم يقل مصادرة لكونه مثالاً لا يتوقف الاستدلال عليه . قوله: (ونحكم عليه أي حكماً إيجابياً فإنه المتبادر من الحكم عليه كما سيصرح به الشارح بقوله : من الأحكام الإيجابية . قوله : ( بأحكام (ثبوتية أي بأمور ثبوتية كما سيصرح به الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين. قوله : ( صادقة) أي على ما لا وجود له في الخارج في نفس الأمر. قوله : (ككونها إلخ) تمثيل للحكم المستفاد من قوله نحكم عليه لا للأحكام الثبوتية يدل عليه قوله : من الأحكام الإيجابية، ولم يقل ككونها ممكنة لأن الإمكان أمر سلبي بخلاف كونه محكوماً عليه . قوله : (سواء كانت إلخ تعميم لقوله : بأحكام ثبوتية لا لقوله من الأحكام الإيجابية لأنها لا تحمل على شيء إنما المحمول الأحكام بمعنى المحمولات . قوله : (صادقة على مفهوم الممتنع كالأخص والأعم. قوله : ( يستدعي (ثبوتها أي ثبوت تلك الأمور المتصورة، فالتذكير في قوله : عليه بالنظر إلى لفظ ما . والتأنيث هاهنا بالنظر إلى معناه وإليه أشار الشارح بقوله : على تلك الأمور المتصورة . قوله : (لغو) إذ هو بصدد بيان الوجود الذهني ولم يثبت بعد ولما لم يتوقف الدليل على هذا القيد بل تم بدونه لم يحكم بالمصادرة بل باللغوية. قوله : ( بأحكام ثبوتية إلخ الظاهر المراد بها هو المحمولات الثبوتية بالمعنى الذي سنذكره على أن الحكم بمعنى المحكوم به وليس المراد بها الأحكام الذهنية الإيجابية، وإن أشعر به قوله إلى غير ذلك من الأحكام الإيجابية الصادقة ما لا يخفى ويدل عليه قوله : ككونها محكوماً عليها بالإمكان العام فإنه مثال للمحكوم به لا الحكم والقضية الإيجابية هاهنا، هو قولنا شريك الباري محكوم عليه بالإمكان العام فالمحمول بحسب المعنى وإن كان بالاشتقاق ما ذكرته لا الإمكان العام حتى يرد أنه ليس مفهوماً ثبوتياً، بل هو سلب ضرورة أحد الطرفين يحتاج إلى الجواب، بأن المراد به هاهنا قابلية أحد الطرفين وهو أمر ثبوتي . قوله: (إذ ثبوت الشيء لغيره فرع ثبوته إلخ) اعترض عليه بأنا نعلم قطعاً أن اجتماع النقيضين محال وشريك الباري ممتنع وإن لم يوجد ذهن ولا قوة مدركة فيلزم ثبوت الممتنع في
And we pass judgment on it, i.e., on what has no existence externally, with true positive attributes (aḥkām thubūtiyya ṣādiqa), such as its being subject to common possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) and being a necessary condition (malzūma) or necessary consequent (lāzima) of some things, and such as the impossible being, for example, more specific than the non-existent and more general than the associate of God (sharīk al-bārī) and its being intelligible, and so on from among the true positive judgments in reality, whether they are true of the concept of the impossible or of what it applies to. And that—i.e., judging those conceived things with true positive judgments—requires their existence (thubūt), since the existence of a thing for something else [presupposes its own existence] in reality. (Commentary): His words: 'pointless' (laghw)—because there is no benefit in the restriction, and he did not say 'begging the question' because it is an example on which the inference does not depend. His words: 'we pass judgment on it'—i.e., a positive judgment, for that is what is understood from 'judgment', as the commentator will clarify with his words: 'from among positive judgments.' His words: 'with true positive attributes'—i.e., with affirmative things (umūr thubūtiyya), as the commentator will clarify in his glosses on Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn. His words: 'true'—i.e., on what has no existence externally in reality. His words: 'such as its being, etc.'—an example of the judgment derived from his words 'we judge it', not of the positive attributes; what indicates this is his words 'from among positive judgments'; and he did not say 'such as its being possible' because possibility is a negative thing, unlike its being subject to judgment. His words: 'whether they are, etc.'—a generalization of his words 'with true positive attributes', not of his words 'from among positive judgments', because these are not predicated of something; rather the predicate is the judgments in the sense of predicated things. His words: 'true of the concept of the impossible, like the more specific and the more general.' His words: 'requires their existence'—i.e., the existence of those conceived things; the masculine pronoun in 'upon it' (ʿalayhi) looks to the word 'what' (mā), while the feminine here looks to its meaning; the commentator indicated this with his words: 'upon those conceived things.' His words: 'pointless'—because he is in the process of explaining mental existence and it has not yet been established; and since the proof does not depend on this restriction but is complete without it, he did not rule it as begging the question but as pointless. His words: 'with positive attributes, etc.'—apparently what is meant by them is the positive predicates (al-maḥmūlāt al-thubūtiyya) in the sense that we will mention, on the understanding that 'judgment' means the thing judged (al-maḥkūm bihī); what is meant is not the positive mental judgments (al-aḥkām al-dhihniyya al-ījābiyya), even if his words 'and so on from among the true positive judgments' suggest it, as is not hidden. This is indicated by his words: 'such as its being subject to common possibility'—for that is an example of the thing judged (al-maḥkūm bihī), not the judgment (al-ḥukm). The positive proposition here is our statement: 'The associate of God is subject to common possibility'; so the predicate according to meaning, even if by derivation it is what I mentioned, is not common possibility, so that it might be objected that this is not an affirmative concept but rather a negation of the necessity of one of the two sides. This requires an answer: that what is meant here is the possibility of one of the two sides, which is an affirmative thing. His words: 'since the existence of a thing for something else is a branch of its existence, etc.'—an objection was raised against it: we know with certainty that the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible and the associate of God is impossible, even if there is no mind or perceiving power, so it would follow that the impossible exists in ...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for mental existence: we make true positive judgments about things that have no external existence. For example, we say that the impossible is subject to common possibility (imkān ʿāmm), or that it is more specific than the non-existent and more general than the associate of God (sharīk al-bārī), and that it is intelligible. These judgments are true in reality (fī nafs al-amr). The proof argues that such true positive judgments require the existence (thubūt) of the subjects judged, because 'the existence of a thing for something else is a branch of its own existence'—i.e., before something can be subject to a predicate, it must exist in some mode. Since these subjects have no external existence, they must have mental existence. The commentary then clarifies each phrase: 'pointless' (laghw) refers to a restriction in the matn that is unnecessary for the proof; 'positive judgment' emphasizes that the judgment is affirmative; 'positive attributes' (aḥkām thubūtiyya) are affirmed realities (umūr thubūtiyya). The commentary distinguishes between the judgment (al-ḥukm) as the act of judging and the thing judged (al-maḥkūm bihī). The example of 'common possibility' is actually an example of the thing judged, not the judgment itself, and it is interpreted as a positive attribute because it means the possibility (or receptivity) of one of the two sides, which is an affirmative concept. The passage concludes with an objection: if the impossible is inconceivable even in the mind, then how can we judge it? This objection is acknowledged but the text cuts off before the answer. The proof thus establishes mental existence as the necessary ground for our true affirmative statements about non-entities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Positive judgments about impossible things are merely verbal and require no real existence.
Correction: The proof shows that for a judgment to be true (sadiqa), the subject must exist in some mode; hence mental existence is necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude from our positive judgments about non-existent things?
A) Such judgments are false.
B) Those things must exist outwardly.
C) Those things must exist in the mind.
D) Those things are simply nothing.

Key Takeaway
True positive judgments about non-existent things prove that those things have mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You make judgments all the time about things that don't exist in the outside world—impossible scenarios, abstract concepts, even falsehoods. If every true judgment presupposes a real subject in your mind, what does that say about the reality of every thought you entertain? Are your mental judgments merely idle, or do they carry real weight before Allah? How will you take greater responsibility for the judgments you form in your heart?
?
The proof uses the example of 'the associate of God' (sharīk al-bārī) to show that even an impossible concept is intelligible and subject to true judgments. Have you ever entertained thoughts about Allah that are impossible or unbefitting of Him—even if only to reject them? What does this passage teach you about the accountability of every mental conception, no matter how fleeting? Commit to guarding your thoughts about Allah from this day forward.
?
The passage argues that positive judgments about impossibles require their existence in the mind. Think of a time you strongly believed something false about yourself or others. That belief was a positive judgment about something that had no external reality. In light of this lesson, how can you use the reality of mental existence to purify your inner world and align your judgments with the truth? What specific step will you take this week to examine one false judgment you hold?

Objections to Mental Existence and Partial Answer
Two objections and start of commentator's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key objections to the theory of mental existence and how the commentator begins to address them.

Core Idea
Even impossibles and absolute non-existents have mental existence when conceived.

Having established that mental existence is required for true judgments, the commentator now faces objections. How can impossibles like 'the associate of God' exist even mentally? And what about the absolutely non-existent, which seems to vanish as soon as we speak of it? This passage presents these objections and begins a delicate answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage has three parts: an objection, another objection, and the start of an answer.
The answer is incomplete in this excerpt.
2
Examine the first objection
The first objection argues that even impossibles (like the conjunction of opposites) would have to exist mentally if mental existence is required for true judgments. Identify the problem this poses.
The objector assumes mental existence implies some mode of reality that seems inappropriate for impossibles.
3
Examine the second objection
The second objection points to a paradox: if 'the absolutely non-existent' is said to be unknown, that statement itself attributes existence to it (as a subject of judgment), creating a contradiction.
This objection is more subtle and targets the very act of speaking about nothing.
4
Observe the beginning of the answer
The commentator begins his reply by saying the objector's statement would be true in some way (the rest of the answer is cut off). Notice that he does not reject the objections outright but works to resolve them.
The full answer continues on later pages.

اعترض عليه بأنا نعلم قطعاً أن اجتماع النقيضين محال وشريك الباري ممتنع وإن لم يوجد ذهن ولا قوة مدركة فيلزم ثبوت الممتنع في نفسه وإذ ليس ثبوت تلك الأمور المتصورة في الخارج فهو في الذهن وهو المطلوب. فإن قلت: لو صح هذا الذي ذكرتم من أن المحكوم عليه بالأحكام الثبوتية الصادقة يجب أن يكون موجوداً، إما خارجاً أو ذهناً لصدق قولنا: المعدوم المطلق الذي لا وجود له أصلاً لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن لا يعلم ولا يخبر عنه لأن كونه معلوماً ومخبراً عنه في نفس الأمر يستلزم وجوده في الجملة وإذ لا وجود له أصلا فلا علم ولا إخبار وإنه تناقض لأن المعدوم المطلق صار محكوماً عليه باتصافه بعدم العلم والإخبار عنه فيكون معدوماً مطلقاً وموجوداً في الجملة قلنا: اللازم مما ذكرنا أنه يصدق قولكم الذي
It was objected: we know for certain that the conjunction of two opposites is impossible, and the associate of God is impossible, even if there is no mind or perceiving power, so it follows that the impossible exists in itself. And since those conceived things do not exist externally, they exist in the mind — and that is what is sought. If you say: if this statement of yours — that the subject of true positive judgments must exist, either externally or mentally — is correct, then our statement would be true: 'The absolutely non-existent, which has absolutely no existence, neither externally nor mentally, is not known nor spoken of,' because its being known and spoken of in reality requires its existence in some way; and since it has no existence at all, there is no knowledge or speech [about it]; and that is a contradiction, because the absolutely non-existent has become subject to the attribution of not being known or spoken of, so it is both absolutely non-existent and existent in some way. We said: the necessary consequence of what we mentioned is that your statement — which...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage contains two objections to the proof of mental existence and the beginning of a response. The first objection argues that if mental existence is required for true judgments, then even impossible things like the associate of God would have to exist mentally, which seems problematic. The second objection points out a contradiction: if we say 'the absolutely non-existent is not known or spoken of,' that judgment itself requires the subject ('the absolutely non-existent') to have some existence, making it existent in some sense. The commentator begins his answer by stating that the implication of his argument is that the objector's own statement would be true in some way (the rest of the answer is on later pages). The passage highlights the subtlety of mental existence: even things that are impossible or non-existent can have a form of conceptual existence in the mind when they are thought about or referred to.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence would give impossibles real existence in the external world.
Correction: Mental existence is only conceptual; impossibles can be conceived but do not have external reality.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection about the absolutely non-existent?

B) It is subject to true positive judgments, creating a paradox.
C) It has no external existence.
D) It is the same as the impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objections show that mental existence theory must carefully handle impossibles and absolute non-existents without granting them external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that 'the conjunction of opposites is impossible'—but do you ever hold contradictory beliefs about yourself or your Creator? What does this passage teach you about the danger of giving mental existence to falsehoods, even unintentionally? Will you examine your thoughts today for hidden contradictions?
?
The absolutely non-existent cannot be spoken of, yet you speak of it. When you think about sins, doubts, or even shirk, you grant them a form of existence in your mind. Are you willing to stop lending mental existence to what displeases Allah? What will you do the next time such a thought arises?
?
The commentator does not reject the objection but engages with it honestly. Is your faith strong enough to face difficult questions without dismissing them? Will you seek to understand before you defend, trusting that truth can withstand scrutiny?

Negative Propositions Do Not Require Subject Existence
Sanusi states the negative nature of the proposition and the logical rule for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a true negative proposition does not require the existence of its subject, focusing on the proposition 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent.'

Core Idea
A true negative proposition does not require subject existence—only an affirmative does.

In this lesson, we examine the opening argument: the proposition in question is negative, so it does not entail that the subject (absolute non-existent) exists. We will see how this logical rule resolves the initial tension and sets the stage for further objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the author first classifies the proposition as negative.
The passage is dense; focus on the first few lines.
2
Identify the key logical rule
The author states: 'The true negative does not require the existence of the subject; rather, what requires it is the true affirmative.' Mark this rule.
3
Apply the rule to the proposition
Understand that the proposition 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent' is negative, so it does not demand that the absolute non-existent exists.
This diffuses the implied contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this rule matter? How does it protect the doctrine of God's attributes?
The absolute non-existent is a key concept in kalam for discussing what is not God.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرتموه قضية ( سالبة بمعنى أنه ليس بمعدوم مطلق يعلم ويخبر عنه ) والسالبة الصادقة لا تقتضي وجود الموضوع، بل المقتضي له هو الموجبة الصادقة فلا تناقض، ( لا ) أنه يصدق بمعنى ( أن ثمة أمراً يصدق عليه في نفس الأمر أنه معدوم مطلق، وصفته أنه لا يعلم ولا يخبر عنه حتى يكون قضية موجبة معدولة مقتضية لوجود الموضوع، فإن عاد وقال : لو صح ما ذكرتم لما صدق قولنا : المعدوم المطلق مقابل للموجود المطلق قلنا : مفهوم المعدوم المطلق من حيث هو هو مقابل للموجود محكوماً عليه، وأن لا يكون محكوماً عليه كما قالوا في مسألة المجهول المطلق لأن الكلام هاهنا مسوق لنفي الوجود الذهني فالمناسب أن يقال : لو صح ما ذكرتم يلزم أن يكون معدوماً وموجوداً، بخلاف مسألة المجهول المطلق، فإنها مسوقة لنفي استدعاء كل تصديق للتصورات الثلاث. قوله : (قلنا اللازم مما ذكرنا إلخ لا يخفى أن ما ذكره قولنا كل من [؟] عليه بحكم ثبوتي صادق يجب أن يكون موجوداً مطلقاً، وهو ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يكون موجوداً مطلقاً أي كل ما هو معدوم مطلقاً، لا يكون محكوماً عليه بحكم ثبوتي صادق على أن يكون قضية موجبة معدولة الطرفين لأن عكس الموجبة الكلية الموجبة الكلية على طريقة القدماء، فلعله بنى الجواب على طريقة المتأخرين، وهو أن عكس الموجبة الكلية السالبة الكلية المركبة من نقيض المحمول وعين الموضوع كما بينه بقوله يصدق سالبة بمعنى أنه ليس بمعدوم مطلق يعلم ويخبر عنه. قوله : (لا تقتضي وجود الموضوع الذي هو مناط لصدق الإيجاب وإن اقتضى تصور الموضوع، وهو لا يستلزم ثبوت الوجود الذهني له ولو كفى مجرد التصور في ذلك لكفى في الاستدلال أن يقال : إنا نتصور ما لا وجود له في الخارج، فيكون موجوداً في الذهن. قوله: (مقتضية لوجود الموضوع على ما هو التحقيق وأما إذا قلنا: بعدم اقتضائها للوجود فالنقض ساقط من أصله . قوله : ( فإن عاد إلخ ) أي عاد الناقض وحرر النقض باعتبار مفهوم المعدوم المطلق، وقال: الحكم، لأنه خروج عن السوق فالجواب تام لكن في تفريع السؤال عما قبله مناقشة ظاهرة، لأن المحمول فيما ذكر أمر عدمي لان المذكور فيما سبق أن الحكم بالمحمولات المطلق على الأمر، حتى يقال : معنى معدوم مطلق مسلوب عنه الوجود المطلق، فيكون موجبة سالبة المحمول، وهي عندهم لا تقتضي أيضاً وجود الموضوع. قوله : ( مفهوم المعدوم إلخ) يعني لا منافاة بين كون مفهوم المعدوم المطلق مقابلاً لموجود المطلق وفرداً منه، فإنه من حيث هو مع قطع النظر عن وجوده في الذهن مقابل له، ومن حيث إنه متصور موجود في الذهن فرد منه ولا استحالة فيه، فإن مفهوم التصديق مقابل للتصور الساذج من حيث هو ومن حيث حصوله في الذهن تصور ساذج وأمثال ذلك كثير.
You mentioned it as a negative proposition, meaning that it is not an absolute non-existent that is known and reported about. And the true negative does not require the existence of the subject; rather, what requires it is the true affirmative, so there is no contradiction. No, it is true in the sense that there is an affair that is truly described as absolute non-existent, and its description is that it is not known nor reported about, so that it becomes an affirmative qualified proposition requiring the existence of the subject. Then if he returns and says: If what you mentioned were correct, our statement 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent' would not be true. We say: The concept of absolute non-existent in itself is opposite to the existent as a subject of judgment, and that it not be a subject of judgment, as they said in the issue of the absolute unknown, because the discourse here is directed to negate mental existence, so the appropriate thing is to say: If what you mentioned were correct, it would necessitate that it be non-existent and existent, unlike the issue of the absolute unknown, for it is directed to negate that every assent requires the three concepts. His statement: 'We say: The necessary from what we mentioned, etc.' It is not hidden that what he mentioned is our statement: Everyone who is judged by a true affirmative judgment must be absolutely existent, and it is converted by contrapositive to our statement: Everything that is not absolutely existent, i.e., everything that is absolutely non-existent, is not judged by a true affirmative judgment, on condition that it be an affirmative qualified proposition of both terms, because the conversion of the universal affirmative is universal affirmative according to the method of the ancients. Perhaps he built the answer on the method of the later scholars, which is that the conversion of the universal affirmative is the universal negative composed of the contrary of the predicate and the subject itself, as he clarified by his statement: It is true as a negative, meaning that it is not an absolute non-existent that is known and reported about. His statement: 'It does not require the existence of the subject which is the basis for the truth of affirmation, although it requires the conceptualization of the subject, and that does not entail the establishment of mental existence for it. If mere conceptualization sufficed for that, then it would suffice in the inference to say: We conceptualize what has no existence in the external, so it would exist in the mind.' His statement: 'Requiring the existence of the subject according to verification; but if we say: It does not require existence, then the rebuttal falls away entirely.' His statement: 'If he returns, etc.' i.e., the rebutter returned and refined the rebuttal with respect to the concept of absolute non-existent, and said: The judgment, because it is out of context, so the answer is complete, but in the derivation of the question from what preceded there is an apparent criticism, because the predicate in what was mentioned is a non-existential matter, since what was previously mentioned is that judgment by affirmative predicates, i.e., those which negation does not enter into their concept, requires one of the two existences, so his statement 'If this were correct, etc.' would not be true except with difficulty, so reflect. And the lack of knowledge and reporting about it is not an affirmative concept such that it would require the existence of the subject, and contradiction would occur by virtue of it, unless the predicate is considered the attribute of both, as the commentator pointed out, but that is far from the author's wording, so contemplate. His statement: 'Until it becomes an affirmative qualified proposition, etc.' The qualification of the proposition and its requirement of the subject's existence is not by considering the attribution of absolute non-existent to the matter, such that it is said: The meaning of absolute non-existent is that absolute existence is negated from it, so it becomes an affirmative with a negative predicate, and according to them that does not require the existence of the subject either. His statement: 'The concept of absolute non-existent, etc.' means there is no incompatibility between the concept of absolute non-existent being opposite to absolute existent and a part of it. For it, in itself, disregarding its existence in the mind, is opposite to it, and in terms of being conceived and existing in the mind, it is a part of it, and there is no impossibility in that. For the concept of assent is opposite to bare concept in itself, and in terms of its occurrence in the mind it is a bare concept, and many such examples.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a dense logical defense of the claim that the proposition 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent' does not entail that the absolute non-existent has any existence, because it is a negative proposition. The author begins by stating that the proposition in question is a negative one (meaning 'it is not an absolute non-existent that is known and reported about'), and a true negative proposition does not require the existence of its subject; only a true affirmative does. Therefore, there is no contradiction. He then considers an objection: someone might claim that the proposition is actually an affirmative but qualified ('there is an affair that is truly described as absolute non-existent...'), which would require existence. The answer is that the concept of absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent in itself, but that does not entail that the concept itself exists in reality; the judgment is about the concept as such. The commentary elaborates on logical conversion and the distinction between existential and non-existential predicates, emphasizing that a true affirmative judgment with a positive predicate requires existence, but here the predicate is non-existential (lack of knowledge and reporting). The passage concludes by clarifying that the concept of absolute non-existent can be both opposite to the existent (in its meaning) and a part of the existent (as a concept in the mind) without contradiction, just as the concept of assent is opposite to the bare concept but itself is a type of concept.

Key Terms
قضية سالبة
قضية موجبة
معدوم مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any true proposition requires the subject to actually exist in reality or in the mind.
Correction: This passage shows that negative propositions (e.g., 'X is not Y') do not require X to exist; only affirmative propositions with positive predicates do.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a true negative proposition require regarding the subject?
A) It requires the subject to exist in external reality.
B) It requires the subject to exist in the mind.
C) It does not require the existence of the subject.
D) It requires the subject to be possible.

Key Takeaway
A true negative proposition does not require its subject to exist, so the proposition about the absolute non-existent does not entail its existence.

Defending the Proposition: Concept vs. Existence
Sanusi defends the truth of the proposition by distinguishing the concept from its mental existence and using logical conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the proposition by distinguishing the concept of absolute non-existent from its existence in the mind or reality.

Core Idea
The concept of absolute non-existent in itself is opposite to the existent, but as a mental concept it exists.

After establishing that the proposition is negative, the author faces a stronger objection: if the subject does not exist at all, how can the proposition be true? The answer lies in the distinction between the concept (what is meant) and the existence of that concept. This lesson unpacks that defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'فإن عاد' to the end. Focus on how the author responds to the objection and uses logical conversion.
The text is complex; read the translation carefully.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims: if your view is correct, then the statement 'the absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent' would be false. Note why.
3
Grasp the answer: concept vs. existence
The author says the concept of absolute non-existent in itself is opposite, but when considered as a mental concept, it is a part of the existent (i.e., it exists in the mind). This resolves the apparent contradiction.
This is a key move in kalam to discuss non-existents.
4
Examine the logical conversion
The author discusses conversion of universal affirmative and shows how the earlier rule about negative propositions aligns with this.
This is technical; focus on the main point.

The Third Argument: Real Propositions Require Mental Existence
Sanusi presents the third argument for mental existence using the real proposition example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the real proposition 'the impossible is non-existent' cannot be true without mental existence, thus proving mental existence.

Core Idea
Real propositions prove mental existence: even 'nothing' must be mentally conceived to be judged.

Sanusi presents a third argument for mental existence based on the nature of universal propositions. The argument uses the example 'the impossible is non-existent'—a true proposition whose subject has no external reality. This lesson unpacks the argument and shows why mental existence is indispensable for such judgments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the real proposition
Learn the definition of a real proposition as a judgment about whatever the subject applies to in reality, including mental individuals.
This is different from external propositions that only apply to existent individuals.
2
Examine the example
Read the example 'the impossible is non-existent' and see how it illustrates the need for mental existence.
The impossible has no external individuals, so the judgment must be about mental individuals.
3
Trace the implication
Follow the logic: if mental individuals did not exist, this affirmative judgment would be false.
This is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر الثالث لولا الوجود الذهني لم يمكن أخذ القضية الحقيقية للموضوع وهي التي حكم فيها على ما يصدق عليه في نفس الأمر الكلي الواقع عنواناً سواء كان موجوداً في الخارج محققاً أو مقدراً أو لا يكون موجوداً فيه أصلاً والتالي باطل وقد أشار إلى بيان الملازمة وبطلان التالي معاً بقوله فإنا إذا قلنا الممتنع معدوم فلا نريد به أن للممتنع أي ما يصدق عليه الممتنع في الخارج معدوم فيه قطعاً أي لا نريد ذلك قطعاً إذ ليس في الخارج ما يصدق عليه الممتنع أصلاً بل نريد به أن الأفراد المعقولة للممتنع أي يصدق عليها الممتنع في العقل من الأفراد المعقولة للمعدوم أي يصدق عليها في العقل بحسب نفس الأمر أنها معدومة في الخارج فلو لم يكن للممتنع أفراد معقولة موجودة في العقل لم يصدق عليها الحكم الإيجابي فلذلك قال وهذا بالحقيقة عائد إلى الأول والحاصل أن قولنا الممتنع معدوم في الخارج قضية صادقة وليست خارجية بل حقيقية مفسرة بما ذكرناه لا بما اشتهر من أن الحكم فيها على الأفراد الخارجية فقط إما محققة أو مقدرة فلولا أن يكون للممتنع أفراد موجودة في الذهن لم يصدق هذا الحكم الإيجابي في هذه القضية الحقيقية ويرد عليه أن مفهوم المعدوم أمر سلبي قوله ويرد عليه إلخ فيه أنك قد عرفت من التقرير والمذكور أن ليس الاستدلال مختصاً بهذا القول المخصوص قيل هو مجرد تمثيل فالمناقشة فيه لا تنفع كيف وجميع المسائل المنطقية أحكام إيجابية بمفهومات ثبوتية هي معقولات ثانية على معقولات أولى نحو كل جنس كذا فلولا الوجود الذهني لم تكن تلك الأحكام صادقة
The third point: Without mental existence, it would not be possible to take the real proposition of the subject, which is that in which judgment is made upon that which the universal applies to in itself, the universal being a title, whether it exists externally actually or hypothetically or does not exist in it at all, and the consequent is false. And he indicated the explanation of the implication and the falsity of the consequent together by his saying: For if we say 'the impossible is non-existent', we do not mean by it that for the impossible, i.e., that which 'the impossible' applies to, externally it is non-existent in it absolutely—i.e., we do not mean that absolutely, since there is nothing externally that 'the impossible' applies to at all—but we mean by it that the conceived individuals of the impossible, i.e., 'the impossible' applies to them in the mind, from the conceived individuals of the non-existent, i.e., it applies to them in the mind according to reality that they are non-existent externally. So if there were not for the impossible conceived individuals existing in the mind, the affirmative judgment would not apply to them. Therefore he said: 'And this in reality returns to the first.' The conclusion is that our saying 'the impossible is non-existent externally' is a true proposition and not external but real, interpreted as we mentioned, not as is commonly known that the judgment in it is on external individuals only, either actual or hypothetical. So if there were not for the impossible individuals existing in the mind, this affirmative judgment would not be true in this real proposition. And it is objected to it that the concept of 'non-existent' is a negative matter. His statement: 'And it is objected to it, etc.' [the commentary]: In it [the answer] is that you have known from the explanation and the mentioned that the proof is not specific to this particular statement. It is said: it is merely an example, so the objection to it does not benefit. How? And all logical issues are affirmative judgments with positive concepts that are secondary intelligibles about primary intelligibles, like 'every genus is such', so without mental existence those judgments would not be true.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and defends it against an objection. The argument is based on the nature of universal affirmative propositions (al-qadiyya al-haqiqiyya). The author states that without mental existence, it would be impossible to formulate a real proposition—that is, a proposition that judges about whatever the subject term applies to in reality, whether the subject exists externally or not. The example given is 'the impossible is non-existent'. This proposition is true, but its subject ('the impossible') has no external reality. The meaning is not that there is some external impossible thing that is non-existent; rather, the judgment applies to the mental individuals of the impossible—the conceived instances of 'impossible' that are in the mind. If these mental individuals did not exist in the mind, the affirmative judgment 'the impossible is non-existent' could not be true. The author notes that this example ultimately reduces to the first argument. The conclusion is that the proposition is a real proposition (haqiqiyya), not an external one (kharijiyya), so it requires mental existence of the subject's instances. An objection is raised: 'the concept of non-existent is a negative matter' (mafhum al-ma'dum amr salbi), implying that negative concepts do not require existence. The response, given in the commentary, is that the proof is not limited to this specific example; it is merely an illustration. All logical propositions are affirmative judgments with positive concepts (mafhumat thubutiyya) that are secondary intelligibles about primary intelligibles. Without mental existence, these judgments would not be true. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
القضية الحقيقية
القضية الخارجية
الممتنع
الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think universal propositions only apply to external existents, but real propositions cover conceived individuals as well.
Correction: Real propositions require mental individuals for their truth, as shown by the example 'the impossible is non-existent'.

Checkpoint
Why does the example 'the impossible is non-existent' require mental existence?
A) Because the impossible actually exists in the mind.
B) Because the proposition is false unless the subject exists externally.
C) Because the judgment applies to mental individuals of the impossible, which must exist in the mind for the proposition to be true.
D) Because 'non-existent' is a positive concept.

Key Takeaway
Real propositions depend on mental individuals, proving that mental existence is necessary for reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that even 'the impossible' must have mental individuals for a true judgment. Think of something you have always considered utterly impossible—like a square circle. Can you conceive it? That conception is real in your mind. How does this humble you about the power and limits of your own intellect? Will you now pause before dismissing something as 'impossible' without considering its mental reality?
?
Sanusi shows that a true proposition about non-existence still requires something to exist in the mind. Consider a truth you hold about something that does not exist—like 'there is no partner with God'. Does your mind contain a mental individual of a 'partner' to negate? How does this make you more aware of the reality of your thoughts and their accountability? Commit to examining one such belief today and trace its mental existence.
?
The argument reduces to the first argument: mental existence is necessary for conceptualization. When you last reflected on your own existence, did you consider that your very thoughts about yourself are mental existents? How does the knowledge that your concepts have reality affect your understanding of self-knowledge? Will you strive to be more conscious of the mental world you inhabit?

Defending the Argument: Objection and Refutation
Sanusi presents an objection to his argument and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the concept of 'non-existent' is negative and the response that all logical propositions rely on positive concepts, thus reinforcing the need for mental existence.

Core Idea
Even negative concepts require positive mental existence for logical judgments.

After presenting the third argument, Sanusi anticipates an objection: 'the concept of non-existent is a negative matter.' This lesson examines the objection and its refutation, showing that the argument is not limited to this example but applies to all logical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection that the concept of 'non-existent' is negative, implying it doesn't require existence.
This challenges the example's validity.
2
Understand the response
The response clarifies that the proof is not restricted to this example; it's about all affirmative logical judgments with positive concepts.
The objection fails because it misses the general principle.
3
Apply the principle
See the statement: all logical issues are affirmative judgments with positive concepts, which need mental existence.
This reinforces the necessity of mental existence for any universal judgment.

الأمر الثالث لولا الوجود الذهني لم يمكن أخذ القضية الحقيقية للموضوع وهي التي حكم فيها على ما يصدق عليه في نفس الأمر الكلي الواقع عنواناً سواء كان موجوداً في الخارج محققاً أو مقدراً أو لا يكون موجوداً فيه أصلاً والتالي باطل وقد أشار إلى بيان الملازمة وبطلان التالي معاً بقوله فإنا إذا قلنا الممتنع معدوم فلا نريد به أن للممتنع أي ما يصدق عليه الممتنع في الخارج معدوم فيه قطعاً أي لا نريد ذلك قطعاً إذ ليس في الخارج ما يصدق عليه الممتنع أصلاً بل نريد به أن الأفراد المعقولة للممتنع أي يصدق عليها الممتنع في العقل من الأفراد المعقولة للمعدوم أي يصدق عليها في العقل بحسب نفس الأمر أنها معدومة في الخارج فلو لم يكن للممتنع أفراد معقولة موجودة في العقل لم يصدق عليها الحكم الإيجابي فلذلك قال وهذا بالحقيقة عائد إلى الأول والحاصل أن قولنا الممتنع معدوم في الخارج قضية صادقة وليست خارجية بل حقيقية مفسرة بما ذكرناه لا بما اشتهر من أن الحكم فيها على الأفراد الخارجية فقط إما محققة أو مقدرة فلولا أن يكون للممتنع أفراد موجودة في الذهن لم يصدق هذا الحكم الإيجابي في هذه القضية الحقيقية ويرد عليه أن مفهوم المعدوم أمر سلبي قوله ويرد عليه إلخ فيه أنك قد عرفت من التقرير والمذكور أن ليس الاستدلال مختصاً بهذا القول المخصوص قيل هو مجرد تمثيل فالمناقشة فيه لا تنفع كيف وجميع المسائل المنطقية أحكام إيجابية بمفهومات ثبوتية هي معقولات ثانية على معقولات أولى نحو كل جنس كذا فلولا الوجود الذهني لم تكن تلك الأحكام صادقة
The third point: Without mental existence, it would not be possible to take the real proposition of the subject, which is that in which judgment is made upon that which the universal applies to in itself, the universal being a title, whether it exists externally actually or hypothetically or does not exist in it at all, and the consequent is false. And he indicated the explanation of the implication and the falsity of the consequent together by his saying: For if we say 'the impossible is non-existent', we do not mean by it that for the impossible, i.e., that which 'the impossible' applies to, externally it is non-existent in it absolutely—i.e., we do not mean that absolutely, since there is nothing externally that 'the impossible' applies to at all—but we mean by it that the conceived individuals of the impossible, i.e., 'the impossible' applies to them in the mind, from the conceived individuals of the non-existent, i.e., it applies to them in the mind according to reality that they are non-existent externally. So if there were not for the impossible conceived individuals existing in the mind, the affirmative judgment would not apply to them. Therefore he said: 'And this in reality returns to the first.' The conclusion is that our saying 'the impossible is non-existent externally' is a true proposition and not external but real, interpreted as we mentioned, not as is commonly known that the judgment in it is on external individuals only, either actual or hypothetical. So if there were not for the impossible individuals existing in the mind, this affirmative judgment would not be true in this real proposition. And it is objected to it that the concept of 'non-existent' is a negative matter. His statement: 'And it is objected to it, etc.' [the commentary]: In it [the answer] is that you have known from the explanation and the mentioned that the proof is not specific to this particular statement. It is said: it is merely an example, so the objection to it does not benefit. How? And all logical issues are affirmative judgments with positive concepts that are secondary intelligibles about primary intelligibles, like 'every genus is such', so without mental existence those judgments would not be true.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and defends it against an objection. The argument is based on the nature of universal affirmative propositions (al-qadiyya al-haqiqiyya). The author states that without mental existence, it would be impossible to formulate a real proposition—that is, a proposition that judges about whatever the subject term applies to in reality, whether the subject exists externally or not. The example given is 'the impossible is non-existent'. This proposition is true, but its subject ('the impossible') has no external reality. The meaning is not that there is some external impossible thing that is non-existent; rather, the judgment applies to the mental individuals of the impossible—the conceived instances of 'impossible' that are in the mind. If these mental individuals did not exist in the mind, the affirmative judgment 'the impossible is non-existent' could not be true. The author notes that this example ultimately reduces to the first argument. The conclusion is that the proposition is a real proposition (haqiqiyya), not an external one (kharijiyya), so it requires mental existence of the subject's instances. An objection is raised: 'the concept of non-existent is a negative matter' (mafhum al-ma'dum amr salbi), implying that negative concepts do not require existence. The response, given in the commentary, is that the proof is not limited to this specific example; it is merely an illustration. All logical propositions are affirmative judgments with positive concepts (mafhumat thubutiyya) that are secondary intelligibles about primary intelligibles. Without mental existence, these judgments would not be true. Thus, the objection fails.

مفهوم المعدوم
معقولات ثانية
معقولات أولى
القضية الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that negative concepts do not require mental existence.
Correction: Even negative judgments involve positive concepts that require mental existence for their truth.

Checkpoint

A) By stating that the concept of 'non-existent' is actually positive.
B) By arguing that the proof applies to all affirmative logical judgments with positive concepts, not just this one example.
C) By denying that the objection is valid.
D) By claiming that the objector misunderstands the concept of 'non-existent'.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the argument rests on the necessity of mental existence for all affirmative logical judgments, not just this example.

Now that you know mental existence is necessary for all logical judgments, will you revisit those arguments with a new respect for the mind's role? What would it take for you to change your approach?
?
This lesson reveals that even the concept of 'non-existence' must exist in your mind for you to think about it. When you think about Allah's attributes, are you aware that your finite mind is actively constructing concepts? How does this humbling realization affect your reliance on logic in matters of faith?

The Necessity of Mental Existence for Universal Propositions
Sanusi argues that universal affirmative propositions prove mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how universal affirmative propositions require mental existence for their truth, using the example of the geometric property of triangles.

Core Idea
Universal truths about things that don't physically exist prove mental existence.

Sanusi presents a second argument for mental existence, this time focusing on universal affirmative propositions like 'every triangle has angles equal to two right angles'. Such judgments apply to all triangles, including those that are not physically present. This lesson unpacks the logical necessity of mental existence for the universality of such propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that this argument is presented as 'And it may be said' indicating a new point.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by asserting that without mental existence, universal affirmative propositions would be invalid. Ask yourself: what is the logical basis for this claim?
3
Analyze the triangle example
Consider the statement 'Every triangle has angles equal to two right angles'. The passage argues that this judgment covers not only external triangles but also conceived ones. Reflect on why conceived triangles must exist in the mind for the statement to be true universally.
The key is that the subject 'triangle' applies to all triangles in reality, including those not physically instantiated.
4
Distinguish from the previous argument
The commentary notes that the earlier argument concerned 'taking a real proposition' while this one concerns the 'universality' of such propositions. Notice how this nuance strengthens the overall case for mental existence.
This distinction shows the depth of the kalam tradition in analyzing logical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال لولا الوجود الذهني لبطلت الحقيقة الموجبة الكلية كقولك كل مثلث تساوي زواياه قائمتين إذ ليس الحكم فيها مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجية بل يتناول ما عداها الأفراد التي يصدق عليها الموضوع في نفس الأمر فلو لم يكن لما عداها وجود في الذهن لم يصدق الحكم الإيجابي فيها قوله وقد يقال إلخ خلاصة السابق أنه لولا الوجود الذهني لم يمكن أخذ القضية الموجبة الحقيقية التي حكم فيها على الأفراد المعقولة جزئية كانت أو كلية وخلاصة هذا الوجه أنه لولا الوجود الذهني لزم بطلان كلية القضية الحقيقية أي بطلان صدق القضية الحقيقية التي حكم فيها على الأفراد الخارجية والمعقولة كالمثال المذكور لأن صدقها كلية يستدعي الحكم على الأفراد المعقولة أيضا وصدقه عليها على تقدير عدم الوجود الذهني محال لعدم وجود الموضوع
And it may be said: Without mental existence, the universal affirmative real proposition would be invalid, such as your saying 'Every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles', since the judgment in it is not confined to external individuals but includes besides them the individuals that the subject applies to in reality. So if there were no existence in the mind for what is besides them, the affirmative judgment in it would not be true. His statement: 'And it may be said, etc.': The summary of the previous is that without mental existence it would not be possible to take the real affirmative proposition in which judgment is made on conceived individuals, whether particular or universal. And the summary of this point is that without mental existence the universality of the real proposition would be invalid, i.e., the truth of the real proposition in which judgment is made on external and conceived individuals would be invalid, like the mentioned example, because its truth as universal requires judgment on conceived individuals as well, and its truth upon them on the assumption of absence of mental existence is impossible due to the non-existence of the subject.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents another argument for mental existence, focusing on universal affirmative propositions (al-qadiyya al-mujiba al-kulliyya). The argument is that without mental existence, such propositions would be invalid. The example given is the geometric truth 'every triangle has angles equal to two right angles'. This judgment is not limited to externally existing triangles; it applies to all triangles that the subject applies to in reality, including those that are merely conceived. If triangles that are not external did not have existence in the mind, the universal judgment could not be true because it would fail to apply to those conceived individuals. The commentary clarifies the distinction between two aspects: the previous argument dealt with the possibility of taking a real proposition (akhdh al-qadiyya al-haqiqiyya), while this argument deals with the universality (kulliyya) of the real proposition. Without mental existence, the universality would collapse because the judgment must cover all instances, including mental ones. The truth of a universal proposition requires that every instance of the subject, whether external or conceived, satisfy the predicate. Since some instances are only conceived, they must have mental existence for the proposition to be true.

Correction: They must cover all possible instances, which requires mental existence for unactualized ones.

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative propositions require mental existence because they must cover all instances, including those that exist only in the mind.

Interpreting 'The Impossible is Non-Existent'
Al-Jurjani interprets the proposition 'the impossible is non-existent' to prove mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani reinterprets the proposition 'the impossible is non-existent' to demonstrate the necessity of mental existence for universal affirmative judgments.

Core Idea
Mental existence is needed for the truth of universal statements about impossibles.

Al-Jurjani tackles a subtle logical problem: how can we truthfully say 'the impossible is non-existent' if there is no impossible thing in the external world? His answer reveals a crucial role for mental existence, where the impossible has conceptual individuals. This passage unpacks that argument and defends it against an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the distinction between external and real (ḥaqīqiyya) propositions.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage first clarifies what the proposition does NOT mean (an external impossible thing) and then what it DOES mean (conceptual individuals in the mind).
3
Understand the role of mental existence
Note the crucial claim: without conceptual individuals in the mind, the affirmative judgment would not apply. This shows mental existence is necessary.
This is the core argument for mental existence.
4
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection that the concept of non-existence is negative, and see how Al-Jurjani responds with the triangle example to reinforce his point.
The triangle example illustrates a universal affirmative proposition that applies to mental as well as external individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممتنع معدوم فلا نريد به أن للممتنع أي ما يصدق عليه الممتنع ( في الخارج معدوم فيه قطعاً ) أي لا نريد ذلك قطعاً، إذ ليس في الخارج ما يصدق عليه الممتنع أصلاً (بل) نريد به أن الأفراد المعقولة للممتنع أي يصدق عليها الممتنع في العقل ( من الأفراد المعقولة للمعدوم ) أي يصدق عليها في العقل بحسب نفس الأمر أنها معدومة في الخارج، فلو لم يكن للممتنع أفراد معقولة موجودة في العقل لم يصدق عليها الحكم الإيجابي، فلذلك قال : ( وهذا بالحقيقة عائد إلى الأول ) والحاصل : أن قولنا : الممتنع معدوم في الخارج قضية صادقة وليست خارجية بل حقيقية مفسرة بما ذكرناه لا بما اشتهر من أن الحكم فيها على الأفراد الخارجية فقط، إما محققة أو مقدرة فلولا أن يكون للممتنع أفراد موجودة في الذهن لم يصدق هذا الحكم الإيجابي في هذه القضية الحقيقية، ويرد عليه أن مفهوم المعدوم أمر سلبي، وقد يقال : لولا الوجود الذهني لبطلت الحقيقة الموجبة الكلية كقولك : كل مثلث تساوي زواياه قائمتين، إذ ليس الحكم فيها مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجية بل يتناول ما عداها الأفراد التي يصدق عليها الموضوع في نفس الأمر ، فلو لم يكن لما عداها
The impossible is non-existent, but we do not mean by that that the impossible, i.e., that to which 'impossible' applies (is non-existent in the external world absolutely) – i.e., we do not mean that absolutely, since there is nothing in the external world to which 'impossible' applies at all. (Rather) we mean by it that the conceptual individuals of the impossible, i.e., that to which 'impossible' applies in the mind (are among the conceptual individuals of the non-existent) – i.e., it applies to them in the mind, according to the actual state of affairs, that they are non-existent in the external world. So if there were no conceptual individuals of the impossible existing in the mind, the affirmative judgment would not apply to them. Therefore he said: (And this in reality returns to the first). The conclusion: that our statement 'the impossible is non-existent in the external world' is a true proposition, not an external one but a real one (ḥaqīqiyya), interpreted as we mentioned, not as is commonly held that the judgment in it is only about external individuals, whether actual or assumed. So if the impossible did not have individuals existing in the mind, this affirmative judgment in this real proposition would not be true. And it is objected that the concept of non-existent is a negative thing. And it may be said: were it not for mental existence, the universal affirmative real proposition would be invalid, like your saying: 'Every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles', since the judgment in it is not confined to external individuals but includes beyond them the individuals to which the subject applies in the actual state of affairs. So if there were not for beyond them [mental individuals]...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the proposition 'the impossible is non-existent' to argue for mental existence. The commentator begins by clarifying the meaning of this proposition: it is not a claim that there is an actual impossible thing in the external world, which would be false. Rather, it is a judgment about the conceptual individuals of the impossible in the mind: they are judged to be non-existent in the external world. Thus, the proposition is a 'real universal' (ḥaqīqiyya), applying to both actual and conceivable individuals. The commentator then states that if there were no mental existence for these conceptual individuals, the affirmative judgment could not be true. He addresses an objection that the concept of non-existence is negative and does not require a subject, and he provides an alternative example: the universal proposition 'every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles' applies to all triangles, including those only conceived in the mind. Hence, mental existence is necessary for the truth of such universal affirmative judgments.

Correction: The proposition is not about an external impossible thing; it is about conceptual individuals of the impossible in the mind, judged to be non-existent externally. Its truth depends on mental existence.
