The Rational Proof Against Distinction of Non-Existents
Presents the second opinion that denies any distinction among non-existents with a formal logical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that denies any distinction among non-existents and the logical syllogism supporting it.

Core Idea
Non-existents cannot be distinct because distinction requires existence.

The first opinion held that non-existents are distinct due to essential differences. The second opinion, presented here, argues that non-existents are pure negation with no reference, and therefore cannot be distinct. The argument is built on a clear syllogism that the commentator elaborates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the flow from the conditional remark to the main proof.
The passage includes both the matn and the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
First, there is a conditional (if non-existents were distinct, their implications would differ) which is not fully expanded. Second, the main argument using pure negation and the positive attribute of distinction.
The conditional serves as a prelude to the main syllogism.
3
Understand the syllogism
The commentator formalizes the proof: minor premise (non-existents have no existence), major premise (everything distinct exists in some manner), conclusion (no non-existent is distinct). Recognize why each premise is considered self-evident.
The minor premise is self-evident because non-existence contradicts subsistence; the major premise is justified because distinction is a positive attribute.
4
Reflect on the self-evident nature
Note that the premises are claimed to be badīhiyya (self-evident) in kalam reasoning, meaning they do not require further proof.
This addresses the objection of circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فله وجود وعدم غير الضد لا يصحح وجود الضد الآخر ( ولولا التمايز) والتعدد اللازم منه بين العدمات لم تختلف مقتضياتها ولا أحكامها من كون بعضها ملزوماً لآخر، أو لازماً له أو مساوياً أو مبايناً إلى غير ذلك مما لا كثرة ( ومنهم من نفاه لأن المعدومات) والعدمات (نفي صرف لا إشارة إليها أصلا، وكل ما هو متميز إما في الذهن وإما في الخارج لأن التميز صفة ،ثبوتية لا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها في الجملة، وما يكون ثابتاً كذلك لم يكن معدوماً، فلا شيء من المعدوم بمتميز أصلاً . قوله : ( لأن المعدومات إلخ) أي من حيث إنها معدومات نفي صرف أي خالص لا إشارة إليها، إذ الإشارة تقتضي الهوية عند العقل المنافية لكونها معدومة وهذه المقدمة بديهية، فلا يرد أن قولكم نفي صرف لا إشارة إليها بمنزلة أنه لا تميز لها، فيكون مصادرة وصورة الاستدلال أن المعدومات من حيث إنها معدومة لا وجود لها أصلاً، وكل ما هو متميز موجود في الجملة أما الصغرى فبديهية لأن العدم ينافي الثبوت، وأما الكبرى فلان كل متميز متصف بالصفة الثبوتية التميز، وكل متصف بالصفة الثبوتية موجود في الجملة.
For it there is existence and the non-existence of other than the opposite does not make the existence of the other opposite possible. And if not for the differentiation and the multiplicity necessary thereby among the non-existences, their implications and judgments would not differ, such as some being a necessary condition (malzūm) for another, or a necessary consequence (lāzim) of it, or equal, or opposite, to other than that which has no multiplicity. And some denied it because non-existents and non-existences are pure negation, having no reference at all. And everything that is distinct is either in the mind or in the external, because distinctness (tamayyuz) is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya); it is necessary that the thing so described exists in some manner, and what is established in such a way is not non-existent. Therefore, no non-existent is distinct at all. His saying: 'Because non-existents etc.' i.e., in terms of their being non-existent, they are pure negation, i.e., absolute, having no reference to them, because reference requires identity (huwiyya) in the intellect which is incompatible with their being non-existent. This premise is self-evident, so it cannot be objected that your statement 'pure negation, no reference to them' is tantamount to saying they have no distinction, thus it would be circular. The form of the inference is: non-existents in terms of their being non-existent have no existence at all, and everything that is distinct exists in some manner. As for the minor premise, it is self-evident because non-existence contradicts subsistence (thubūt). As for the major premise, it is because everything distinct is characterized by the positive attribute of distinctness, and everything characterized by a positive attribute exists in some manner.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion, which denies that non-existents are distinct. The proof is introduced: if non-existents were distinct, their necessary implications and judgments would differ (e.g., one being a cause, another an effect), but this difference could be due to their relations to privations, not to themselves. Then the outright denial is stated: non-existents and non-existences are pure negation with no reference; distinction requires a positive attribute, which necessitates existence. Therefore, no non-existent can be distinct. The commentator explains that the phrase 'pure negation' means there is no mental reference to them because reference requires identity, which contradicts non-existence. He then formalizes the argument into a syllogism: (1) Non-existents as such have no existence. (2) Everything distinct exists in some manner. The minor premise is self-evident; the major premise is because distinctness is a positive attribute, and anything with a positive attribute exists. Thus, the conclusion is that non-existents cannot be distinct.

Key Terms
المعدومات
العدمات
نفي صرف
الإشارة
الصفة الثبوتية
التميز
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that 'non-existents are pure negation' is circular because it already assumes they have no distinction.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it is a valid syllogism. The minor premise (non-existents have no existence) is self-evident, and the major premise (everything distinct exists) is justified by the nature of distinction as a positive attribute. The commentator explicitly shows that it is not a petition principle.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise in the syllogism used to deny distinction of non-existents?
A) Non-existents are pure negation.
B) Everything that is distinct exists in some manner.
C) Non-existence contradicts subsistence.
D) Reference requires identity.

Key Takeaway
The second opinion denies distinction of non-existents by arguing that non-existents are pure negation with no reference, and distinction requires existence, so no non-existent can be distinct.

?

The Linguistic Meaning of Condition in the Condition Argument
Clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'condition' and resolves objections about the condition argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'condition' in the condition argument is interpreted linguistically to avoid a logical contradiction regarding the complete cause.

Core Idea
The condition argument works only if 'condition' means linguistic dependence, not a part of the cause.

The passage tackles a critical objection: if condition is part of the complete cause, then any part's non-existence would cause the effect to cease, contradicting the proof that only condition's non-existence does. The commentator explains that 'condition' here is used in its linguistic sense (that on which something depends in general), not the technical sense of a part of the complete cause. This resolution saves the argument and clarifies the meaning of condition in kalam debates.

Guided Path
1
Overview of the Objection
Read the passage to understand the main objection: if condition is a part of the complete cause, then non-existence of any part would cause the effect to cease, contradicting the proof.
The objection arises from a technical definition of condition.
2
The Two Interpretations
Identify the two possible meanings of 'condition': linguistic (general dependence) and technical (part of complete cause). The commentator argues that the proof uses the linguistic meaning.
This distinction is key to resolving the objection.
3
How the Resolution Works
See how the linguistic interpretation makes the proof valid: only the non-existence of the condition (in the linguistic sense) along with the rest of the cause leads to the effect's non-existence, while non-existence of other parts does not.
4
Reflection on Significance
Consider why this clarification matters for kalam debates: precise definition of terms prevents logical fallacies.
This shows the importance of terminological precision in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اقتصر على قوله فله وجود ولم يتعرض للوجود الذهني، ونفيه عن المعدوم الذي كلامنا فيه لكان أنسب بقوله : ( والحق فيه) أي في الخلاف في تمايز ثابتا المصطلح حتى يرد أن غير الشرط من أجزاء العلة التامة يوجب عدم المشروط، ولو أريد بقوله : لا يوجب عدم المشروط من حيث إنه مشروط يصح حمله على المعنى الاصطلاحي، فإن يوجب لا عدم سائر أجزاء العلة إنما يوجب عدمه من حيث إنه معلول لا من حيث أنه مشروط . قوله : (لم تختلف مقتضياتها فيه أن اللازم منه تمايز العدمات مطلقاً، والمطلوب تمايزها من حيث إنها معدومة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف بسبب إضافتها إلى ملكاتها ؟ . قوله : ( لأن المعدومات إلخ) قد مضى شرحه . قوله : ( ونفيه عن المعدوم التعرض لنفي الوجود الذهني في الصغرى مستفاد من التعرض له في الكبرى ليتكرر في الأوسط بأن يقال : المعدومات لا وجود لها في الذهن، والخارج وكل ما هو متميز موجود إما في الذهن أو في الخارج. قوله : ( أنسب ) لئلا يكون الاستدلال المذكور مشعراً بالتفصيل المذكور في قوله والحق . قوله : (فإن عدم غير الشرط لا يوجب عدم المشروط فإن قلت : عدم أي جزء كان من العلة التامة يوجب عدم المعلول، فما معنی هذا الكلام؟ قلت : المراد بالشرط ها هنا معناه اللغوي، وهو ما يتوقف عليه الشيء في الجملة، فيصح الكلام وإن حمل على معناه الاصطلاحي، فنقول مراده من قوله : فإن عدم الشرط يوجب عدم المشروط أن عدمه مع وجود باقي العلة التامة يوجبه، فحينئذ يكون معنى قوله : فإن عدم غير الشرط لا يوجب إلخ، أنه لا يوجب عدمه مع وجود أجزاء العلة التامة التي فرض وجودها في الصورة الأولى، وهذا حكم صحيح لأن تلك الأجزاء إذا فرض تحققها في الصورة الثانية أيضاً، كان غير الشرط الذي فرض عدمه فيها أجنبياً، ليس بجزء
And if he had only said 'for there is existence' and not mentioned mental existence, and its denial for the non-existent that is the subject of our discussion, it would be more appropriate to his saying: 'And the truth (al-ḥaqq) in it' i.e., in the dispute about differentiation, is established in the technical terminology such that it could be objected that other than the condition among the parts of the complete cause necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned. And if what is meant by his saying 'does not necessitate the non-existence of the conditioned' is in terms of it being conditioned, it is valid to interpret it according to the technical meaning, for indeed the non-existence of the other parts of the cause necessitates its non-existence only in terms of it being an effect, not in terms of it being conditioned. His saying: 'their implications would not differ' — in it (it is argued) that what necessarily follows is the differentiation of non-existences absolutely, but what is required is their differentiation in terms of being non-existent; so why is it not possible that this difference is due to their addition to their privations? His saying: 'Because non-existents etc.' its explanation has already passed. His saying: 'and its denial for the non-existent' — the mention of denial of mental existence in the minor premise is derived from mentioning it in the major premise so that it is repeated in the middle term, by saying: non-existents have no existence in the mind or the external, and everything that is distinct exists either in the mind or the external. His saying: 'more appropriate' so that the aforementioned inference does not hint at the detail mentioned in his saying 'and the truth'. His saying: 'for the non-existence of other than the condition does not necessitate the non-existence of the conditioned' — if you say: the non-existence of any part whatsoever of the complete cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect, so what is the meaning of this statement? I say: the intended meaning of 'condition' here is its linguistic meaning, which is that on which something depends in some way. Thus the statement is valid, even if interpreted according to its technical meaning. For we say: his intended meaning from his saying 'the non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned' is that its non-existence along with the existence of the rest of the complete cause necessitates it. Therefore, the meaning of his saying 'the non-existence of other than the condition does not necessitate' etc. is that it does not necessitate its non-existence along with the existence of the parts of the complete cause that were assumed to exist in the first scenario. This is a correct judgment because those parts, if they are assumed to be realized in the second scenario as well, then the non-condition whose non-existence was assumed in it is extraneous, not a part.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage addresses interpretative issues in the first opinion's proof. The commentator first notes that it would have been better if the argument had not mentioned mental existence, to avoid confusion. He discusses the meaning of 'condition' (shart): if taken technically (as part of the complete cause), then the non-existence of any part of the complete cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect, which would contradict the proof's claim that only the condition's non-existence does so. The resolution is that 'condition' here is used in the linguistic sense (what something depends on in general), not the technical sense. Alternatively, if the technical sense is intended, the proof can be reinterpreted: the non-existence of the condition along with the existence of the other parts necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned, whereas the non-existence of a non-condition (an extraneous part) does not. The commentator also addresses the objection that the difference in implications among non-existences could be due to their relations to privations, not to their own natures. He then explains that the denial of mental existence in the minor premise is clarified by the logical structure. Finally, he justifies the linguistic interpretation of 'condition' to maintain the proof's validity.

Key Terms
الشرط (condition)
العلة التامة (complete cause)
المعنى اللغوي (linguistic meaning)
المعنى الاصطلاحي (technical meaning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'condition' in the proof refers to the technical term of a part of the complete cause, leading to an apparent contradiction.
Correction: It actually refers to the linguistic meaning of general dependence, which allows the proof to hold that only the non-existence of this condition (not other parts) necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned.

Checkpoint

B) It is interpreted linguistically as that on which something depends in general.
C) It is interpreted as the opposite of the effect.
D) It is considered irrelevant to the argument.

Key Takeaway
The resolution of the condition argument relies on understanding 'condition' in its broad linguistic sense, not as a technical part of the cause.

The Proof That Non-Existents Are Not Distinct
Presents the proof that non-existents cannot be distinct because they are pure negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that non-existents cannot be distinct from each other, based on their being pure negations.

Core Idea
Pure negation cannot be distinct — distinctness requires existence.

This short but powerful passage presents a key argument in the debate about the nature of non-existents (ma'dumat). The author cites a group of scholars who deny that non-existents can be distinct from one another. Their reasoning is simple and logical: since non-existents are pure negations with no reference in reality or the mind, and since being distinct is a positive attribute that requires some form of existence, it follows that no non-existent can be distinct. As you work through this argument, pay attention to how the premises build a rigorous syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud. Notice the key terms: معدوم, عدم, نفي صرف, تميز, صفة ثبوتية. Then read the translation.
The text is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has a minor premise: non-existents are pure negation with no reference. And a major premise: everything distinct has existence. Find these in the text.
The major premise includes the explanation that distinctness is a positive attribute requiring existence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Combine the premises to reach the conclusion: no non-existent is distinct. Ask yourself why this conclusion follows necessarily.
If distinctness needs existence, and non-existents lack existence, they cannot be distinct.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider how this argument relates to the debate about the attributes of non-existents. The commentary will help clarify the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من نفاه لأن المعدومات والعدمات نفي صرف لا إشارة إليها أصلا، وكل ما هو متميز إما في الذهن وإما في الخارج لأن التميز صفة ثبوتية لا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها في الجملة، وما يكون ثابتا كذلك لم يكن معدوما، فلا شيء من المعدوم بمتميز أصلا.
And some of them denied it because non-existents and non-existents are pure negation, with no reference to them at all, and everything that is distinct is either in the mind or in external reality, because distinction is a positive attribute, and it is necessary that the one described by it be existent in some way, and whatever is existent in such a way is not non-existent, so no non-existent is distinct at all.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the distinctness of non-existents (ma'dumat). The author states that some scholars deny the distinctness of non-existents. Their argument: non-existents are pure negation (nafy sirf) with no reference (ishara) to them at all. Then they establish a premise: everything that is distinct is either in the mind or in external reality, because distinction (tamayyuz) is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya), and the thing described by such an attribute must be existent in some way (thabit). Whatever is existent in this way is not non-existent (ma'dum). Therefore, no non-existent can be distinct. The argument works as a syllogism: the minor premise—non-existents have no existence; the major premise—everything distinct has existence in some way. Since non-existents lack existence, they cannot possess the attribute of distinctness.

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum)
عدم (adam)
نفي صرف (nafy sirf)
تميز (tamayyuz)
صفة ثبوتية (sifa thubutiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'pure negation' means absolute nothingness without any conceptual distinction, but the argument is about lack of external or mental existence for distinction.
Correction: In this context, 'pure negation' means that non-existents have no existence at all (neither in the mind nor externally), and therefore cannot have positive attributes like distinctness.

Checkpoint

C) Because distinction is only possible in the mind.
D) Because non-existents have existence in the mind only.

Key Takeaway
Distinctness is a positive attribute that requires existence, so non-existents, being pure negations, cannot be distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you imagine yourself as a better Muslim in the future—praying more, giving charity—that future self does not exist. Are you using its non-existence as an excuse to delay action? What concrete step can you take today to turn that 'non-existent distinctness' into real existence through an act of worship?

The Correct View: Non-Existents Are Distinct Only in the Mind
States the correct position that non-existents are distinct only in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinctness of non-existents is confined to the mind and how this relates to the debate on mental existence.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct only in the mind, not in external reality.

This lesson presents the correct position regarding the distinctness of non-existent entities (ma'dumat). The author explains that this issue is a branch of the larger debate about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). Through a concise argument, he shows that distinctions among non-existents can only occur in the mind, because external reality cannot contain what does not exist. A conditional follow-up reinforces the logical necessity of this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a conditional statement towards the end.
2
Identify the core claim
The author states that the correct view is that the distinctness of non-existents is a branch of the disagreement about mental existence. Understand why he connects these two issues.
3
Follow the reasoning
Trace the argument: why is there no distinctness among non-existents except in the mind? Because external non-existents have no subsistence (thubut) in external reality, so they cannot be described there.
Note the phrase 'bi-hasab nafs al-amr' (according to the actual state of affairs).
4
Analyze the conditional
Examine the conditional: if distinctness in the mind implied mental existence, then no non-existent would ever be conceived. Explain why this would be a problem.
The conditional shows that the correct view does not require actual mental existence for non-existents.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Summarize the lesson: non-existents are distinct in the mind as concepts, not as existents. This maintains the distinction between mental and external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق فيه أي في الخلاف في تمايز المعدوم أنه فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني و ذلك لأنه لا تمايز بين المعدومات إلا في العقل فإن تلك الأحكام إنما تتصف بها المعدومات بحسب نفس الأمر في العقل لا في الخارج، إذ لا ثبوت للمعدوم الخارجي في الخارج، حتى يمكن اتصافه فيه بشيء فلا تمايز بينها إلا في العقل فإن كان ذلك التمايز الحاصل لها في العقل لوجود لها في الذهن لم يتصور معدوم مطلقا بل كل ما يتصور من المعدومات له وجود ذهني.
The truth of the matter—concerning the disagreement about the distinctness of the non-existent—is that it is a branch of the disagreement about mental existence. And that is because there is no distinctness among non-existents except in the mind. For those attributes are only attributed to non-existents according to the actual state of affairs in the mind, not in external reality, since there is no subsistence for an external non-existent in external reality, such that it could be described by something therein. So there is no distinctness among them except in the mind. If that distinctness which occurs for them in the mind is because they have existence in the mind, then no non-existent is conceived at all; rather, every non-existent that is conceived has mental existence.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the correct view (al-haqq) concerning the distinctness of non-existents. The author states that this issue is a branch of the disagreement about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The reason: there is no distinctness among non-existents except in the mind (al-aql). The attributes that distinguish non-existents (such as being a condition or consequence) are only attributed to them according to the actual state of affairs (bi-hasab nafs al-amr) in the mind, not in external reality. This is because an external non-existent has no subsistence (thubut) in external reality, so it cannot be described by anything there. Therefore, distinctness among non-existents is only in the mind. Then the author adds a conditional: if that distinctness that occurs for them in the mind is because they have existence in the mind, then no non-existent at all would be conceived; rather, every non-existent that is conceived would have mental existence. That is, if the only way to be distinct is through mental existence, then what is conceived as a non-existent would actually be existent in the mind, contradicting its being non-existent. So the correct view implies that non-existents are distinct in the mind without needing actual mental existence—they are considered by the mind as distinct concepts.

Key Terms
الحق (al-haqq)
تمايز (tamayyuz)
معدوم (ma'dum)
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni)
عقل (aql)
ثبوت (thubut)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the correct view means non-existents have real distinctness in the mind, implying they have actual mental existence.
Correction: The text emphasizes that the distinctness is only according to the mind's consideration (bi-hasab nafs al-amr), not that non-existents have actual mental existence. The conditional shows that if it implied mental existence, it would negate the concept of a non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view, where does the distinctness among non-existents occur?
A) In external reality.
B) In the mind only.
C) Neither in the mind nor in external reality.
D) Both in the mind and external reality.

Key Takeaway
Non-existents are distinct only in the mind as mental concepts, without requiring actual mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect on how your mental distinctions (hopes, fears) affect your actions. Are you treating non-existents as if they have external subsistence? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of your mind's creations? Will you consciously remind yourself that these images are only mental considerations?
?
Consider a mistake you made in the past that no longer exists. You might still carry its burden in your mind. How does this lesson's teaching that non-existents are distinct only in the mind, not in external reality, challenge you to let go of that which has no external subsistence? Will you release it today by acknowledging that its distinctness is merely a mental concept?
?
The text says that if distinctness required mental existence, then nothing would be truly non-existent. Think about something you desire but do not have. In your mind, you give it distinct attributes. Does your intense focus on it blur the line between mental existence and external reality? Are you living more in your mind than in reality? What concrete step can you take to align with the truth that non-existents are only mental considerations, not external realities?

The Importance of the Non-Existent's Thingness
Al-Razi links the issue to existence-essence addition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question of whether the non-existent is a thing is considered a foundational issue in kalam and how it relates to the doctrine that existence is additional to essence.

Core Idea
The thingness of the non-existent is the pivot for many theological rulings.

This lesson introduces a major kalam debate: Is a non-existent entity considered a 'thing'? The author explains that this issue is one of the 'mothers' of kalam, as many other issues branch from it, such as whether essences are created. Imam al-Razi ties this question directly to the relationship between existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the key claims.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ummahat al-masa'il'.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main points: (1) this issue is one of the mother issues of kalam, (2) it branches from the doctrine of the addition of existence to essence.
3
Reflect on the branching
Ask yourself: if existence and essence are identical, can the question of the non-existent's thingness even be posed? The passage says no.
This shows how fundamental the existence-essence distinction is.

المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا
في أن المعدوم شيء أم لا وإنها ) أي هذه المسألة ( من أمهات المسائل) الكلامية إذ يتفرع عليها أحكام كثيرة من جملتها أن الماهيات غير مجعولة، وسيرد عليك بعضها عن قريب قال الإمام الرازي: المسألة متفرعة على القول بزيادة الوجود على الماهية فإن القائل باتحادهما لا يمكنه القول بها، قيل ويمكن أن يعكس الحكم
في
بعدم التمايز لعدم الثبوت أصلاً، لأنه إذا كان التمايز باعتبار كونها موجودة في الذهن لم يكن تمايزها من حيث إنها معدومات والكلام فيه ولأن الكلام في تمايز المعدومات مطلقاً لا تمايز المعدومات الخارجية فتدبر، فإن كل ذلك منشؤه عدم التدبر لمحل النزاع . قول : ( إن المعدوم شيء أم (لا الجزء الأول لكونه مهملة في حكم الجزئية، والجزء الثاني
سالبة كلية .
قوله : ( من جملتها أن الماهيات غير مجعولة) إن أريد بالمسألة المردد بين الإيجاب والسلب، فتقديره أو مجعولة وإن أريد الجزء الأول منها فلا حاجة إلى التقدير، ثم تفرع مسألة الجعل على تلك المسألة . أما على ما ذكره المصنف في آخرها من أن عاقلاً لم يقل بأن الماهية الممكنة مستغنية في تقررها وثبوتها في الخارج عن الفاعل إلا ما ينسب إلى المعتزلة من أن المعدومات الممكنة ذوات متقررة ثابتة في أنفسها من غير تأثير للفاعل فيها، وإنما تأثيره في الاتصاف بالوجود وأما على ما هو التحقيق في هذه المسألة من أن الماهيات أنفسها أثر الفاعل أو اتصافها بالوجود، ولا شك في تفرعها على شيئية المعدوم ،وعدمه وأما على ما ذكره الشارح من أن معناها أن الماهية في كونها ماهية غير مجعولة، إذ لا يمكن توسط الجعل بين الشيء ونفسه لعدم التغاير إنما المجعول اتصافها بالوجود على سيجيء فلا شك أن عدم الجعل بهذا المعنى لا يتوقف على ثبوت الماهيات حال العدم كما لا يخفى . قول : ( فإن القائل إلخ) أي القائل باتحادهما في الصدق لا يمكنه القول بتلك المسألة المرددة إذ يصير المعنى أن المعدومات أي الماهية المرتفعة بالمرة موصوفة بالثبوت في الخارج أم لا . قوله : قيل ويمكن أن يعكس إلخ لعله اعتراض على ما ذكره الإمام بأن استلزام أحد
ما
قوله : ( من جملتها أن الماهية غير مجعولة تفرع هذه المسألة على شيئية المعدوم بناء على ما ذكره المصنف من الاستدلال عليها وأما على تحقيق الشارح الذي أورده فيما سيأتي فمدارها على عدم تصور توسط الجعل بين الماهية ونفسها، ولا دخل لشيئية المعدوم في ذلك . قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي هذه المسألة متفرعة إلخ) يعني المسألة الأولى وهي الجزء الأول المنفصلة، فإن ما ذكره في الحقيقة مسألتان ثم التفرع في كلام الإمام بمعنى التوقف وفي كلام القائل بالعكس بمعنى اللزوم وهذا أظهر في معنى التفرع.
The First Observatory - The Sixth Section: Is the non-existent a thing or not? Concerning whether the non-existent is a thing or not, and it (i.e., this issue) is one of the mother issues (ummahat al-masa'il) of kalam, for many rulings branch from it, among them that essences (al-mahiyat) are not made (ghayr maj'ula). Some of them will come to you soon. Imam al-Razi said: The issue branches from the doctrine of the addition of existence to essence, for the one who affirms their identity cannot affirm it. It was said: And it is possible to reverse the ruling. Concerning the negation of distinction due to lack of positivity at all, because if distinction is by virtue of being existent in the mind, then their distinction is not insofar as they are non-existents, and the discussion is about it. And because the discussion is about the distinction of non-existents absolutely, not the distinction of external non-existents. So reflect, for all that stems from lack of reflection on the locus of dispute. His statement: (Is the non-existent a thing or not) the first part is a particular negative because it is a hypothetical proposition in the ruling of particularity, and the second part is a universal negative. His statement: (Among them that essences are not made) If by the issue is meant the disjunction between affirmation and negation, then its estimation is 'or made', and if the first part is meant, then there is no need for estimation. Then the issue of making (al-ja'l) branches from that issue. As for what the author mentioned at its end: that no rational person said that the possible essence is independent in its determination and establishment in the external from the agent, except what is attributed to the Mu'tazila: that possible non-existents are self-determined essences established in themselves without any effect of the agent in them, and His effect is only in the attribute of existence. And as for what is the verification in this issue: that the essences themselves are the effect of the agent, or their attribute of existence, and there is no doubt about its branching from the thingness of the non-existent and its negation. And as for what the commentator mentioned: that its meaning is that the essence as an essence is not made, for no making can mediate between a thing and itself due to lack of differentiation; rather, what is made is its attribute of existence, as will come. So there is no doubt that the negation of making in this sense does not depend on the establishment of essences in the state of non-existence, as is not hidden. His statement: (For the one who affirms, etc.) i.e., the one who affirms their identity in truth cannot affirm that disjunctive issue, for then the meaning becomes: Are non-existents, i.e., the essence elevated entirely, described by positivity in the external or not? His statement: 'It was said: And it is possible to reverse, etc.' Perhaps it is an objection to what the Imam mentioned, that the implication of one... His statement: (Among them that the essence is not made) The branching of this issue on the thingness of the non-existent is based on what the author mentioned of the proof for it. But according to the verification of the commentator which he will present later, its pivot is on the inconceivability of mediating making between the essence and itself, and the thingness of the non-existent has no role in that. His statement: (Imam al-Razi said: This issue branches, etc.) means the first issue, which is the first disjunctive part. For what he mentioned is in reality two issues. Then the branching in the Imam's speech is in the sense of dependence (tawaqquf), and in the speech of the one who says the reverse is in the sense of implication (luzum), and this is more apparent in the meaning of branching.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth section, which addresses the question: Is the non-existent (al-ma'dum) something (shay') or not? The author states that this is one of the major issues of kalam because many rulings depend on it, including the problem of whether essences (al-mahiyat) are created or not. The commentary explains that Imam al-Razi linked this issue to the doctrine that existence is additional to essence; if one holds that existence and essence are identical, then the question of whether the non-existent is a thing cannot be meaningfully asked. The passage also discusses a possible reversal of this relationship. It then delves into the meaning of the proposition 'Is the non-existent a thing or not?' analyzing its logical form as a particular negative and a universal negative. The commentary notes that the issue of 'making' (ja'l, i.e., creation) branches from whether the non-existent is a thing. Different positions are examined: the Mu'tazila hold that non-existent possible things are self-determined essences, with the agent only affecting existence; others hold that essences themselves are the effect of the agent. The commentator (al-Jurjani) offers his own verification: that the essence as essence is not made because making cannot mediate between a thing and itself; rather, what is made is the attribute of existence. This understanding does not depend on the thingness of the non-existent. The passage concludes by clarifying that Imam al-Razi's statement actually involves two issues, and the relationship between them is one of dependence (tawaqquf) or implication (luzum).

Key Terms
معدوم
شيء
ماهية
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the question 'Is the non-existent a thing?' is a simple yes/no question with no further implications.
Correction: In fact, it is a foundational issue that affects many other theological rulings, and its answer depends on the prior doctrine of the relationship between existence and essence.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, what does the issue of the non-existent's thingness branch from?
A) The doctrine that existence and essence are identical
B) The doctrine that existence is additional to essence
C) The doctrine that essences are created
D) The doctrine that non-existents are things

Key Takeaway
The question of the non-existent's thingness is a central kalam issue that branches from the existence-essence addition doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you have ever done or will do was known to Allah as a 'thing' before you did it, how does that change the way you approach your choices today? Will you pause before your next action because it is already a 'thing' in His knowledge?
?
You often speak of 'creating' opportunities or 'making' your own future. In light of this lesson, who truly brings things into existence? Are you attributing any power of creation to yourself that belongs only to Allah? What is one area of your life where you need to repent of this?
?
Al-Razi connects this issue to whether your essence is distinct from your existence. Your essence is not made by you—it is given. How does that humble your pride? Can you name one character trait you thought you built yourself, but now realize is a gift from Allah?

Analyzing the Proposition Logically
Logical analysis of the proposition into two judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proposition 'Is the non-existent a thing?' and why it is parsed as a particular negative and a universal negative.

Core Idea
The proposition is not simple: it hides two logical judgments.

The author now examines the exact wording of the question. He breaks it into two parts: the first part is a particular negative (not all non-existents are things?), and the second is a universal negative (no non-existent is a thing?). This logical clarification is essential to avoid confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the terms 'particular negative' and 'universal negative'.
The passage is short but packed with logical terminology.
2
Identify the two parts
The proposition 'Is the non-existent a thing or not?' is analyzed as having two parts: the first is a particular negative (i.e., some non-existents are not things), the second is a universal negative (no non-existent is a thing).
3
Understand the logical terms
A 'particular negative' in logic means 'Some S are not P'; a 'universal negative' means 'No S is P'. The author is clarifying which logical form each part takes.
This shows the importance of precise logical analysis in kalam.

How the Thingness of the Non-Existent Affects Creation
Discussion of creation (ja'l) in relation to the thingness of the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate on the thingness of the non-existent influences the doctrine of creation (ja'l) and the Mu'tazila position of self-determined essences.

Core Idea
Whether essences are created depends on whether they have reality before existence.

This lesson explores the consequences of the thingness debate. The author explains that if non-existents are things, then essences might be self-determined, as the Mu'tazila claim. The commentator al-Jurjani offers a different viewpoint, separating the essence from its attribute of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the different positions on creation: the Mu'tazila, the author's verification, and the commentator's view.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila say that possible non-existents are self-determined essences, and the agent only affects their attribute of existence.
This implies thingness of the non-existent.
3
Understand the author's verification
The author says the verification is that the essences themselves are the effect of the agent, or their attribute of existence. This branches from the thingness of the non-existent.
4
Grasp the commentator's solution
The commentator (al-Jurjani) says that the essence as such is not made because making cannot mediate between a thing and itself; what is made is the attribute of existence. This does not depend on the thingness of the non-existent.
This is a distinct view that avoids the problem.

قوله : ( من جملتها أن الماهيات غير مجعولة) إن أريد بالمسألة المردد بين الإيجاب والسلب، فتقديره أو مجعولة وإن أريد الجزء الأول منها فلا حاجة إلى التقدير، ثم تفرع مسألة الجعل على تلك المسألة . أما على ما ذكره المصنف في آخرها من أن عاقلاً لم يقل بأن الماهية الممكنة مستغنية في تقررها وثبوتها في الخارج عن الفاعل إلا ما ينسب إلى المعتزلة من أن المعدومات الممكنة ذوات متقررة ثابتة في أنفسها من غير تأثير للفاعل فيها، وإنما تأثيره في الاتصاف بالوجود وأما على ما هو التحقيق في هذه المسألة من أن الماهيات أنفسها أثر الفاعل أو اتصافها بالوجود، ولا شك في تفرعها على شيئية المعدوم ،وعدمه وأما على ما ذكره الشارح من أن معناها أن الماهية في كونها ماهية غير مجعولة، إذ لا يمكن توسط الجعل بين الشيء ونفسه لعدم التغاير إنما المجعول اتصافها بالوجود على سيجيء فلا شك أن عدم الجعل بهذا المعنى لا يتوقف على ثبوت الماهيات حال العدم كما لا يخفى . قول : ( فإن القائل إلخ) أي القائل باتحادهما في الصدق لا يمكنه القول بتلك المسألة المرددة إذ يصير المعنى أن المعدومات أي الماهية المرتفعة بالمرة موصوفة بالثبوت في الخارج أم لا . قوله : قيل ويمكن أن يعكس إلخ لعله اعتراض على ما ذكره الإمام بأن استلزام أحد

The First Observatory - The Sixth Section: Is the non-existent a thing or not? Concerning whether the non-existent is a thing or not, and it (i.e., this issue) is one of the mother issues (ummahat al-masa'il) of kalam, for many rulings branch from it, among them that essences (al-mahiyat) are not made (ghayr maj'ula). Some of them will come to you soon. Imam al-Razi said: The issue branches from the doctrine of the addition of existence to essence, for the one who affirms their identity cannot affirm it. It was said: And it is possible to reverse the ruling. Concerning the negation of distinction due to lack of positivity at all, because if distinction is by virtue of being existent in the mind, then their distinction is not insofar as they are non-existents, and the discussion is about it. And because the discussion is about the distinction of non-existents absolutely, not the distinction of external non-existents. So reflect, for all that stems from lack of reflection on the locus of dispute. His statement: (Is the non-existent a thing or not) the first part is a particular negative because it is a hypothetical proposition in the ruling of particularity, and the second part is a universal negative. His statement: (Among them that essences are not made) If by the issue is meant the disjunction between affirmation and negation, then its estimation is 'or made', and if the first part is meant, then there is no need for estimation. Then the issue of making (al-ja'l) branches from that issue. As for what the author mentioned at its end: that no rational person said that the possible essence is independent in its determination and establishment in the external from the agent, except what is attributed to the Mu'tazila: that possible non-existents are self-determined essences established in themselves without any effect of the agent in them, and His effect is only in the attribute of existence. And as for what is the verification in this issue: that the essences themselves are the effect of the agent, or their attribute of existence, and there is no doubt about its branching from the thingness of the non-existent and its negation. And as for what the commentator mentioned: that its meaning is that the essence as an essence is not made, for no making can mediate between a thing and itself due to lack of differentiation; rather, what is made is its attribute of existence, as will come. So there is no doubt that the negation of making in this sense does not depend on the establishment of essences in the state of non-existence, as is not hidden. His statement: (For the one who affirms, etc.) i.e., the one who affirms their identity in truth cannot affirm that disjunctive issue, for then the meaning becomes: Are non-existents, i.e., the essence elevated entirely, described by positivity in the external or not? His statement: 'It was said: And it is possible to reverse, etc.' Perhaps it is an objection to what the Imam mentioned, that the implication of one... His statement: (Among them that the essence is not made) The branching of this issue on the thingness of the non-existent is based on what the author mentioned of the proof for it. But according to the verification of the commentator which he will present later, its pivot is on the inconceivability of mediating making between the essence and itself, and the thingness of the non-existent has no role in that. His statement: (Imam al-Razi said: This issue branches, etc.) means the first issue, which is the first disjunctive part. For what he mentioned is in reality two issues. Then the branching in the Imam's speech is in the sense of dependence (tawaqquf), and in the speech of the one who says the reverse is in the sense of implication (luzum), and this is more apparent in the meaning of branching.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth section, which addresses the question: Is the non-existent (al-ma'dum) something (shay') or not? The author states that this is one of the major issues of kalam because many rulings depend on it, including the problem of whether essences (al-mahiyat) are created or not. The commentary explains that Imam al-Razi linked this issue to the doctrine that existence is additional to essence; if one holds that existence and essence are identical, then the question of whether the non-existent is a thing cannot be meaningfully asked. The passage also discusses a possible reversal of this relationship. It then delves into the meaning of the proposition 'Is the non-existent a thing or not?' analyzing its logical form as a particular negative and a universal negative. The commentary notes that the issue of 'making' (ja'l, i.e., creation) branches from whether the non-existent is a thing. Different positions are examined: the Mu'tazila hold that non-existent possible things are self-determined essences, with the agent only affecting existence; others hold that essences themselves are the effect of the agent. The commentator (al-Jurjani) offers his own verification: that the essence as essence is not made because making cannot mediate between a thing and itself; rather, what is made is the attribute of existence. This understanding does not depend on the thingness of the non-existent. The passage concludes by clarifying that Imam al-Razi's statement actually involves two issues, and the relationship between them is one of dependence (tawaqquf) or implication (luzum).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the debate about creation of essences is independent of the question of whether non-existents are things.
Correction: For the Mu'tazila and the author's verification, the creation debate branches directly from the thingness of the non-existent; only the commentator's view avoids this dependency.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator (al-Jurjani), what is 'made' (maj'ul)?
A) The essence itself
B) The attribute of existence
C) The non-existent thing
D) The agent

Key Takeaway
The issue of creation (ja'l) is intimately tied to the thingness of the non-existent for most theologians, though al-Jurjani offers a way to separate them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you perceive yourself as a 'thing' that was already determined before God created you, or do you acknowledge that your very essence is entirely dependent on His creative act? How does your answer shape your sense of humility and gratitude before Him?
?
If your essence (your 'whatness') were not directly created by God, what would that imply about your need for Him? In what areas of your life do you live as if your core identity is self-sufficient rather than a constant gift from the Creator?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that non-existents have a kind of reality independent of God. Is there any area of your life where you act as if something is 'real' apart from God's will — a habit, a relationship, or a goal? What would it mean to surrender even that to His creative authority?

Is the Non-Existent a Thing? Three Views
Three positions on whether the non-existent is a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam dispute over whether the non-existent can be considered a 'thing', and the arguments of the Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, and philosophers.

Core Idea
The non-existent as a thing? It depends on whether quiddity can be separate from existence.

In this lesson, we explore one of the most debated topics in Islamic theology: is the non-existent (al-ma'dum) a thing? At stake is the nature of quiddity (mahiyya) and its relationship to existence. The passage presents three distinct positions from the Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, and philosophers, each with their own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation. Notice the three groups mentioned: the Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, and philosophers.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three positions
Find where each position begins: the Mu'tazila view starts with 'فقال غير...', the Ash'ariyya with 'ومنعه الأشاعرة...', and the philosophers with 'وبه قال الحكماء...'.
Note the qualifications: the Mu'tazila are a subset, and the Ash'ariyya deny absolutely.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila argument
Understand that the Mu'tazila distinguish quiddity from existence; they say the possible non-existent is a 'thing' meaning it is determined in the external world without existence.
They restrict to the possible because the impossible has no determination.
4
Compare with the Ash'ari argument
The Ash'ariyya hold that existence is identical to reality; therefore, if the non-existent were a thing, it would imply a contradiction: being both existent and non-existent.
This is a direct rejection of the Mu'tazila position.
5
Understand the philosopher's agreement
The philosophers agree that the non-existent is not a thing, but for a different reason: quiddity never lacks existence (external or mental). Determination is itself existence.
This shows how different premises can lead to the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن من المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا قال بها يجب عليه القول بزيادة الوجود قطعاً ( فقال غير أبي الحسين البصري وأبي الهذيل العلاف والكعبي ومتبعيه من البغداديين ) من المعتزلة أن المعدوم الممكن شيء ) أي ثابت متقرر في الخارج منفك عن صفة الوجود، (فإن الماهية عندهم غير الوجود معروضة له، وقد تخلو عنه مع كونها متقررة متحققة في الخارج وإنما قيدوا المعدوم بالممكن، لأن الممتنع منه منفي لا نقرر له أصلاً اتفاقاً ( ومنعه الأشاعرة مطلقاً أي في المعدوم الممكن والممتنع جميعاً، فقالوا: المعدوم الممكن ليس بشيء كالمعدوم الممتنع ( لأن الوجود عندهم نفس الحقيقة فرفعه رفعها ) أي رفع الوجود رفع الحقيقة فلو تقررت الماهية في العدم منفكة عن الوجود، لكانت موجودة معدومة معاً، فلا يمكنهم القول بأن المعدوم شيء (وبه ) أي بما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة ( قال الحكماء ) أيضا ( فإن الماهية الممكنة وإن كان وجودها زائداً على ذاتها إلا أنها ) لا تخلو عندهم عن الوجود الخارجي أو الذهني ) يعني أنها إذا كانت متقررة متحققة فهي موجودة بأحد الوجودين لأن تقررها وتحققها عين وجودها
For from the first observatory - the sixth intention: is the non-existent a thing? He said: it necessitates him to definitively affirm the addition of existence. So (said other than Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf, al-Ka'bi, and their followers from the Baghdadis) of the Mu'tazila that the possible non-existent is a thing, i.e., established and determined in the external, separate from the attribute of existence. (For quiddity according to them is other than existence, a substrate for it, and it may be free of it while being determined and realized in the external. And they only restricted the non-existent to the possible because the impossible from it is negated, having no determination for it at all by agreement). And the Ash'ariyya denied it absolutely, i.e., regarding both the possible non-existent and the impossible non-existent, so they said: the possible non-existent is not a thing, like the impossible non-existent (because existence according to them is the very reality, so its removal removes it), i.e., removal of existence removes reality, for if quiddity were determined in non-existence separate from existence, it would be existent and non-existent together, so they cannot say that the non-existent is a thing. And by it, i.e., by what the Ash'ariyya held, the philosophers also said (for the possible quiddity, even if its existence is additional to its essence, yet it does not lack, according to them, external or mental existence), meaning that if it is determined and realized, it is existent by one of the two existences because its determination and realization are identical to its existence.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the classical kalām dispute over whether the non-existent (ma'dūm) can be called a 'thing' (shay'). The author first states the thesis: the one who holds a certain position must affirm the addition of existence. Then he reports the view of a group of Mu'tazila (excluding certain figures like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and others) who say that the possible non-existent (al-ma'dūm al-mumkin) is a thing. They mean by 'thing' something established and determined in the external world, separate from the attribute of existence. According to them, quiddity (māhiyya) is distinct from existence and can be free of it while still being determined externally. They restrict this to the possible non-existent because the impossible has no determination at all by consensus. The Ash'ariyya, on the other hand, deny this absolutely, for both possible and impossible non-existents. They argue that existence is identical to reality (ḥaqīqa) itself, so removing existence removes the reality. If quiddity were determined in non-existence apart from existence, it would be both existent and non-existent simultaneously—a contradiction. Therefore, the non-existent cannot be a thing. The philosophers (ḥukamā') agree with the Ash'ariyya, but for a different reason. Although they hold that existence is additional to essence (dhāt), they maintain that quiddity never lacks either external or mental existence. If a quiddity is determined and realized, it is existent in one of the two modes of existence, because determination (taqarrur) and realization (taḥaqquq) are identical to existence. Thus, there is no 'thing' that is non-existent.

الشيء
الوجود
الماهية
التقرر
التحقق
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila claim the non-existent is a thing in the same way an existent is.
Correction: The Mu'tazila mean it is a determined entity (muttaqarrir) in the external world, not actually existing; it is separate from the attribute of existence.

Checkpoint
Why do the Ash'ariyya deny that the possible non-existent is a thing?
A) Because existence is additional to essence, so non-existence negates the additional existence.
B) Because existence is identical to reality, so removing existence removes the reality itself.
C) Because the non-existent cannot be subject to judgment.
D) Because the Mu'tazila restrict it to the possible only.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether the non-existent is a thing hinges on whether quiddity is distinct from existence and can be determined without it.

The Absolute Quiddity's Relation to Existence
Presents and distinguishes a second interpretation of the absolute quiddity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of 'absolute quiddity never lacks existence' and how it differs from the first interpretation.

Core Idea
Quiddity, even without determination, always has some form of existence.

We continue exploring the relationship between quiddity and existence. The previous interpretation considered quiddity with determination. Now, a second view is presented: quiddity, taken absolutely—without any specification—never lacks one of the two existences. This passage clarifies what 'absolutely' means and distinguishes this interpretation from the first.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast between 'with determination' and 'without determination'.
The phrase 'وقيل' introduces the second view.
2
Identify the two possible meanings of 'absolutely'
The commentary gives two options: (1) generalizing quiddity to both possible and impossible, or (2) considering possible quiddity without assuming determination.
3
Compare with the first interpretation
Reflect on how this interpretation differs from the earlier one. The first required determination, the second does not.
This is the 'point of difference' mentioned in the passage.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check your grasp of the material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: هي مطلقاً لا تخلو عنهما. قوله: (وقيل هي مطلقاً) أي الماهية مطلقاً أي الممكنة والممتنعة أو الممكنة فرض تقررها، أو لا، لا تخلو عن أحد الوجودين. قوله: (وقيل هي مطلقاً لا تخلو إلخ) الإطلاق بالنسبة إلى التقرر أي الماهية الممكنة من غير اعتبار تقرر معها لا تخلو عن الوجود، وأما الكلام الأول فهو أن الماهية الممكنة مأخوذة مع التقرر وباعتباره لا تخلو عن الوجود فهذا وجه الفرق بين الكلامين ويحتمل أن يحمل الإطلاق على تعميم الماهية للممكنة والممتنعة جميعاً.
And it was said: It absolutely never lacks either of the two. His statement: (And it was said: It absolutely) means quiddity absolutely, i.e., the possible and the impossible, or the possible—whether one assumes its determination or not—never lacks one of the two existences. His statement: (And it was said: It absolutely never lacks etc.) The absoluteness is relative to determination, i.e., the possible quiddity without considering any determination with it is not free of existence. As for the first statement, it is that the possible quiddity taken with determination and in consideration of it is not free of existence. This is the point of difference between the two statements. And it is possible to interpret the absoluteness as generalizing quiddity to both the possible and the impossible.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a second interpretation of the earlier statement. Some scholars (G) say that quiddity absolutely never lacks one of the two existences, meaning that any quiddity—whether possible or impossible, and whether considered with determination or not—always has some existence. The commentary then distinguishes this from the first interpretation: the first considered quiddity with determination, while this one considers quiddity without determination. It also suggests that 'absolutely' might mean quiddity in general, covering both possible and impossible quiddities. Thus, the passage clarifies two different scopes of the claim about quiddity and existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that the second interpretation is identical to the first and that 'absolutely' adds nothing new.
Correction: The second interpretation explicitly drops the condition of determination, making the claim more general. It also opens the possibility of including impossible quiddities.

Checkpoint
According to the second interpretation ('وقيل'), what does 'absolutely' (مطلقاً) refer to?
A) Only possible quiddities.
B) Quiddities considered without determination.
C) Both possible and impossible quiddities, or possible quiddities without considering determination.
D) Only determined quiddities.

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation broadens the claim: quiddity, whether possible or impossible and with or without determination, always has some existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that your very essence (quiddity) is never without existence. When you look at your own life, do you sometimes feel that your existence is separate from who you really are? How does this truth affect the way you live each day?
?
If quiddity—even impossible ones—always has existence in some form, what does that say about your own potential and limitations? Are there areas where you use ‘I can’t’ as an excuse while ignoring the existence that Allah has already given you?
?
The second interpretation broadens the claim to include without determination. How might you be restricting your own understanding of your identity by insisting on certain conditions? Can you embrace your existence as given without adding your own conditions?

Refutation of the Proof from Judgment on Quiddity
Refutes the argument that every quiddity must exist mentally because it can be judged upon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key refutation of the argument that all quiddities must have mental existence simply because they can be judged upon.

Core Idea
Not every judgment requires full mental representation of the quiddity.

This passage examines a common argument for mental existence and then refutes it. The author first presents the proof, then considers an objection and a stronger critique. You'll see how careful distinctions—between essence vs. aspect, and actual vs. potential judgment—undermine the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Understand the claim: every quiddity must be judged upon as distinct, judgment requires conception, conception is mental existence, so every quiddity has mental existence.
This is the target of the refutation.
2
Note the objection about aspectual knowledge
An opponent says judgment does not require full knowledge of the essence (bi-l-kunn), only partial knowledge (bi-l-wajh), so full mental existence is not needed.
This is a common counterargument.
3
See how the commentator dismisses that objection
The commentator argues that knowledge of the aspect is knowledge of the quiddity itself, either due to the identity of knowledge and known or on the shadow theory.
This dismissal may not be entirely convincing, setting up the stronger critique.
4
Identify the decisive refutation
The commentator then offers a stronger critique: the proof assumes actual judgment, but if judgment is only potential, no actual conception is required, so the proof fails.
This is the main point of the lesson.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this refutation affects arguments for mental existence and the relationship between thought and reality.
Think about the broader metaphysical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( لأن كل ماهية حاصله أن كل ماهية يجب كونها محكوماً عليها بالامتياز والحكم على الشيء يستدعي تصوره الذي هو وجود ذهني له فكل ماهية لها وجود ذهني. وأما ما أورد عليه من أن الحكم لا يستدعي تصور المحكوم عليه بالكنه، بل بالوجه والشيء إذا علم بالوجه لم تكن ماهيته موجودة، بل ماهية الوجه فليس بشيء لأن وجود الوجه هو وجود الماهية، بناء على اتحاد العلم والمعلوم على التحقيق، أو لأن معنى وجود الماهية أن تكون صورتها موجودة في الذهن على رأي القائلين بالشبح. نعم يرد عليه أنه إن أراد أنه يجب كونها محكوماً عليها بالفعل فممنوع، وإن أراد بالقوة فهو لا يستدعي تصوره بالفعل.
His statement: (because every quiddity) the sum of it is that every quiddity must be judged upon as being distinct, and judgment upon something necessitates its conception, which is mental existence for it, so every quiddity has mental existence. As for the objection raised against it, that judgment does not necessitate conception of the judged in its essence (bi-l-kunn), but rather by an aspect (bi-l-wajh), and when something is known by an aspect, its quiddity does not exist, rather the quiddity of the aspect—this is nothing, because the existence of the aspect is the existence of the quiddity, based on the unification of knowledge and the known in reality, or because the meaning of the existence of the quiddity is that its form exists in the mind according to the view of those who hold the 'shadow' (shabah). Yes, an objection against it is that if he means it must be actually judged upon, that is denied; if he means potentially, then it does not necessitate its actual conception.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and its refutation. The argument claims every quiddity must have mental existence because judgment on something requires its conception, which is its mental existence. The refutation begins with an objection: judgment does not require full knowledge of the quiddity, only partial knowledge (by an aspect). The commentator dismisses this objection by arguing that knowledge of the aspect is knowledge of the quiddity due to the identity of knowledge and known, or on the 'shadow' view. However, the commentator then offers a stronger critique: if the judgment is actual, then conception is needed; but the quiddity might be judged only potentially, which does not require actual conception. Thus the proof fails.

Key Terms
محكوم عليه
تصور
وجود ذهني
كنه
وجه
شبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any judgment about a thing requires a complete mental representation of that thing's essence.
Correction: Judgment can be based on partial knowledge (by an aspect), and potential judgment does not require actual conception at all.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the proof that every quiddity exists mentally because it can be judged upon?
A) Judgment does not require any conception of the subject.
B) The quiddity may be judged only potentially, which does not require actual conception.
C) Mental existence is not real existence.
D) The quiddity of the aspect is different from the quiddity of the thing.

Key Takeaway
The proof that every quiddity must exist mentally because it can be judged upon fails because judgment may be only potential or based on an aspect, not requiring full mental representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge yourself as a 'failure' or 'success', are you basing that judgment on an aspect of yourself or your full quiddity? According to this lesson, what is the difference between judging by an aspect and by the essence? How will you change your self-judgment based on this distinction?
?
Consider a belief you hold strongly—perhaps about a person or a doctrine. Is it based on actual, complete knowledge (mental existence of the quiddity) or merely a potential understanding from limited aspects? How does this lesson’s distinction between actual and potential judgment challenge you to deepen your conviction or reconsider your certainty?

Thingness and Existence Are Synonymous
Declares that thingness and existence are coextensive and that the non-existent is a thing only in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for these scholars, thingness (shay'iyya) is coextensive with existence, and that the non-existent is only a thing in the mind.

Core Idea
Thingness and existence are two sides of the same coin.

This passage clarifies a foundational theological term: what does it mean to call something a 'thing'? The author argues that thingness is not more general than existence; instead, it is identical in scope. Only a mind can give reality to non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the denial of three alternatives
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author denies three possibilities about the non-existent being a thing externally, absolutely, or mentally.
The denial uses 'أما ... أو ... فكلا'.
2
Understand the thesis: thingness equals existence
The passage states that thingness is coextensive with existence and equal to it, though different in concept.
This is the core claim of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the example of blackness
The example shows that 'blackness exists' is more informative than 'blackness is a thing', illustrating the conceptual difference despite identical scope.
Consider why 'exists' adds value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم المعدوم في الخارج يكون عندهم شيئاً في الذهن وأما أن المعدوم في الخارج شيء في الخارج أو المعدوم المطلق شيء مطلقا أو المعدوم في الذهن شيء في الذهن فكلا فالشيئية عندهم تساوق الوجود وتساويه وإن غايرته لأن قولنا: السواد موجود يفيد فائدة يعتد بها دون قولنا: السواد شيء. وللنافي أي للذي ينفي كون المعدوم ثابتاً وجوه: الأول الثبوت والتحقق والتقرر أمر زائد على الذات.
Yes, the non-existent in the external is, according to them, a thing in the mind. As for whether the non-existent in the external is a thing in the external, or the absolute non-existent is absolutely a thing, or the non-existent in the mind is a thing in the mind—all are denied. Thus, thingness (al-shay'iyya) according to them is coextensive with existence and equal to it, although it differs from it in concept, because our saying: 'blackness exists' gives a significant benefit that our saying: 'blackness is a thing' does not. And for the denier (al-nāfī) – i.e., the one who denies that the non-existent is established – there are aspects: The first: establishment (al-thubūt), realization (al-taḥaqquq), and determination (al-taqarrur) are an extra thing beyond the essence.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage begins by affirming that the non-existent in the external world is considered a thing only in the mind (according to the scholars discussed). It then denies that the non-existent can be a thing externally or absolutely. The core claim is that 'thingness' (shay'iyya) is coextensive with existence, though the concepts differ: saying 'blackness exists' is more informative than saying 'blackness is a thing.' Finally, it introduces the first argument of the denier (al-nāfī, likely the Mu'tazila or philosophers who deny that the non-existent is a fixed thing): that establishment (thubūt), realization (taḥaqquq), and determination (taqarrur) are attributes additional to the essence. This sets up a debate about whether non-existent entities have any kind of reality.

Key Terms
شيئية
وجود
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'thing' applies to any object of thought, regardless of existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that for these scholars, thingness is tied to existence; the non-existent is only a thing in the mind, not a real thing.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the relationship between thingness (الشيئية) and existence (الوجود)?
A) Thingness is broader than existence.
B) Thingness and existence are coextensive and equal, though different in concept.
C) Thingness applies only to external existence.
D) Existence is a subset of thingness.

Key Takeaway
Thingness and existence are coextensive; the non-existent has reality only in the mind as a concept, not as a real thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of something you long for but do not yet have, do you treat it as a 'thing' in your mind as if it already exists? How does this passage compel you to distinguish between mental concepts and actual existence in your intentions and worship?
?

?
The passage says 'blackness exists' gives more benefit than 'blackness is a thing'. What does this reveal about the value of affirming existence in your speech? Are you avoiding affirming the reality of truth in your life when it is uncomfortable?

The Denier's First Argument: Thubut, Tahqiquq, Taqarrur Are Extra
Presents the first aspect of the denier who denies that the non-existent is established: that thubut, tahqiquq, taqarrur are additional to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument of those who deny that the non-existent is a fixed thing: that terms like thubut are additional attributes beyond the essence.

Core Idea
For the denier, existence and thubut are not the same thing.

After establishing the positive view that thingness equals existence, the author turns to objections. The first denier argues that words like 'thubut' (establishment) are extra qualities, not identical to the essence. This sets up a debate about the nature of reality and whether non-existents have any kind of fixedness.

Guided Path
1
Identify who al-nāfī is
The denier is the one who denies that the non-existent is established (thābit). This could refer to the Mu'tazila or philosophers who reject the Ash'ari view that non-existents have some kind of fixed reality.
Al-nāfī is a technical term for the opponent on this issue.
2
Understand the claim: thubut etc. are extra
The first aspect is that thubut, tahqiquq, and taqarrur are attributes additional to the essence (dhāt). This means they are not identical to existence or the thing itself.
Think about what it means for an attribute to be 'extra'.
3
Consider the implication
If these are extra, then a non-existent could be said to have thubut without existence. This challenges the coextensivity of thingness and existence.
This sets up the debate for the next aspects.

Thingness and Existence: Coextensive but Distinct
Defines thingness as coextensive with existence with a linguistic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that thingness (shay'iyya) and existence (wujud) are coextensive in scope but differ in meaning, and why this distinction is important in kalam.

Core Idea
Thingness and existence share the same scope but not the same meaning.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational distinction: thingness is not simply another word for existence. He shows that while everything that exists is a thing and vice versa, the two concepts carry different information. This distinction is crucial for debates about the reality of non-existents and the attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first addresses a potential objection about non-existents.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence where the author states that thingness and existence are coextensive and equal but differ. This is the core idea.
Look for the phrase 'تساوق الوجود وتساويه وإن غايرته'.
3
Examine the proof
The author provides a linguistic argument: saying 'blackness exists' gives useful information beyond 'blackness is a thing'. Ask yourself why this shows they are not identical.
Consider what 'exists' adds that 'thing' does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم المعدوم في الخارج يكون عندهم شيئاً في الذهن وأما أن المعدوم في الخارج شيء في الخارج أو المعدوم المطلق شيء مطلقا أو المعدوم في الذهن شيء في الذهن فكلا فالشيئية عندهم تساوق الوجود وتساويه وإن غايرته لأن قولنا : السواد موجود يفيد فائدة يعتد بها دون قولنا : السواد شيء
Yes, the non-existent in the external world is, according to them, a thing in the mind. As for the non-existent in the external world being a thing in the external world, or the absolute non-existent being a thing absolutely, or the non-existent in the mind being a thing in the mind, it is not so. Thus thingness with them is coordinate with existence and equal to it, although differing from it, because our saying 'blackness exists' conveys a benefit that is taken into account, unlike our saying 'blackness is a thing'.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines thingness (shay'iyya) and its relationship to existence (wujud). It first clarifies that the non-existent can be considered a thing in the mind, but not in the external world absolutely. Then it asserts that thingness and existence are coextensive (musawaqa) and equal (tasāwī) in extension, though they differ in concept. The proof is that saying 'blackness exists' provides useful information beyond saying 'blackness is a thing', indicating that existence adds a benefit (fā'ida) that thingness alone does not. This shows that they are not identical in meaning but share the same reference.

Key Terms
الشيئية (shay'iyya)
الوجود (wujud)
المعدوم (al-ma'dum)
الذهن (al-dhihn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That thingness and existence are identical in meaning, not just coextensive.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are coextensive but differ (wa in ghāyarathu), and the proof shows existence provides additional information.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between thingness (shay'iyya) and existence (wujud)?
A) They are identical in both meaning and reference.
B) They are coextensive but differ in meaning.
C) They are completely unrelated.
D) Existence is more general than thingness.

Key Takeaway
Thingness and existence are coextensive but conceptually distinct; existence adds meaningful information beyond thingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that saying 'this exists' provides a benefit that 'this is a thing' does not. When was the last time you truly felt the weight of the existence of something—a person, a blessing, or even your own life—and not just labeled it as a 'thing'? What would change in your daily attitude if you consciously replaced 'It is a thing' with 'It exists by Allah's will'?
?
The non-existent is only a 'thing' in the mind, not in external reality. How many of your current worries or ambitions exist only in your mind, and have no real existence outside? Are you treating them as if they are as real as existing matters? Write down one mental 'thing' that you need to either let go of or bring into existence through action.
?
When the passage says 'blackness exists' is informative while 'blackness is a thing' is not, it reveals that existence adds meaning. Consider your own declaration of faith: 'La ilaha illa Allah' — it affirms existence and uniqueness. Does your heart feel the benefit of that existence, or has it become a mere 'thing' you say? What practical step can you take today to ensure that the truth of Allah's existence impacts your decisions more than just the concept?
