The Dispute over Non-Existence is Verbal
Objection and answer on thubūt vs. wujud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate over whether the non-existent is a thing is resolved by distinguishing between thubūt and wujud, showing it is a verbal dispute.

Core Idea
The dispute over whether the non-existent is a thing is resolved by noting that thubūt is broader than wujud.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection in the debate over whether the non-existent can be considered a 'thing' (shay'). The objector argues that existence (wujud) and affirmation (thubūt) are identical, making the debate unnecessary. The author refutes this by showing that thubūt is more general, and if one insists on equating them, the disagreement becomes merely verbal. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objector's claim and the author's initial response that it is a mere figuration.
Notice how the objection tries to collapse thubūt into wujud.
2
Understand the core distinction
Focus on the line 'thubūt is more general than existence'. This is the key resolution. Ask why this distinction solves the debate.
3
See the verbal resolution
Notice the conditional: 'if thubūt is interpreted as existence, then it is verbal'. Understand what makes a dispute verbal rather than real.
The debate disappears when parties realize they mean different things by the same word.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this insight prevents real disagreements from arising over terminology. Reflect on other similar debates where clarifying terms resolves the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت يكفي أن يقال : لا معنى للوجود سوى الثبوت فلا حاجة إلى أن الثبوت زائد على الذات والاستدلال عليه بالاشتراك وإفادة الحمل قلت في هذه المقدمة تخييل للاتحاد بين الوجود والثبوت، لأن كلاً منهما زائد على الذات ومشترك ومفيد قلنا : بل هو أي الثبوت أعم من الوجود، فلا يلزم من ثبوت المعدوم في الخارج وجوده فيه فإن فسر الثبوت به أي بالوجود فلفظي
For if you say: It suffices to say that there is no meaning of existence other than thubūt, so there is no need for thubūt to be additional to the essence, nor for the proof of it by common participation and benefit of predication. I say: In this premise there is a figuration of union between existence and thubūt, because both are additional to the essence, common, and beneficial. We say: Rather, thubūt is more general than existence; so the affirmation of the non-existent in the external world does not necessitate its existence in it. For if thubūt is interpreted as existence, then the dispute is verbal.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents a hypothetical objection and its refutation. The objector claims that existence is nothing other than thubūt (affirmation/establishment), so thubūt need not be an additional attribute of the essence, and the previous proofs of its additional nature are unnecessary. The author responds that this premise is a mere figuration (takhayyul) of identity between existence and thubūt. In reality, both are additional to the essence, common to many things, and beneficial in predication. Then the resolution is given: thubūt is more general than existence. Therefore, affirming that a non-existent is 'thābit' in the external world does not mean it is existent there. If one insists on interpreting thubūt as existence, then the dispute becomes merely verbal (lafzī): both parties use the same word with different meanings.

Key Terms
الثبوت (thubūt)
الوجود (wujud)
الذات (dhat)
لفظي (lafzī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the disagreement over whether the non-existent is a thing is a real theological contradiction.
Correction: The passage shows it is a verbal dispute: the broader meaning of thubūt allows one to say the non-existent is 'thābit' without implying existence.

Checkpoint
How does the passage resolve the disagreement over whether the non-existent is a thing?
A) By proving that the non-existent is indeed existent in the external world.
B) By showing that 'thubūt' is more general than existence, making the dispute verbal.
C) By asserting that existence and thubūt are identical.
D) By denying that the non-existent is anything at all.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement over whether the non-existent is a thing is resolved by distinguishing between the broader term thubūt and the specific term wujud, making the dispute verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent disagreement you had about a religious or intellectual matter. Could it be that the dispute was merely verbal, rooted in different definitions of a key term? Are you willing to examine this honestly, and if so, to relinquish your insistence on the terminology for the sake of mutual understanding? What action will you take today to practice this discernment?
?
The author distinguishes between thubūt and wujud to resolve a theological dispute. In your own life, do you treat your ambitious plans or your self-image as if they are already existent (wujud) rather than merely affirmed in your mind (thubūt)? How might this confusion lead to self-deception or neglect of reality? What is one area where you need to check your assumptions against the actual state of affairs?
?
The passage warns against takhayyul (figuration) of unity between distinct concepts. Is there any area where you have falsely assumed two things are one, leading to confusion or flawed reasoning? For example, equating your emotional certainty with objective truth? How will you guard against such conflation in your pursuit of knowledge?

Thubut and the Verbal Nature of the Dispute
The commentator argues that the dispute is verbal if thubut equals existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute over thubut (affirmation) of non-existent essences is considered a verbal dispute when thubut is equated with existence.

Core Idea
The dispute over thubut is verbal because both sides agree on meaning but differ in terminology.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key debate in kalam: whether the non-existent is 'thabit' (affirmed). He argues that if thubut is taken to mean existence, then the disagreement between the Mu'tazila and their opponents is merely verbal. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the relationship between thubut and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text from the beginning up to the statement 'فالنزاع بيننا وبينكم لفظي'. Notice how the commentator first presents the problem and then resolves it.
2
Identify the key claim
The commentator says 'بل هو أعم من الوجود' (rather it is more general than existence). This is crucial: thubut is broader.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
If thubut is interpreted as existence, then the dispute becomes verbal: both sides say the same thing in different words.
4
Reflect on implications
This shows that precise definition can resolve apparent conflicts. The real issue is about the meaning of thubut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا أي الماهية لاشتراكه بين الذوات المعدومة (دونها ) أي دون خصوصية الذات فإن ذات السواد مثلاً ليست مشتركة بينه وبين البياض، فلا يكون الثبوت نفس الذات المعدومة ولا جزءها ، وإلا لزم التسلسل ( ولإفادة الحمل ) فإن قولنا : السواد ثابت يفيد فائدة بخلاف قولنا : السواد سواد ) ولا معنى للوجود إلا هو أي الثبوت، فلو كان المعدوم : ثابتاً لكان موجوداً، هذا خلف فإن قلت يكفي أن يقال : لا معنى للوجود سوى الثبوت فلا حاجة إلى أن الثبوت زائد على الذات والاستدلال عليه بالاشتراك وإفادة الحمل قلت في هذه المقدمة تخييل للاتحاد بين الوجود والثبوت، لأن كلاً منهما زائد على الذات ومشترك ومفيد قلنا : بل هو أي الثبوت (أعم من الوجود، فلا يلزم من ثبوت المعدوم في الخارج وجوده فيه فإن فسر) الثبوت (به) أي بالوجود ( فلفظي ( أي فالنزاع بيننا وبينكم لفظي لأنا نقول : المعدوم ثابت ونريد به معنى أعم من الوجود وأنتم تقولون : ليس بثابت بمعنى أنه ليس بموجود. الوجه قوله : ( وإلا لزم التسلسل) كما مر تقريره في إبطال جزئية الوجود، وهو أنه لو كان جزءاً لكان ثابتاً لامتناع كون المنفي جزءاً للثابت، فيكون الثبوت جزءاً له وهو أيضاً ثابت وهكذا. قوله : (تخييل إلخ) إشارة إلى ما نقل عن الحكماء أنهم إذا حاولوا التعليم والتفهيم، ابتدؤوا بالتخييلات للترغيب، ثم بالإقناعيات ثم الجدل ثم البرهان . قوله : ( فالنزاع بيننا وبينكم (لفظي أي الإثبات والنفي راجع إلى شيء واحد بحسب اللفظ، حيث قلنا : إنه ثابت وقلتم : إنه ليس بثابت أما إذا لوحظ المعنى، فلا نزاع لعدم اتحاد مورد الإثبات والنفي، لأن الثبوت عندنا أعم من الوجود وأنتم أردتم به الوجود، وليس المراد أن كل واحد من الفريقين يعترف ما يدعيه الآخر من حيث المعنى كما لا يخفى . قائلون باشتراك الذات بين الذوات وإنما التمايز بالأحوال لأن الاستدلال إلزامي كما يدل عليه سياق الأدلة . قوله لاشتراكه بين الذوات المعدومة تقييد اشتراك الثبوت بقوله: بين الذوات المعدومة نظراً إلى كلام الخصم وإلزامه له ولا يخفى أنه لو عمم الاشتراك ولم يقيد بما ذكر لكان أظهر بالنسبة إلى التخييل الذي ذكره بعيد هذا. ، قوله : ( ولا جزءها وإلا لزم التسلسل) كما مر مشروحاً في الدليل الثالث على زيادة الوجود الممكن، فإن التسلسل المذكور هناك على تقدير جزئية الوجود جار على تقدير جزئية الثبوت كما لا يخفى . قوله: (تخييل للاتحاد إنما قال : تخييل لأن صورة الشكل هكذا الثبوت زائد، والوجود زائد وشرط إنتاج الشكل الثاني وهو اختلاف المقدمتين مفقود هاهنا . قوله : قلنا بل هو أعم من (الوجود أي قلنا من طرف المعتزلة، فلا غبار كما توهمه من حكم بأن لفظة قلنا : سهو من القلم والأولى قيل.
The First Observatory - The Sixth Section: Is the non‑existent a thing or not? That is, the quiddity because of its sharing among non‑existent essences (excluding it) i.e. excluding the particularity of the essence, for the essence of blackness, for example, is not shared between it and whiteness. So thubut (affirmation) is neither the non‑existent essence itself nor a part of it, otherwise there would be regress. (And for the benefit of predication) because our saying 'blackness is thabit (affirmed)' gives a benefit unlike our saying 'blackness is blackness'. And there is no meaning of existence except it, i.e., thubut. So if the non‑existent were thabit, it would be existent; this is a contradiction. If you say: It suffices to say that there is no meaning of existence except thubut, so there is no need for thubut to be additional to the essence and the inference upon it by sharing and benefit of predication. I say: In this premise there is a takhyil (imagining) of the union between existence and thubut, because each of them is additional to the essence, shared, and beneficial. We said: Rather, it (i.e., thubut) is more general than existence, so the thubut of the non‑existent in the external does not necessitate its existence therein. If thubut is interpreted as it (i.e., existence), then (the dispute is) verbal, i.e., the dispute between us and you is verbal, because we say: the non‑existent is thabit and we intend by it a meaning more general than existence, and you say: it is not thabit in the sense that it is not existent. The aspect: His saying: (and otherwise there would be regress) as its explanation passed in the refutation of existence being a part, namely that if it were a part, it would be thabit because it is impossible for the negated to be part of the thabit, so thubut would be a part of it, and it is also thabit, and so on. His saying: (takhyil etc.) is a reference to what is transmitted from the philosophers that when they attempt teaching and making understand, they begin with takhyilat (imaginations) for encouragement, then with persuasive arguments, then dialectic, then demonstration. His saying: (the dispute between us and you is verbal) i.e., affirmation and negation refer to one thing in terms of the word, where we said it is thabit and you said it is not thabit. But if the meaning is considered, there is no dispute because the locus of affirmation and negation is not unified, because thubut with us is more general than existence, and you intend by it existence. And it is not meant that each of the two groups acknowledges what the other claims in terms of meaning, as is not hidden. (Some) say that the essence is shared among essences, and differentiation is only by states, because the inference is obligating as the context of proofs indicates. His saying: (because of its sharing among non‑existent essences) restricts the sharing of thubut by his saying 'among non‑existent essences' considering the opponent's statement and obligating him, and it is not hidden that if he had generalized the sharing and not restricted it as mentioned, it would be more apparent with respect to the takhyil which he mentioned, far from this. His saying: (and not a part of it, otherwise there would be regress) as explained in full in the third proof on the increase of existence in the possible, for the regress mentioned there on the assumption of existence being a part applies on the assumption of thubut being a part as is not hidden. His saying: (takhyil of union) he only said 'takhyil' because the form of the figure is: thubut is additional, and existence is additional, and the condition of producing the second figure, which is difference of the two premises, is missing here. His saying: (We said rather it is more general than existence) i.e., we said on the part of the Mu'tazila, so there is no objection as imagined by one who judged that the word 'qulna' is a slip of the pen and the better is 'qila'.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an argument and response in the debate over whether the non‑existent is a thing. The commentator first explains that 'thubut' (affirmation) is not identical to the non‑existent essence nor a part of it, because it is shared among distinct non‑existent essences. He then argues that if thubut were the same as existence, then affirming the non‑existent would imply its existence, which is contradictory. An objector says that existence can be equated with thubut, eliminating the need for thubut to be additional. The commentator responds that this is a takhyil (imagination) of the union of existence and thubut, but in truth thubut is more general than existence. Hence, the thubut of a non‑existent in the external does not imply its existence. If the opponent insists on interpreting thubut as existence, the dispute becomes merely verbal: both parties agree on the meaning but differ in terminology. The commentator then provides detailed glosses on phrases from the original text, including explanations of the regress argument, the pedagogical method of philosophers, and the nature of takhyil. He also clarifies that the restriction of thubut to non‑existent essences is for the sake of obligating the opponent.

Key Terms
ثبوت
وجود
تخييل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think thubut is exactly identical to existence.
Correction: Thubut is more general than existence; affirming a thing does not always mean it exists externally.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the dispute is verbal?
A) Because the Mu'tazila deny thubut entirely.
B) Because if thubut is equated with existence, both sides agree on the meaning.
C) Because the commentator refuses to argue.
D) Because the proof of application shows finitude.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over thubut is verbal because when thubut is meant as existence, both sides affirm the same reality with different terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on a specific religious term in a discussion, even though you and your interlocutor agreed on the underlying meaning? How does this lesson challenge you to examine whether your disputes are about words or reality? What will you do to avoid such verbal arguments moving forward?
?
The passage shows that affirming something (thubut) does not require its existence. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse merely affirming a truth with living it out? Identify one belief you affirm but have not acted upon, and commit to turning that affirmation into action today.

The Role of Takhyil in the Argument
The commentator explains the concept of takhyil and the philosophical method of teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's use of 'takhyil' and how it relates to the philosophers' pedagogical method.

Core Idea
Takhyil is a preliminary imaginative persuasion, not rigorous proof.

In the midst of the argument, the commentator refers to a premise as a 'takhyil' and explains that the philosophers use takhyil, then persuasion, then dialectic, then demonstration. This lesson focuses on that reference and its implications for understanding the nature of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Locate the mention of takhyil
Find in the source where the commentator says 'تخييل للاتحاد' and reads the explanation.
2
Learn the four stages
The commentator mentions four stages: takhyil, persuasion, dialectic, demonstration. Understand their order and purpose.
3
Apply to the argument
The objector's premise is only at the takhyil level, so it is not a solid proof. The commentator's response is at a higher level.
4
Appreciate the pedagogical insight
This shows that kalam arguments are built from weaker to stronger, and one must be aware of the level of evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think takhyil is a valid proof.
Correction: Takhyil is only imaginative persuasion, not a logical proof. It is the weakest form of argumentation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct order of the philosophers' pedagogical methods?
A) Takhyil, then persuasion, then dialectic, then demonstration.
B) Demonstration, then dialectic, then persuasion, then takhyil.
C) Persuasion, then takhyil, then dialectic, then demonstration.
D) Dialectic, then demonstration, then takhyil, then persuasion.

Key Takeaway
Takhyil is the weakest form of argument, used only to encourage, not to demonstrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers admit that the highest truths are taught gradually, starting with takhyil. When you present Islam to others, do you rely on imaginative stories or do you ensure you have solid demonstration behind your words? Think of one area where your own understanding might still be at the level of takhyil.
?
The objector's premise was accepted by many because it used a convincing imagination. Have you ever believed something about Allah or His attributes simply because it sounded beautiful or intuitive, without checking if it meets the standard of demonstration? Will you now examine one such belief and seek burhan?
?
Your soul loves certainty, yet you often settle for takhyil in matters of faith. What is one conviction you hold today that you have never subjected to rigorous, demonstrative proof? Are you willing to risk the discomfort of questioning it for the sake of true knowledge?

Proof of Finitude of Non‑Existent Essences by Application
The commentator demonstrates that non‑existent essences must be finite using the proof of application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and how it demonstrates that non‑existent essences must be finite, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
The proof of application shows that infinite non‑existent essences lead to logical contradiction.

This lesson unpacks a rigorous kalam argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that non‑existent essences are infinite. The commentator explains the proof of application step by step, showing why infinite non‑existent essences cannot be sustained. By the end, you will see how a simple comparison of aggregates compels finitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall structure. Notice how the proof is introduced and the key terms like 'deficient aggregate' and 'greater aggregate'.
The proof relies on the idea that removing a finite number from an infinite set should leave an infinite remainder, but here it leads to a contradiction.
2
Identify the proof's logical steps
Trace the proof: two aggregates are formed, one lacking the existents, one including them. Apply one to the other; the deficient must terminate, forcing finitude.
The commentator provides a detailed exposition of the well‑known method (الطريقة المشهورة).
3
Understand the objection and resolution
Notice why the author restricts the discussion to essences 'in non‑existence' and how the proof still applies to all essences established in non‑existence, including those that become existent.
The Mu'tazila also accept the world is temporal, so all existents were once non‑existent.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is important: it shows that the Mu'tazilite notion of infinite potential individuals is rationally impossible, supporting the Ash'ari position that only Allah is infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا ( الثاني الذوات) المتقررة (عندكم) في العدم ) غير متناهية ) لأنكم تقولون : بأن الثابت من كل نوع من الأنواع الممكنة أفراد غير متناهية (مع أنها ) أي تلك الذوات المتقررة ( إذ أخذت بدون ما قد خرج منها إلى الوجود كانت أقل من الكل) المتناول لما خرج ولما لم يخرج (بمتناه) هو ما خرج منها إلى الوجود، فإن الموجودات متناهية اتفاقا ) والأكثر من غيره بمتناه متناه ببرهان التطبيق لأنا نطبق الجملة الناقصة التي هي الذوات الباقية على العدم على الجملة الزائدة التي هي مشتملة على تلك الذوات مع الموجودات فلا بد أن تنقطع الناقصة فتكون متناهية، والزائدة إنما قوله : ( الثاني الذوات المتقررة بين أي تقريره على ما هو الطريقة المشهورة في جريان برهان التطبيق أنه لو كانت الذوات المتقررة في العدم غير متناهية فإذا فصل منها عدد متناه كالتي خرج منها إلى الوجود حصل جملتان إحداهما زائدة على الأخرى بمتناه، فنطبق إحداهما بالأخرى فإن وجد في الناقصة بإزاء ما في الزائدة يلزم أن لا تكون الناقصة ناقصة، وإن لم يوجد انقطعت الناقصة، والزائدة زائدة عليها بقدر ،متناه، فتكون متناهية فعلم مما ذكرنا أن المصنف إنما اعتبر التفاوت بينهما بدخول الموجودات وعدمه بطريق التمثيل ليكون ما به التفاوت بين جملتي الذوات المتقررة في العدم أمراً محققاً، لا بمجرد الاعتبار وإنما تعرض لبيان تناهي الأكثر الذي هو مشتمل على تلك الذوات مع الموجودات بعد لزوم تناهي الأول الذي هو تلك الذوات المتقررة فقط لأنهم قالوا إن الثابت في العدم من كل نوع أفراد غير متناهية لا أن الباقية منها بعد إخراج الموجودات غير متناهية فتدبر، فإنه مماخفي على بعض الناظرين . ، : قوله : ( والأكثر من غيره أي من غير الأكثر سواء كان الغير متناهياً أو غير متناه بقدر متناه متناه، والمراد بالأكثر الكثير ولذا استعمله باللام وكلمة من . قوله : (الذوات المتقررة عندكم في العدم قيل : التقييد بقوله : في العدم لأن وضع المسألة فيه وإلا فإذا أخذ مطلق الذوات المتناولة للمعدومات الغير المتناهية، والموجودات المتناهية كانت غير متناهية وأنت خبير بأن العالم حادث عند المعتزلة أيضاً، فكل موجود متقرر في العدم قبل الوجود، فالذوات المتقررة في العدم متناولة للمعدومة والموجودة معاً، لا أنها مختصة بالمعدومة كما يشعر به كلام القائل وبهذا التناول يشعر سياق الكلام في مواضع كما لا يخفى على الفطن، نعم تخصيص تقرر الذوات الموجودة بكونه في العدم أيضاً، لأنه الأنسب للسياق كما لا يخفى هذا ويمكن أن يقال : في تقرير الوجه الثاني الذوات المتقررة عندكم في العدم وإن كانت باقية عليه غير متناهية، مع أن تناهيها لازم ببرهان التطبيق بأن يعتبر منها جملتان، ويطبق إحداهما بالأخرى. قوله : ( والأكثر من غيره جمع في العبارة بين حرف التعريف، من وهذا وإن كان مخالفاً للقاعدة شائع في عبارات المصنفين .
The First Observatory - The Sixth Section: Is the non‑existent a thing or not? (The second: the essences) that are established (with you) in non‑existence are infinite, because you say that the affirmed from each kind of possible kinds are infinite individuals. (While they) i.e., those established essences (when taken without what has come out from them to existence are less than the whole) that encompasses what came out and what did not come out (by a finite) which is what came out from them to existence, for existents are finite by agreement. And the greater than another by a finite is finite by the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq), for we apply the deficient aggregate which is the essences remaining in non‑existence to the greater aggregate which comprises those essences together with the existents, so the deficient must terminate and thus be finite, and the greater... His saying: (the second: the essences established) i.e., its exposition is according to the well-known method in the running of the proof of application: if the essences established in non‑existence were infinite, then when a finite number is separated from them like those that emerged to existence, two aggregates result, one of which is greater than the other by a finite. So we apply one to the other: if there is found in the deficient corresponding to what is in the greater, then it would follow that the deficient is not deficient; and if not found, the deficient terminates and the greater is greater than it by a finite, so it is finite. Thus it is known from what we said that the author only considered the disparity between them by the entry of existents and their non‑entry by way of example so that what causes the disparity between the two aggregates of essences established in non‑existence is a realized thing, not by mere consideration. And he addressed the demonstration of the finitude of the greater which comprises those essences together with existents after the finitude of the first which is those essences only is necessary, because they said that the established in non‑existence from each kind is infinite individuals, not that the remaining after the extraction of existents is infinite, so reflect, for it is something that was hidden from some observers. His saying: (and the greater than another) i.e., from other than the greater, whether the other is finite or infinite by a finite quantity, is finite. And by 'greater' is meant 'much', so he used it with the definite article and the word 'min'. His saying: (the essences established with you in non‑existence) it is said: the restriction by 'in non‑existence' is because the position of the question is therein; otherwise, if taken absolutely the essences encompassing the infinite non‑existents and the finite existents would be infinite. And you are aware that the world is temporal (hadith) according to the Mu'tazila as well, so every existent is established in non‑existence before existence, so the essences established in non‑existence encompass both the non‑existent and the existent together, not that they are specific to the non‑existent as the speaker's words suggest, and this encompassing is suggested by the context of the speech in places as is not hidden to the intelligent. Yes, restricting the establishment of existent essences to being in non‑existence is also because it is more suitable to the context, as is not hidden. And it is possible to say: in the exposition of the second aspect: the essences established with you in non‑existence, although they remain upon it infinite, yet their finitude is necessary by the proof of application by considering from them two aggregates and applying one to the other. His saying: (and the greater than another) combines in the expression the definite article and 'min', and this, although contrary to the rule, is common in the expressions of authors.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that non‑existent essences are infinite. The argument uses the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) to show that if there were infinitely many essences in non‑existence, then removing a finite number (the existents) would leave a deficient aggregate that must be finite, implying the original aggregate is finite. The commentator explains the proof in detail: consider two aggregates—one consisting of all essences (including existents) and one consisting only of the essences that remain in non‑existence. The latter is deficient by a finite amount (the existents). By applying one to the other, we see that the deficient aggregate must terminate, so both aggregates are finite. The commentator also addresses possible objections, such as the restriction to essences in non‑existence and the nature of the 'greater' aggregate. He concludes that the author's presentation is sound and that the proof demonstrates the finitude of non‑existent essences, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view.

Key Terms
برهان التطبيق
ذات
معدوم
ممكن
متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non‑existent essences can be infinite because there is no physical limit to possibilities.
Correction: The proof of application shows that even conceptual infinities in non‑existence lead to logical contradiction when combined with finite actual existents. Therefore, non‑existent essences must be finite.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) regarding non‑existent essences?
A) They are infinite because of the variety of possibilities.
B) They must be finite because a deficient aggregate after removing a finite part must be finite.
C) They are neither finite nor infinite.
D) They are finite only if the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The proof of application is a rigorous demonstration that the Mu'tazilite claim of infinite non‑existent essences is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that infinite possibilities lead to contradiction. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if your options are infinite, delaying decisions because you think more possibilities will arise? How does knowing that God has set a finite order on all things—even on what could be—call you to trust His decree and act with decisive faith?
?
The proof of application uses the simple act of comparison to reveal a deep truth about existence. Have you ever stopped to consider that your own existence—finite and contingent—is directly sustained by the One who knows all possibilities? Does that realization humble you or fill you with gratitude? What will you do today to live in light of that dependency?
?
This lesson demonstrates that even non‑existent essences are finite and measured by God. Are there areas in your life where you have assumed that your potential or your future is boundless, ignoring the limits God has placed? How might acknowledging those limits free you to seek what truly matters for your Hereafter?

The Fourth Proof: Non-Existence Negates Its Subject
Sanusi presents the fourth proof with a short argument and supporting analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument of the fourth proof: that non-existence, being an attribute of negation, logically entails that its subject is negated (non-fixed).

Core Idea
Non-existence is a negative attribute; its subject is therefore negated.

The fourth proof uses a simple but powerful logical principle: the type of attribute determines the status of its subject. If the attribute is negative, the subject is negated. Sanusi applies this to non-existence to show that the non-existent is absolutely negated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a reason, and an analogy.
The passage is concise; every phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The argument has three steps: (1) Non-existence is an attribute of negation. (2) Anything qualified by a negative attribute is negated. (3) Therefore, the non-existent is negated. There is also an analogy with positive attributes.
The analogy reinforces the symmetry: positive attribute → affirmed subject; negative attribute → negated subject.
3
Reflect on the strength of this proof
This proof does not rely on causation but on logical attribution. Ask yourself: why is this considered a valid theological argument?
It is simple and direct, relying on the very definition of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الرابع أن العدم صفة نفي ( أي صفة منفية غير ثبوتية لأنه رفع الوجود ) والموصوف بصفة النفي نفي أي منفي غير ثابت ) كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات) أي بالصفة الثبوتية ( إثبات ) أي مثبت غير منفي، فالمعدوم المتصف بالعدم منفي قال الآمدي : ( هذا المسلك وإن حوم على معناه جمع من المتكلمين، كمحمد الشهرستاني وغيره إلا أنه هكذا مقرراً محرراً لم نجده لغيرنا فضلاء . قوله : (صفة نفي الظاهر أن الإضافة بيانية أي صفة حقيقته النفي لأنه رفع الوجود وعلى تقدير تأويله بالنفي، فاللائق أن يقال : غير ثابتة لا غير ثبوتية سواء أريد بها ما ليس السلب داخلاً مفهومه أو ما هو موجود فإن الاستدلال بعدم ثبوت الصفة على عدم ثبوت الموصوف أقوى، وأظهر من الاستدلال بعدم ثبوتها لأحد المعنيين على عدم ثبوته . قوله : (حوم) على (معناه في القاموس حوم في الأمر استدام فكلمة على بمعنى في أو بتضمين معنى الاستعلاء. قوله : ( وإنه في غاية الضعف) بكسر إن عطف على قوله قال الآمدي : فهو من كلام المصنف . قوله : (لا) نسلم أن المتصف إلخ يمكن دفعه بأنه لو كان ثابتاً لزم ثبوت تلك الصفة
The fourth aspect: that non-existence (al-'adam) is an attribute of negation (sifat nafy), i.e., a negated attribute, non-positive (ghayr thubutiyya), because it is the removal of existence. And that which is qualified by an attribute of negation is negation (nafy), i.e., negated, non-fixed (ghayr thabit). Just as that which is qualified by an attribute of affirmation (sifat al-ithbat) is affirmation (ithbat) i.e., affirmed, not negated. So the non-existent, qualified by non-existence, is negated. Al-Amidi said: This path, even though a group of theologians like Muhammad al-Shahrastani and others have hovered around its meaning, yet established and refined as we have it, we have not found it for others among the virtuous. His statement: (Attribute of negation): It is apparent that the idafa is bayaniyya, i.e., an attribute whose essence is negation because it is the removal of existence. On the interpretation of it as negation, it is more fitting to say: non-fixed (ghayr thabita) rather than non-positive (ghayr thubutiyya), whether what is meant is that negation is not part of its concept, or that it is existent. For reasoning from the non-fixedness of the attribute to the non-fixedness of the qualified is stronger and clearer than reasoning from its non-fixedness in one of the two senses to its non-fixedness. His statement: (hovered around) its meaning: in al-Qamus, 'homa' in a matter means he continued, so the word 'ala is in the sense of 'fi' or with the inclusion of the meaning of superiority. His statement: (And it is extremely weak): with kasr of 'in', it is a conjunction to the saying of al-Amidi, so it is from the speech of the author. His statement: (No) we do not accept that the qualified etc. can be repelled by [saying] that if it were fixed, it would necessitate the fixedness of that attribute.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This is the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi'). The argument is straightforward: non-existence is a negative attribute (sifat nafy). Anything qualified by a negative attribute is itself negated (i.e., non-fixed). So the non-existent, which is qualified by non-existence, is negated (manfi). This is an argument from attribution (al-wasf). The passage also includes a comment by al-Amidi that this argument, though touched upon by earlier scholars, is presented here in a refined form. Then there is linguistic commentary on the phrase 'sifat nafy' and the expression 'homa 'ala ma'nahu'. Finally, there is a rebuttal: if one objects that the qualified might still be fixed despite having a negative attribute, the answer is that if it were fixed, that attribute would also have to be fixed, which is not the case.

Key Terms
عدم
صفة نفي
صفة إثبات
منفي
ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that non-existence is merely a lack of existence and does not carry any implication about the subject's status.
Correction: Non-existence is a negative attribute, and any subject described by it is logically negated — it cannot be considered even potentially 'affirmed' in any way.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what does the fact that non-existence is a negative attribute (sifat nafy) imply about the non-existent (al-ma'dum)?
A) It is non-existent in a neutral sense
B) It is negated (manfi) and non-fixed
C) It is affirmable but absent
D) It is a possible object of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is a negative attribute, so the non-existent is necessarily negated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is merely the removal of existence and negates its subject, what does that mean for the temporary pleasures or possessions you cling to? Are they ultimately negated? How does this free you to focus on what truly exists?
?
The author argues that negative attributes negate their subject. Reflect on a habit or sin that you know is non-existent in value (e.g., it brings no lasting benefit). How does this proof help you see it as something to be negated in your life? What step can you take today to remove it?
?
Since only the existent can be affirmed, and Allah is the Only Necessary Existent, how does this proof deepen your awareness of His reality? What will you do this week to make His existence more present in your daily thoughts and actions?

Refining the Proof: Al-Amidi's Endorsement and Linguistic Clarifications
Al-Amidi's remark, linguistic analysis, and a defensive argument against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's evaluation of the fourth proof and the precise meaning of 'sifat nafy' and the defense against a key objection.

Core Idea
The proof is original and refined; careful wording avoids logical pitfalls.

After presenting the proof, Sanusi includes a remark from al-Amidi that this argument, though hinted at by earlier theologians, is presented here in a uniquely refined form. Then he clarifies the phrase 'attribute of negation' and defends the proof against an objection that the subject might still be fixed.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's comment
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Amidi's statement. Notice that he praises the refinement of this proof.
Al-Amidi is a key authority; his endorsement strengthens the argument.
2
Study the linguistic clarification
The author explains why 'sifat nafy' should be understood as 'an attribute whose essence is negation' and why 'ghayr thabita' is better than 'ghayr thubutiyya'.
This shows attention to precise terminology.
3
Understand the objection and reply
The objection claims that even with a negative attribute, the subject might be fixed. The reply is that if the subject were fixed, the attribute would also have to be fixed.
The reply is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الرابع أن العدم صفة نفي ( أي صفة منفية غير ثبوتية لأنه رفع الوجود ) والموصوف بصفة النفي نفي أي منفي غير ثابت ) كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات) أي بالصفة الثبوتية ( إثبات ) أي مثبت غير منفي، فالمعدوم المتصف بالعدم منفي قال الآمدي : ( هذا المسلك وإن حوم على معناه جمع من المتكلمين، كمحمد الشهرستاني وغيره إلا أنه هكذا مقرراً محرراً لم نجده لغيرنا فضلاء . قوله : (صفة نفي الظاهر أن الإضافة بيانية أي صفة حقيقته النفي لأنه رفع الوجود وعلى تقدير تأويله بالنفي، فاللائق أن يقال : غير ثابتة لا غير ثبوتية سواء أريد بها ما ليس السلب داخلاً مفهومه أو ما هو موجود فإن الاستدلال بعدم ثبوت الصفة على عدم ثبوت الموصوف أقوى، وأظهر من الاستدلال بعدم ثبوتها لأحد المعنيين على عدم ثبوته . قوله : (حوم) على (معناه في القاموس حوم في الأمر استدام فكلمة على بمعنى في أو بتضمين معنى الاستعلاء. قوله : ( وإنه في غاية الضعف) بكسر إن عطف على قوله قال الآمدي : فهو من كلام المصنف . قوله : (لا) نسلم أن المتصف إلخ يمكن دفعه بأنه لو كان ثابتاً لزم ثبوت تلك الصفة
The fourth aspect: that non-existence (al-'adam) is an attribute of negation (sifat nafy), i.e., a negated attribute, non-positive (ghayr thubutiyya), because it is the removal of existence. And that which is qualified by an attribute of negation is negation (nafy), i.e., negated, non-fixed (ghayr thabit). Just as that which is qualified by an attribute of affirmation (sifat al-ithbat) is affirmation (ithbat) i.e., affirmed, not negated. So the non-existent, qualified by non-existence, is negated. Al-Amidi said: This path, even though a group of theologians like Muhammad al-Shahrastani and others have hovered around its meaning, yet established and refined as we have it, we have not found it for others among the virtuous. His statement: (Attribute of negation): It is apparent that the idafa is bayaniyya, i.e., an attribute whose essence is negation because it is the removal of existence. On the interpretation of it as negation, it is more fitting to say: non-fixed (ghayr thabita) rather than non-positive (ghayr thubutiyya), whether what is meant is that negation is not part of its concept, or that it is existent. For reasoning from the non-fixedness of the attribute to the non-fixedness of the qualified is stronger and clearer than reasoning from its non-fixedness in one of the two senses to its non-fixedness. His statement: (hovered around) its meaning: in al-Qamus, 'homa' in a matter means he continued, so the word 'ala is in the sense of 'fi' or with the inclusion of the meaning of superiority. His statement: (And it is extremely weak): with kasr of 'in', it is a conjunction to the saying of al-Amidi, so it is from the speech of the author. His statement: (No) we do not accept that the qualified etc. can be repelled by [saying] that if it were fixed, it would necessitate the fixedness of that attribute.

Commentary
This is the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi'). The argument is straightforward: non-existence is a negative attribute (sifat nafy). Anything qualified by a negative attribute is itself negated (i.e., non-fixed). So the non-existent, which is qualified by non-existence, is negated (manfi). This is an argument from attribution (al-wasf). The passage also includes a comment by al-Amidi that this argument, though touched upon by earlier scholars, is presented here in a refined form. Then there is linguistic commentary on the phrase 'sifat nafy' and the expression 'homa 'ala ma'nahu'. Finally, there is a rebuttal: if one objects that the qualified might still be fixed despite having a negative attribute, the answer is that if it were fixed, that attribute would also have to be fixed, which is not the case.

Objection and response on negative attributes
Commentator objects to a claim about negative attributes and defends the possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negative attributes do not negate the subject's existence and how the commentator refutes an analogical argument to the contrary.

Core Idea
Negative attributes negate a quality, not the subject's existence.

The commentator addresses a key objection: if describing something by a negation implies the thing itself is negated. He defends the possibility of existent beings having negative attributes (like blindness) without implying their non-existence. In this passage, he dissects an analogical argument and explains the logical difference between affirmative and negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core objection
Read the passage and locate the claim being criticized: that describing something by a negative attribute makes the subject itself a negation. Notice how the commentator rejects this as weak.
2
Examine the analogical refutation
The commentator attacks an analogy that compares affirmative and negative attributes. See why he says the analogy fails: because the two cases have different requirements for the subject's existence.
3
Understand the logical distinction
Focus on the final part where the commentator explains: positive attributes require the subject's existence in itself, but negative attributes only negate something from the subject without negating the subject itself.
This is the key takeaway.

المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا

( وهو في غاية الإحكام والحسن وإنه في غاية الضعف ) والقبح ( إذ لا نسلم أن المتصف بصفة النفي نفي لجواز اتصاف الموجود بالسلب ( أي بالصفة السلبية التي لا ثبوت لها في نفسها كاتصاف زيد بالعمى ( وأما قوله كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات إثبات فقياس) تمثيلي (من غير جامع) بين المقيس والمقيس عليه (مع ظهور الفرق ( بينهما لأن ثبوت الشيء لغيره فرع على ثبوت ذلك الغير في نفسه، فلا يجوز أن يتصف المعدوم بصفة ،ثبوتية، بل لا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها ثابتاً في نفسه، وليس انتفاء الشيء من غيره فرعاً عن انتفاء ذلك الغير في نفسه، فجاز أن يتصف الموجود بصفة سلبية، فلا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بها منتفياً في نفسه.
The First Observatory - The Sixth Section: Is the non-existent a thing or not? (And it is in utmost perfection and beauty, and it is in utmost weakness) and ugliness (since we do not accept that the one described by a attribute of negation is a negation, because of the permissibility of describing the existent by a privation, i.e., by a negative attribute which has no existence in itself, like describing Zayd as blind. (And as for his saying: 'Just as the one described by an attribute of affirmation is an affirmation,' it is an analogical comparison without a common point between the compared and the compared-to, with the difference apparent between them because the existence of a thing for another is subsidiary to the existence of that other in itself, so it is not permissible for the non-existent to be described by a positive attribute; rather, the one described by it must be established in itself. And the negating of a thing from another is not subsidiary to the negation of that other in itself, so it is permissible for the existent to be described by a negative attribute, so the one described by it does not have to be negated in itself.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response concerning negative attributes (al-sifāt al-salbiyya). The commentator begins by stating that a previous claim (perhaps by al-Amidi) that something described by a negative attribute is itself negated is weak. He argues that the existent can be described by a negative attribute (salb), like Zayd's blindness, which is a privation with no positive reality. Then he critiques the analogical argument that 'as the one described by an affirmative attribute is affirmed, so the one described by a negative attribute is negated.' He says this analogy is flawed because the two cases differ: a positive attribute requires the subject's existence in itself, while a negative attribute does not require the subject's non-existence; rather, it negates something from the subject, but the subject itself remains existent. Thus, the non-existent cannot have positive attributes, but the existent can have negative attributes without being negated.

صفة الإثبات
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that describing something by a negative attribute implies the subject itself is negated.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that negative attributes only negate a specific quality, not the subject's existence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is it permissible for an existent to be described by a negative attribute?
A) Because the existent can have positive attributes.
B) Because the negation of a thing from something else does not require the negation of that thing in itself.
C) Because the existent is necessarily connected to its attributes.
D) Because negative attributes require the subject's existence in itself.

Key Takeaway
Negative attributes do not negate the subject's existence; they only negate a specific quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a negative attribute you often ascribe to yourself, like 'I am not patient.' Does this description make you feel as if you are somehow 'less existent' or less worthy? How does this lesson's distinction between negating a quality and negating existence change how you view your own shortcomings? What will you do to remind yourself of this truth when you next feel defined by a flaw?
?
When you describe someone else with a negative attribute (e.g., 'he is not generous'), do you inadvertently treat them as if they are 'nothing' in that regard? Based on this passage, how should your understanding of negative attributes affect your treatment of others? Write one specific intention to act on today.
?
The commentator says that describing Zayd as blind does not negate Zayd's existence. How often do you let a single negative attribute (in yourself or others) overshadow the person's entire existence? What will you do to separate the attribute from the person?

The Trilemma of Non-Existent Essences
Presentation of the trilemma about non-existent essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that presents a trilemma regarding non-existent things being distinct or united by their essences, and the absurd consequences of each option.

Core Idea
The trilemma forces a choice between three absurdities—unless the premise is rejected.

This passage presents a key philosophical argument about the nature of non-existent things (al-ma'dumat). The argument claims that if essences in non-existence are either distinct, united, or neither, each case leads to a contradiction or absurdity. Understanding this trilemma is crucial for later theological discussions about the attributes of things before creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the structure of the trilemma. Notice the three options presented.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: لو, فإن, وإلا.
2
Identify the three options
List the three possibilities: (1) non-existents are distinct by their essences, (2) they are united by their essences, (3) neither distinct nor united. Note the consequence of each.
3
Analyze the consequences
For each option, understand why it leads to an absurdity: different essences for same species, only one individual per species, or substrate for alternating attributes (sophistry).
The third option is said to lead to sophistry (safsata).
4
Reflect on the argument’s force
Consider why this argument was challenging for theologians. How does it problematize the notion of fixed essences in non-existence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس) المعدومات الثابتة في العدم لو تباينت لذواتها كان كل شيئين مختلفين بالذات فيلزم أن يكون كل فردين موجودين من نوع واحد كسوادين مثلاً متباينين متخالفين بالذات لأن مقتضى ذوات الأشياء لا يختلف ولا يتخلف عنها وإلا أي وإن لم تتباين لذواتها فإن اتحدت لذواتها لم تتكثر في الوجود بل كانت متصفة بالوحدة التي تقتضيها ذواتها فيلزم أن يكون النوع الواحد كالسواد مثلاً منحصراً في فرد واحد وإلا أي وإن لم تتحد لذواتها أيضاً كما لم تتباين لذواتها فالمعدوم حال العدم مورد للمتزايلات أي الصفات المتعاقبة فإن ذات المعدوم لما لم تقتض الوحدة ولا الكثرة اللازمة للتباين جاز أن يعرض له كل واحدة منهما بسبب أمر خارج عنه ويلزم السفسطة أعني جواز تعاقب الحركات والسكنات على المعدوم قلنا : قولك لذواتها إن أردت به لماهيتها اخترنا أنها لا تتباين لذواتها ولا تتحد أيضاً لذواتها، ولا يلزم كونها مورداً للمتزايلات، إذ التميز إنما يعرض للهويات الثابتة في العدم وكل ما تمتاز به هوية عما عداها فإنه لازم لها فلا توارد ولا تزايل بالنسبة إلى الهويات، نعم يلزم أن تكون الماهية المشتركة بين تلك الهويات مقارنة لأمور بها يمتاز بعض أفرادها عن بعض وأما أن ذلك التقارن على سبيل التوارد والتزايل، فلا فإن قلت : إذا لم تقتض الماهية الواحدة ولا الكثرة جاز تعاقبهما عليها قوله : ( المعدومات الثابتة في العدم إلخ ) يعني أن المعدومات الثابتة لا شك أنها مختلفة بأمور متباينة، فالاختلاف الحاصل لكل واحد منها مع قطع النظر عن الآخر، إن كان مقتضى ذاته بأن تقتضي ذاته ذلك الأمر الذي به الاختلاف، يلزم أن يكون كل موجودين في الخارج مختلفين بالذات لامتناع اختلاف مقتضى الشيء الواحد، وتخلفه عنه وإن لم يكن مقتضى ذاته، فإن كان مقتضى ذاته الاتحاد بأن يقتضي أمراً واحداً، لزم أن لا يوجد في الخارج من كل نوع إلا فردِ واحد، وإن لم تقتض ذاته الاختلاف ولا الاتحاد يلزم جواز كون المعدوم حال العدم مورداً للمتزايلات بالنظر إلى ذاته وبما حررنا اندفع ما يتوهم من أنه يجوز أن لا يقتضي كل المعدومات التباين ولا الاتحاد بل يقتضي البعض التباين وبعضها الاتحاد فلا يلزم شيء من الأمرين، وأن التباين ليس مقابلاً للاتحاد، بل التعدد فالترديد غير حاصر، ولو أريد به التعدد لا يلزم اختلاف كل شيئين، بل تعددهما وهو واقع فتدبر قوله : ( جاز أن يعرض له كل واحدة منهما ) أي بالنظر إلى ذاته فيلزم جواز تعاقب الحركات والسكنات عليه بالنظر إلى ذاله ، وذا باطل قطعاً، فلا يرد أن يقال : عدم اقتضاء الماهية الوحدة والكثرة في نفسها لا ينافي امتناع تعاقبها نظراً إلى أمر آخر مانع فإن مجرد قابلية المحل لا يكفي قوله : ( مقارنة لأمور إلح فيه بحث لا يخفى لأن السؤال لا يرد بالنظر إلى تلك الأمور المقارنة للماهية المشتركة لأن الماهية لا تقتضي شيئاً منها، وإلا انحصرت في هوية واحدة,
The Fifth Aspect: The non-existents established in non-existence, if they were distinct by their essences, then every two things would be different in essence, and it would follow that every two existing individuals of one species, like two blacknesses for example, would be mutually distinct and different in essence, because the requirement of things' essences does not differ and does not fall away from them. Otherwise (i.e., if they are not distinct by their essences), if they are united by their essences, they would not multiply in existence but would be characterized by the unity that their essences require, and then it would follow that the one species, like blackness for example, would be confined to a single individual. Otherwise (i.e., if they are also not united by their essences, just as they are not distinct by their essences), then the non-existent, in the state of non-existence, is a substrate for alternators, i.e., successive attributes, because the essence of the non-existent does not require unity nor the multiplicity necessary for distinctness, so it is possible for each of them to occur to it due to an external cause, and sophistry follows, meaning the permissibility of motions and rests alternating upon the non-existent. We say: your statement 'by their essences' if you mean by it their quiddities, we choose that they are neither distinct by their essences nor united by their essences, and their being a substrate for alternators does not follow, for differentiation only occurs to the identities (hawiyyat) established in non-existence, and everything by which an identity is distinguished from what is other than it is necessary for it, so there is no succession nor alternation with respect to identities. Yes, it follows that the common quiddity among those identities is concomitant with matters by which some of its individuals are distinguished from others. But that this concomitance is in the mode of succession and alternation—no. And if you say: when the quiddity requires neither oneness nor multiplicity, it is possible for them to alternate upon it. His statement: 'The non-existents established in non-existence, etc.' means that the established non-existents are undoubtedly different by distinct matters. So the difference occurring to each of them, disregarding the other, if it is required by its essence—i.e., its essence requires that matter by which the difference occurs—then it follows that every two existents in the external world are different in essence, because it is impossible for the requirement of one thing to differ and to fall away from it. And if it is not required by its essence, then if its essence requires unity—i.e., it requires one matter—then it would follow that in the external world, from each species, only one individual exists. And if its essence requires neither difference nor unity, then it follows that it is possible for the non-existent, in the state of non-existence, to be a substrate for alternators with respect to its essence. And by what we have set forth, the misconception is repelled that it is permissible that not all non-existents require distinctness nor unity, but rather some require distinctness and some unity, so that neither of the two consequences follows, and that distinctness is not the opposite of unity but rather multiplicity, so the division into three is not exhaustive. And if multiplicity were intended, then the difference of every two things would not follow, but rather their multiplicity, and that is actual—so reflect. His statement: 'It is possible for each of them to occur to it'—i.e., with respect to its essence, so the permissibility of motions and rests alternating upon it follows with respect to its essence, and that is absolutely false. So it does not follow that it be said: the non-requirement of the quiddity for unity and multiplicity in itself does not contradict the impossibility of their alternation with respect to another preventing matter, for mere receptivity of the locus is not sufficient. His statement: 'concomitant with matters, etc.'—there is in it an investigation that is not hidden, because the question does not arise with respect to those matters concomitant to the common quiddity, because the quiddity does not require any of them; otherwise it would be confined to a single identity.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-wajh al-khamis) regarding whether non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) are distinct by their essences in non-existence, and then the commentator's response. The argument is a trilemma: If non-existents are distinct by their essences, then every two individuals of a species (e.g., two blacknesses) would be different in essence, contradicting the fact that they share the same essence. If they are united by their essences, then only one individual of each species could exist. If they are neither distinct nor united, then each non-existent essence would be a substrate for alternating attributes (al-mutazayilat), like motion and rest, which is absurd (sophistry). The commentator responds by clarifying that 'by their essences' refers to their quiddities (mahiyyat). He chooses that non-existents are neither distinct nor united by their quiddities; rather, differentiation occurs to the identities (hawiyyat) in non-existence, and the distinguishing properties are necessary for each identity, not subject to alternation. The common quiddity is accompanied by differentiating matters, but not in a successive manner. He then addresses potential objections, including that some essences might require distinctness and some unity, and that the division is not exhaustive. He concludes that the argument's conclusion that the non-existent is a substrate for alternators is false, as the quiddity's non-requirement does not entail permissibility of alternation, and mere receptivity is insufficient.

الذات
التباين
الاتحاد
المتزايلات

Checkpoint
What does the fifth argument claim would follow if non-existents are neither distinct nor united by their essences?
A) They would be completely identical.
B) They would be nonexistent.
C) They would be substrates for alternating attributes, leading to sophistry.
D) They would necessarily exist in the external world.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument presents a trilemma that challenges the view that non-existent things have fixed essences by showing each possible relation (distinct, united, or neither) leads to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The trilemma shows that assuming non-existent things have fixed essences leads to absurdity. Think of a time you assumed something about someone who is absent (a 'non-existent' in a sense) and later found your assumption led to a contradiction or error. How does this lesson urge you to be more cautious in making judgments about unseen realities?
?
In your own life, do you ever hold onto a belief about God or existence that, if examined logically, might lead to absurd consequences? Are you willing to let go of such assumptions, even if they are comfortable?
?
The third option in the trilemma leads to sophistry—accepting that anything can happen. In your spiritual journey, have you ever been tempted to accept logical contradictions because they seem convenient? How does this passage call you to intellectual integrity even when it is difficult?

Refuting the Trilemma: Quiddity vs. Identity
Refutation of the fifth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the fifth argument by distinguishing between quiddities and identities, showing that non-existents are neither distinct nor united in quiddity yet differentiation occurs through necessary properties.

Core Idea
Non-existents are not distinct or united in quiddity, but differentiation occurs through identities.

The commentator responds to the trilemma by clarifying the meaning of 'by their essences.' He argues that non-existents are neither distinct nor united by their quiddities, but differentiation occurs at the level of identities (hawiyyat) which have necessary distinguishing properties. This avoids the absurdities of the trilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic text from 'قلنا' to the end, along with the translation. Notice how the commentator begins by reinterpreting 'by their essences'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the commentator distinguishes between quiddity (mahiyya) and identity (hawiyya). Understand how this distinction allows a middle path.
Non-existents are neither distinct nor united in quiddity, but identities have necessary distinguishing properties.
3
Understand the response to the third horn
See why the third option (neither distinct nor united) does not lead to sophistry. The commentator argues that identities have necessary properties, preventing alternation.
4
Analyze the objection about exhaustiveness
Read how the commentator addresses the objection that the trilemma might not be exhaustive because some essences could require distinctness and some unity. He shows that the argument still holds for those cases.
5
Reflect on the overall resolution
Consider how the response resolves the initial problem. What does it mean for the nature of non-existent essences?

الاتحاد فلا يلزم شيء من الأمرين، وأن التباين ليس مقابلاً للاتحاد، بل التعدد فالترديد غير حاصر، ولو أريد به التعدد لا يلزم اختلاف كل شيئين، بل تعددهما وهو واقع فتدبر قوله : ( جاز أن يعرض له كل واحدة منهما ) أي بالنظر إلى ذاته فيلزم جواز تعاقب الحركات والسكنات عليه بالنظر إلى ذاله ، وذا باطل قطعاً، فلا يرد أن يقال : عدم اقتضاء الماهية الوحدة والكثرة في نفسها لا ينافي امتناع تعاقبها نظراً إلى أمر آخر مانع فإن مجرد قابلية المحل لا يكفي قوله : ( مقارنة لأمور إلح فيه بحث لا يخفى لأن السؤال لا يرد بالنظر إلى تلك الأمور المقارنة للماهية المشتركة لأن الماهية لا تقتضي شيئاً منها، وإلا انحصرت في هوية واحدة,
The Fifth Aspect: The non-existents established in non-existence, if they were distinct by their essences, then every two things would be different in essence, and it would follow that every two existing individuals of one species, like two blacknesses for example, would be mutually distinct and different in essence, because the requirement of things' essences does not differ and does not fall away from them. Otherwise (i.e., if they are not distinct by their essences), if they are united by their essences, they would not multiply in existence but would be characterized by the unity that their essences require, and then it would follow that the one species, like blackness for example, would be confined to a single individual. Otherwise (i.e., if they are also not united by their essences, just as they are not distinct by their essences), then the non-existent, in the state of non-existence, is a substrate for alternators, i.e., successive attributes, because the essence of the non-existent does not require unity nor the multiplicity necessary for distinctness, so it is possible for each of them to occur to it due to an external cause, and sophistry follows, meaning the permissibility of motions and rests alternating upon the non-existent. We say: your statement 'by their essences' if you mean by it their quiddities, we choose that they are neither distinct by their essences nor united by their essences, and their being a substrate for alternators does not follow, for differentiation only occurs to the identities (hawiyyat) established in non-existence, and everything by which an identity is distinguished from what is other than it is necessary for it, so there is no succession nor alternation with respect to identities. Yes, it follows that the common quiddity among those identities is concomitant with matters by which some of its individuals are distinguished from others. But that this concomitance is in the mode of succession and alternation—no. And if you say: when the quiddity requires neither oneness nor multiplicity, it is possible for them to alternate upon it. His statement: 'The non-existents established in non-existence, etc.' means that the established non-existents are undoubtedly different by distinct matters. So the difference occurring to each of them, disregarding the other, if it is required by its essence—i.e., its essence requires that matter by which the difference occurs—then it follows that every two existents in the external world are different in essence, because it is impossible for the requirement of one thing to differ and to fall away from it. And if it is not required by its essence, then if its essence requires unity—i.e., it requires one matter—then it would follow that in the external world, from each species, only one individual exists. And if its essence requires neither difference nor unity, then it follows that it is possible for the non-existent, in the state of non-existence, to be a substrate for alternators with respect to its essence. And by what we have set forth, the misconception is repelled that it is permissible that not all non-existents require distinctness nor unity, but rather some require distinctness and some unity, so that neither of the two consequences follows, and that distinctness is not the opposite of unity but rather multiplicity, so the division into three is not exhaustive. And if multiplicity were intended, then the difference of every two things would not follow, but rather their multiplicity, and that is actual—so reflect. His statement: 'It is possible for each of them to occur to it'—i.e., with respect to its essence, so the permissibility of motions and rests alternating upon it follows with respect to its essence, and that is absolutely false. So it does not follow that it be said: the non-requirement of the quiddity for unity and multiplicity in itself does not contradict the impossibility of their alternation with respect to another preventing matter, for mere receptivity of the locus is not sufficient. His statement: 'concomitant with matters, etc.'—there is in it an investigation that is not hidden, because the question does not arise with respect to those matters concomitant to the common quiddity, because the quiddity does not require any of them; otherwise it would be confined to a single identity.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-wajh al-khamis) regarding whether non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) are distinct by their essences in non-existence, and then the commentator's response. The argument is a trilemma: If non-existents are distinct by their essences, then every two individuals of a species (e.g., two blacknesses) would be different in essence, contradicting the fact that they share the same essence. If they are united by their essences, then only one individual of each species could exist. If they are neither distinct nor united, then each non-existent essence would be a substrate for alternating attributes (al-mutazayilat), like motion and rest, which is absurd (sophistry). The commentator responds by clarifying that 'by their essences' refers to their quiddities (mahiyyat). He chooses that non-existents are neither distinct nor united by their quiddities; rather, differentiation occurs to the identities (hawiyyat) in non-existence, and the distinguishing properties are necessary for each identity, not subject to alternation. The common quiddity is accompanied by differentiating matters, but not in a successive manner. He then addresses potential objections, including that some essences might require distinctness and some unity, and that the division is not exhaustive. He concludes that the argument's conclusion that the non-existent is a substrate for alternators is false, as the quiddity's non-requirement does not entail permissibility of alternation, and mere receptivity is insufficient.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a quiddity does not require unity or multiplicity, then it is possible for contradictory attributes like motion and rest to alternate upon it.
Correction: The commentator shows that identities have necessary distinguishing properties that prevent such alternation, and mere non-requirement of the quiddity does not entail possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what prevents non-existents from being substrates for alternating attributes?
A) The quiddity requires unity.
B) The quiddity requires multiplicity.
C) Identities have necessary properties that preclude alternation.
D) Non-existents do not exist, so no attributes can occur.

Key Takeaway
By distinguishing quiddity from identity, the commentator shows that non-existents are not subject to the trilemma's absurdities, as identities have necessary distinguishing properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator says mere non-requirement does not entail possibility. Where in your life have you assumed something was possible simply because it was not impossible? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that assumption?
?
Consider the common quiddity and distinguishing matters: you share humanity with others, yet your identity is uniquely yours. How does this distinction affect your sense of responsibility and purpose? Are you using your unique identity to serve God, or blending in with the crowd?

The Objection and Re-Explanation of the Trilemma on Quiddity
Al-Jurjani re-explains the trilemma on quiddity's entailment of unity or multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani re-explains the trilemma of a quiddity entailing either unity, multiplicity, or neither, in response to an objection about association not being by succession.

Core Idea
A quiddity's intrinsic requirement forces one of three logical outcomes.

Al-Jurjani faces an objection: even if the common quiddity does not entail unity or multiplicity, it might be associated with distinguishing features without alternating. He re-explains the original argument by considering the essence of nonexistent things, setting up three possibilities. This passage lays out the foundational trilemma that underlies the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the claim that the association of distinguishing matters to the common quiddity is not by way of succession and alternation. Then read the re-explanation of the trilemma.
The objection tries to avoid the trilemma by denying perpetual alternation.
2
Identify the three branches of the trilemma
The passage presents three possibilities: the essence requires difference, requires unity, or requires neither. each leads to a distinct consequence.
3
Understand why the trilemma is exhaustive
Every nonexistent thing's essence must either require the difference, require unity, or require neither. There is no fourth option for a given quiddity.
This sets up the rebuttal of the supposition that some quiddities could vary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يلزم أن تكون الماهية المشتركة بين تلك الهويات مقارنة لأمور بها يمتاز بعض أفرادها عن بعض وأما أن ذلك التقارن على سبيل التوارد والتزايل، فلا فإن قلت: إذا لم تقتض الماهية الواحدة ولا الكثرة جاز تعاقبهما عليها . قوله: ( المعدومات الثابتة في العدم إلخ ) يعني أن المعدومات الثابتة لا شك أنها مختلفة بأمور متباينة، فالاختلاف الحاصل لكل واحد منها مع قطع النظر عن الآخر، إن كان مقتضى ذاته بأن تقتضي ذاته ذلك الأمر الذي به الاختلاف، يلزم أن يكون كل موجودين في الخارج مختلفين بالذات لامتناع اختلاف مقتضى الشيء الواحد، وتخلفه عنه وإن لم يكن مقتضى ذاته، فإن كان مقتضى ذاته الاتحاد بأن يقتضي أمراً واحداً، لزم أن لا يوجد في الخارج من كل نوع إلا فردِ واحد، وإن لم تقتض ذاته الاختلاف ولا الاتحاد يلزم جواز كون المعدوم حال العدم مورداً للمتزايلات بالنظر إلى ذاته وبما حررنا اندفع ما يتوهم من أنه يجوز أن لا يقتضي كل المعدومات التباين ولا الاتحاد بل يقتضي البعض التباين وبعضها الاتحاد فلا يلزم شيء من الأمرين، وأن التباين ليس مقابلاً للاتحاد، بل التعدد فالترديد غير حاصر، ولو أريد به التعدد لا يلزم اختلاف كل شيئين، بل تعددهما وهو واقع فتدبر.
Yes, it is necessary that the common quiddity (al-mahiyya al-mushtaraka) among those individual identities is associated with matters by which some individuals are distinguished from others, but that association is not by way of succession and alternation. For if you say: if the quiddity neither entails unity nor multiplicity, then succession of them upon it is possible. He said: (The nonexistent things established in nonexistence etc.) means that the established nonexistent things are undoubtedly different by diverse matters. The difference occurring to each one, disregarding the other, if it is required by its essence (i.e., its essence entails that matter by which the difference occurs), then it would be necessary that every two things existing externally are essentially different, because it is impossible for a single thing's requirement to differ and to deviate from it. And if it is not required by its essence, then if its essence entails unity (i.e., it entails one matter), it would be necessary that nothing exists externally except one individual of each species. And if its essence entails neither difference nor unity, then the possibility that the nonexistent during nonexistence is a locus for successive accidents with respect to its essence follows. By what we have set forth is repelled the supposition that it is permissible that not all nonexistent things entail diversity nor unity but rather some entail diversity and some unity, so nothing from the two matters follows; and that diversity is not the opposite of unity but rather multiplicity, so the disjunction is not exhaustive; and if it is intended as multiplicity, it does not require the difference of every two things but rather their multiplicity, and that is actual – so reflect.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: even if the quiddity does not entail unity or multiplicity, it might be associated with certain distinguishing matters, but not by way of succession. The objection claims that if the quiddity entails neither unity nor multiplicity, then both could succeed upon it. The commentator explains the original argument again: the difference in each nonexistent thing, considered alone, if it is required by its essence, then all existent things would be essentially different; if unity is required, then each species would have only one individual; if neither, then the nonexistent would be a locus for successive accidents. He then refutes a misunderstanding: one might think that some nonexistent things entail diversity and some unity, so the disjunction is not exhaustive. But the reply is that 'diversity' (tabayun) here means multiplicity (ta'addud), not the opposite of unity; and even if some entail diversity and some unity, the argument still holds because the quiddity must be consistent. The passage ends with an encouragement to reflect.

Key Terms

التقارن
التوارد والتزايل
مقتضى الذات
التباين
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if the difference of a nonexistent thing is required by its essence?
A) It would entail unity of all things.
B) Every two existent things would be essentially different.
C) Only one individual per species would exist.
D) The nonexistent could have successive accidents.

Key Takeaway
A quiddity's intrinsic requirement forces one of three outcomes regarding its instantiations: essential difference, essential unity, or possibility of successive accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat any of your core beliefs as indifferent to truth or falsehood, assuming they can be either without consequence? This trilemma shows that even quiddities cannot be neutral—what does that demand of your convictions?
?
If a quiddity's essence forces a logical outcome, then what about your own actions? Are they merely accidental, or do they flow from your character? This lesson's logic presses you: what does your constant behavior reveal about what you truly are?

Rebuttal of the Misunderstanding about the Disjunction
Al-Jurjani refutes the misunderstanding that the disjunction is not exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the supposition that the disjunction is not exhaustive because some quiddities could entail diversity and some unity.

Core Idea
Diversity here means multiplicity, so the trilemma remains exhaustive for each quiddity.

After re-examining the trilemma, al-Jurjani addresses a potential misunderstanding: one might think that not all nonexistent things entail either diversity or unity — some could entail one and some the other. He rebuts this by clarifying that 'diversity' in this context is synonymous with multiplicity, and that the argument is about each individual quiddity's essence requirement. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the supposition and the rebuttal
The supposition claims that some nonexistent things entail diversity and some unity, so the disjunction is not exhaustive. The rebuttal states that this supposition is repelled by the previous argument.
2
Understand the redefinition of 'diversity'
Al-Jurjani clarifies that 'diversity' (tabayun) here is not the opposite of unity but rather multiplicity (ta'addud). Hence the disjunction is not exhaustive in the sense of logical opposites, but that does not affect the trilemma.
The trilemma is about each quiddity individually.
3
See why the supposition fails
Even if some quiddities entail diversity and some unity, each quiddity still must be consistent. The trilemma applies per quiddity, not across all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
