Attributive Descriptions and Succession
Al-Jurjani explains that unity and multiplicity are attributive descriptions and addresses the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how attributive descriptions (wasf i'tibari) differ from real attributes and why their succession does not entail sophistry.

Core Idea
Attributive descriptions are not real attributes; their succession does not lead to absurdity.

In the ongoing discussion about the quiddity of nonexistents, an objection arises: if unity and multiplicity can succeed each other, wouldn't that allow motions and rests to succeed on the nonexistent? Al-Jurjani responds by clarifying that unity and multiplicity are attributive descriptions (wasfan i'tibariyyan), not real attributes. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It addresses the objection about the succession of unity and multiplicity.
2
Identify the key term
Notice the term 'wasfan i'tibariyyan' (attributive description). What does it mean?
Contrast it with real attributes like motion.
3
Understand the rebuttal
See why the succession of attributive descriptions does not imply the succession of real attributes.
The conclusion avoids sophistry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( جاز أن يعرض له كل واحدة منهما ) أي بالنظر إلى ذاته فيلزم جواز تعاقب الحركات والسكنات عليه بالنظر إلى ذاته، وذا باطل قطعاً، فلا يرد أن يقال : عدم اقتضاء الماهية الوحدة والكثرة في نفسها لا ينافي امتناع تعاقبها نظراً إلى أمر آخر مانع فإن مجرد قابلية المحل لا يكفي . قوله: ( مقارنة لأمور إلخ فيه بحث لا يخفى لأن السؤال لا يرد بالنظر إلى تلك الأمور المقارنة للماهية المشتركة لأن الماهية لا تقتضي شيئاً منها، وإلا انحصرت في هوية واحدة، لأمر خارج عنها قلت : هما وصفان اعتباريان، فلا يلزم من جواز تعاقبهما جواز تعاقب الصفات الموجودة حتى تلزم السفسطة المذكورة ( وإن أردت ) به ( لهوياتها فنختار تباينها وتكثرها لذواتها قولك فكل شيئين مختلفان بالذات قلنا : نعم فإن الهوية لا تعرض لها كثرة ) ولا فهو يتصور فيها شركة بل كل هويتين فهما مختلفتان بالذات، والحقيقة الشخصية ) وبالجملة إن ( أي ما ذكرتم في العدم ) وارد عليكم في الوجود ) فإن ماهية السواد من حيث هي اقتضت الاتحاد، انحصرت في شخص، وإن اقتضت التباين كان كل سوادين متباينين بالذات وإن لم تقتض شيئا منهما كانت مورداً للمتزايلات، مع أنها من حيث هي ليست موجودة فإن قلت : لا استحالة في جواز تعاقب الصفات الاعتبارية عليها في زمان كونها موجودة، قلت : قد عرفت أنه لا لا قوله : قلت : هما وصفان إلخ إنما لم يجب المصنف بهذا الجواب لأنه خلاف الواقع إذ يصح القول بأنه المعدومات التي هي هويات شخصية مورد للوحدة والكثرة . قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ) ما مر كان نقضاً تفصيلياً، وهذا نقض إجمالي والتعبير عن النقض الإجمالي بلفظ بالجملة شائع في كلامهم وليس معناه مجمل الكلام السابق وخلاصته كما وهم، فاعترض بأنه غير واقع موقعه والفاء في فهو زائدة ومدخولها أعني مجموع المبتدأ والخبر وهو قوله : فهو وارد عليكم مبتدأ بتأويل هذا خبره بالجملة أو خبر له على أن الباء زائدة . قوله : ( وإن اقتضت التباين أي الاختلاف والتكثر بأمور متباينة . قوله : ( كانت ) أي الطبيعة من حيث هي مورداً للمتزايلات بالنظر إلى ذاتها، مع أن مورد المتزايلات لا يكون إلا الموجود كيلا يلزم السفسطة . قوله : ( لا استحالة إلخ) يعني أن الماهية من حيث هي عبارة عن الماهية لا بشرط شيء، وهو لا ينافي الوجود، فيجوز كونها مورداً للمتزايلات في زمان وجودها، إنما الاستحالة في أن تكون الماهية من حيث هي بمعنى الماهية بشرط الإطلاق والتجرد مورداً لها لأنها لا تكون موجودة . قوله : قلت : قد عرفت إلخ لا يخفى أن مقصود السائل أن المذكور في الاستدلال لزوم كون المعدوم حال العدم مورداً للمتزايلات، وهو غير لازم في صورة النقض فلا نقض، وهذا الجواب لا يدفعه إذ حاصله وإن ما أوردتم على النقض وارد على الاستدلال المذكور أيضاً . فيجوز بالنظر إلى نفس الماهية تعاقب تلك الأمور عليها، مع أنهم اتفقوا على عدم جوازه، فإن قلت : يحتمل أن تكون الماهية من قبيل الأحوال قلت: لزوم السفسطة ليست باعتبار لزوم تجویز قيام الحركات بالمعدوم، بل بما ليس بموجود فعلى تقدير تسليم حاليتها لزومها بحاله، والوجه أن يقال : الاتفاق في الصفات الثابتة، فيجوز أن يدعي عدم ثبوت تلك الأمور المقارنة للماهية المشتركة كما ادعى عدم ثبوت الوحدة والكثرة ولا بعد في ذلك فإن التشخص لما ميز الموجود الخارجي مع أنه اعتباري عندنا فلان ميز الصفة المنفية الغير الثابتة للمعدوم الثابت أولى .
His statement: (it is possible for each of them to occur to it) i.e., with respect to its essence, so the possibility of the succession of motions and rests upon it with respect to its essence follows, and that is absolutely false. So it cannot be said that the quiddity not entailing unity and multiplicity in itself does not contradict the impossibility of their succession due to another preventing factor, for mere receptivity of the locus is not sufficient. His statement: (association with matters etc.) There is a discussion in it that is not hidden, because the question does not arise with respect to those matters associated with the common quiddity, for the quiddity does not entail any of them; otherwise it would be restricted to a single individual identity. Due to an external cause, I said: they are two attributive descriptions (wasfan i'tibariyyan), so from the permissibility of their succession does not follow the permissibility of the succession of existing attributes such that the mentioned sophistry would follow. (And if you mean) thereby (their individual identities, then we choose their diversity and multiplicity by their essences. Your statement: then every two things are essentially different. We say: yes, for the individual identity does not admit multiplicity nor does it admit sharing, but every two individual identities are essentially different, and the personal reality (al-haqiqa al-shakhsiyya). (And in summary, indeed) what you mentioned about nonexistence applies to you regarding existence. For the quiddity of blackness as such: if it entailed unity, it would be restricted to a person; if it entailed diversity, then every two blacknesses would be essentially different; and if it entailed neither of them, it would be a locus for successive accidents, even though as such it is not existent. For if you say: there is no impossibility in the succession of attributive descriptions upon it at the time of its existence. I said: you have known that it is not... His statement: I said: they are two attributive descriptions etc. The author did not answer with this reply because it contradicts reality, for it is correct to say that the nonexistent things which are personal individual identities are a locus for unity and multiplicity. His statement: (And in summary etc.) What passed was a detailed counterargument, and this is a summary counterargument, and the expression of the summary counterargument by the word 'bil-jumla' is common in their speech; its meaning is not the summary of the previous speech and its conclusion as was mistakenly thought. It was objected that it is not in its proper place, and the 'fa' in 'fahuwa' is redundant, and its object (i.e., the combination of the subject and predicate) which is his statement 'fahuwa waridun 'alaykum' is a subject with the interpretation 'this is its predicate in summary', or a predicate with the 'ba' redundant. His statement: (and if it entails diversity) i.e., difference and multiplicity by diverse matters. His statement: (it would be) i.e., the nature as such would be a locus for successive accidents with respect to its essence, while the locus of successive accidents is only existent so that sophistry does not follow. His statement: (no impossibility etc.) means that the quiddity as such is the expression for the quiddity without condition (la bi-shart shay'), and that does not contradict existence; thus it is permissible for it to be a locus for successive accidents during the time of its existence. The impossibility is only that the quiddity as such, meaning the quiddity with the condition of absoluteness and abstraction (bi-shart al-itlaq wa al-tajarrud), be a locus for them because it would not be existent. His statement: I said: you have known etc. It is not hidden that the intention of the questioner is that what is mentioned in the argument is the necessity of the nonexistent during nonexistence being a locus for successive accidents, which is not necessary in the form of the counterargument, so there is no counterargument. And this answer does not repel it, for its sum is that what you brought against the counterargument applies also to the mentioned argument. So it is permissible, with respect to the quiddity itself, the succession of those matters upon it, while they agreed on its impossibility. For if you say: it is possible that the quiddity is of the type of states (ahwal). I said: the necessity of sophistry is not due to the necessity of permitting motions to subsist in the nonexistent, but rather in what is not existent. So on the assumption of its state-ness, its necessity remains. And the correct way is to say: the agreement is regarding established attributes, so it is permissible to claim the non-establishment of those matters associated with the common quiddity just as the non-establishment of unity and multiplicity was claimed. There is no improbability in that, for individualization (tashakhkhus) distinguished the external existent even though it is relational (i'tibari) with us, so it is more appropriate that it distinguishes the denied attribute that is not established for the established nonexistent.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the final counterargument (al-naqd al-ijmali). The commentator first explains a previous point: the possibility of unity and multiplicity occurring to the nonexistent is with respect to its essence, but that would permit the succession of motions and rests, which is absurd. He then addresses the objection that the quiddity's association with external matters could prevent succession, but replies that the quiddity does not entail those matters. He then clarifies that unity and multiplicity are attributive descriptions (wasfan i'tibariyyan), so their succession does not imply sophistry. He then presents the summary counterargument: the same reasoning used for nonexistents applies to existents – if the quiddity of, say, blackness entails unity, it would be one individual; if diversity, then every black differs essentially; if neither, it would be a locus for successive accidents even though it is not existent as such. The objector might say that there is no impossibility in that, but the reply is that the quiddity as such (with no condition) does not preclude existence. The commentator then clarifies the terminology: 'bil-jumla' indicates a summary counterargument, not a summary of previous speech. He further explains that the locus for successive accidents must be existent, and the impossibility is only for the quiddity under the condition of abstraction. Finally, he suggests that since individualization distinguishes the external existent even though it is relational, it is even more appropriate that it distinguishes a denied attribute for the established nonexistent, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الوصف الاعتباري (attributive description)
الماهية (quiddity)
المحل (locus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributive descriptions are real attributes that inhere in the nonexistent.
Correction: Attributive descriptions are relational concepts, not real accidents; they do not require a locus in the same way.

Checkpoint
Why does the succession of attributive descriptions not lead to sophistry?
A) Because attributive descriptions are not real attributes.
B) Because the nonexistent cannot have attributes.
C) Because unity and multiplicity are not attributes.
D) Because the quiddity is not a locus.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are attributive descriptions, not real attributes; their succession does not imply the succession of real accidents, thus avoiding sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about abstract concepts like 'unity' or 'multiplicity', do you ever treat them as if they were concrete realities? How might this mislead your understanding of God's attributes, and what can you do to avoid this mistake?
?
Have you ever used a conceptual distinction—like calling something 'just an idea'—to avoid confronting a difficult truth about yourself or your beliefs? Reflect on a specific instance and commit to a next step.

The Summary Counterargument and Its Resolution
Al-Jurjani presents and resolves the summary counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the summary counterargument (al-naqd al-ijmali) that the reasoning about nonexistents applies to existents, and how al-Jurjani resolves it.

Core Idea
The same reasoning used for nonexistents applies to existents, but the quiddity as such avoids the problem.

Having detailed the objections, al-Jurjani now presents a final summary counterargument: if the reasoning works for nonexistents, it also works for existents, leading to the same absurdity. He then provides the resolution by distinguishing between the quiddity as such and the quiddity under a condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary counterargument
The passage presents the argument: the quiddity of blackness, if it entails unity or diversity, leads to problems; if neither, it is a locus for succession even when not existent.
2
Understand the resolution
Al-Jurjani clarifies that the quiddity as such (la bi-shart shay') does not preclude existence, so it can be a locus for succession when existent.
3
Note the terminology
The term 'bil-jumla' indicates a summary counterargument, not a summary of previous speech.
This is a common misunderstanding.

His statement: (it is possible for each of them to occur to it) i.e., with respect to its essence, so the possibility of the succession of motions and rests upon it with respect to its essence follows, and that is absolutely false. 5 min
First Relied-Upon Proof: Non-Existent as Thing Negates Divine Power
The author states the first relied-upon proof that the non-existent is not a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first relied-upon proof that the non-existent is not a thing and how it uses a dilemmatic argument to negate divine power.

Core Idea
The non-existent cannot be a fixed entity because that would make it eternal and beyond God's power.

This passage presents the first of two relied-upon proofs for the position that the non-existent is not a thing. It is a dilemmatic (ilzami) argument that forces the opponent into a contradiction. Let's see how the proof works step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the core proof statement
Read the Arabic sentence carefully. It states that claiming existence (thubut) for the non-existent negates possibility (maqduriyya) because essences would be fixed and eternal, so power does not attach to them.
Notice the claim: if non-existent is a thing, it is eternal; eternal things cannot be objects of power.
2
Understand the key concepts
Grasp the terms: 'eternal' (azali) means having no beginning and not needing a cause; 'possible' (maqdur) means being an object of power. The proof links them: what is eternal cannot be possible.
Eternal entities are self-sufficient and independent of a creator.
3
Explore the dilemmatic structure
The proof is a dilemma (ilzami). It forces the opponent to admit either that existence is a state (hal) or not. In both cases, claiming the non-existent is fixed leads to absurdity.
The commentator explains the two horns: for the negator, it implies power over the state; for the affirmer, it implies power over the state while denying attachment to states.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
This proof supports the Ash'ari position that the non-existent is not a thing, meaning only existent entities are real and can be objects of divine power.
It refutes the view that non-existents have a fixed essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد في إثبات هذا المطلب: وجهان: الأول أن القول بثبوت المعدوم في حال العدم ينفي المقدورية لأن الذوات ثابتة أزلية فلا تتعلق القدرة.
قوله: (إن القول إلخ) هذا الدليل إلزامي مركب من مقدمات محققة، تقدير ثبوتها أزلية وأن الأزلية تنافي المقدورية وأن الوجود حال ومقدمة للنافي، وهي الحال، ومقدمة للمثبت وهي عدم تعلق القدرة بالأحوال، وتقرير الدليل أنه لو كانت الذات ثابتة لزم نفي المقدورية، إذ لو تحقق المقدور يلزم على النافي القول بتأثير القدرة في الحال، وعلى المثبت القول بتأثير القدرة في الحال مع عدم تعلق القدرة بها، وكلا الأمرين باطلان، فما قيل: إنه لا مجال للتحقيق بل هو إلزامي، ولا إلزام أيضاً لأنه إما أن يعترف الخصم بأن الوجود حال أو لا، وعلى الأول لا يصح قوله مع أنه لا حال عندكم، وعلى الثاني لا يصح قوله: لكان ذلك التأثير في الحال ليس بشيء.
قوله: (فلا تتعلق القدرة إلخ) لأن الأزلية تنافي المقدورية لأنها إذا كانت ثابتة في أنفسها، فلا تحتاج إلى علة فضلاً عن كونها مقدورة بخلاف ما إذا لم تكن ثابتة، فإن القدرة تتعلق بأنفسها بمعنى أن ذواتها أثر القادر كما هو مذهب الأشعري.
And the relied-upon in establishing this claim: two aspects: the first: that the statement of the existence (thubut) of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) in the state of non-existence negates possibility (al-maqduriyya), because the essences (al-dhawat) are fixed (thabita) eternal (azaliyya), so power (al-qudra) does not attach to them.
His saying: (the statement etc.) This proof is a dilemmatic proof (ilzami) composed of verified premises: the supposition of their being fixed (thubut) is eternal (azaliyya), and that eternality contradicts possibility, and that existence (al-wujud) is a state (hal) and a premise for the negator, and it is the state, and a premise for the affirmer, and it is the non-attachment of power to states (al-ahwal). And the explanation of the proof: if the essence (al-dhat) were fixed, then the negation of possibility would follow, for if the possible (al-maqdur) is realized, it would necessitate for the negator the statement of the effect of power in the state, and for the affirmer the statement of the effect of power in the state while there is no attachment of power to it. Both matters are false. So what is said: that there is no room for verification but rather it is ilzami is not an ilzam either, because either the opponent admits that existence is a state or not. On the first, his statement is not valid since there is no state according to you; on the second, his statement is not valid: that effect in the state would not be something.
His saying: (so power does not attach etc.) Because eternality contradicts possibility, because if they are fixed in themselves, they do not need a cause (illa), let alone being possible (maqdur). Unlike if they were not fixed, for then power attaches to themselves, meaning that their essences are the effect of the Powerful (al-qadir) as is the school of al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the first relied-upon proof (al-wajh al-awwal al-mu'tamad) for the position that the non-existent is not a thing (thubut al-ma'dum). It is a dilemmatic proof (dalil ilzami) that forces the opponent into a contradiction. The core argument: If the non-existent were a fixed entity (thabita), it would be eternal (azaliyya). But what is eternal cannot be subject to divine power (al-qudra) because it does not require a cause. However, the non-existent is said to be brought into existence by God, so it must be possible (maqdur). Thus, the claim that the non-existent is a thing leads to the absurdity that God cannot create it. The commentator explains the proof in detail: The proof operates on two possible admissions by the opponent. First, if the opponent admits that existence is a state (hal) distinct from the essence, then saying the non-existent is fixed would require power to affect the state, but the opponent denies that power attaches to states. Second, if the opponent does not admit existence as a state, then saying the non-existent is fixed would still require an effect in the state, which is meaningless. In either case, the denial of possibility follows. The commentator also clarifies that eternality negates possibility: what is eternal needs no cause, so it cannot be an object of power. This aligns with the Ash'ari school, which holds that power attaches to the essences themselves as the effect of the Powerful.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الثبوت
المقدورية
الأزلية
الحال
القدرة
الذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof is circular, assuming what it wants to prove.
Correction: The proof is not circular but a dilemmatic (ilzami) argument: it forces the opponent to accept a contradiction regardless of their position on the status of existence, thus demonstrating that the opponent's view is untenable.

Checkpoint
According to the first relied-upon proof, why does asserting the existence (thubut) of the non-existent negate divine power (al-maqduriyya)?
A) Because the non-existent is not a thing at all.
B) Because if the non-existent were fixed, it would be eternal and not need a cause, so power could not affect it.
C) Because divine power only affects existent things.
D) Because the non-existent has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The first relied-upon proof shows that claiming the non-existent is a fixed entity leads to the absurdity that it is eternal and thus not subject to God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the non-existent were a fixed entity, what would that mean for your own reliance on God as the Creator of everything? How does this truth shape your daily trust in His power?
?
Consider something you desperately want God to bring into your life. Based on this lesson, what assurance do you have that He is able to create it? Are you willing to wait on His timing, knowing that He alone brings things from non-existence into existence?
?
The proof shows that claiming the non-existent has fixed existence limits God's power. When you think about your own sins, do you treat them as 'fixed' and unchangeable? Can you truly believe God can remove them and create something new in you? If not, what does that say about your understanding of His power?

The Second Argument: If the Possible Non-Existent Were Affirmed
The author presents the second argument assuming the possible non-existent is affirmed, leading to a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that assuming the possible non-existent is a thing leads to the conclusion that the negated (manfī) is affirmed, which is contradictory.

Core Idea
If the possible non-existent is a thing, then the concept of 'non-existent' entails that the negated is affirmed.

This lesson presents the core of the second argument in the debate over whether the non-existent (ma'dūm) is a thing (shay'). The author constructs a conditional argument that forces a contradiction if one accepts the Mu'tazila's position. We will follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the core passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...' followed by implications.
2
Identify the assumption
The argument begins with the assumption that the possible non-existent is affirmed (thābit). Identify what that means in context.
The possible non-existent is the ma'dūm mumkin, which the Mu'tazila consider a thing.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow how the author deduces that the absolute non-existent is more general than the negated, then that the concept of non-existent is distinct from that of negated, and then that the concept is affirmed.
The key move is that distinctness (tamayyuz) implies affirmation (thubūt) according to the Mu'tazila.
4
Conclude the contradiction
Understand how the argument ends with the conclusion that the negated itself would be affirmed, which is contradictory to its meaning.
This forces the Mu'tazila to reject the original assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني لو كان) المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً) كان المعدوم المطلق (أعم) مطلقاً ( من المنفي) لشموله الثابت والمنفي معاً ( فيكون ) مفهوم المعدوم مطلقاً متميزاً عنه) أي عن مفهوم المنفي ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن متميزاً عنه ( لكان) المفهوم ( العام عين) المفهوم الخاص) وهو محال (فيكون ) مفهوم المعدوم أمراً ( ثابتاً لأن كل متميز عن غيره ) ثابت عندكم وإنه ) يعني مفهوم المعدوم ( صادق على المنفي) أي على ما صدق عليه المنفي (و) كل ما يصدق عليه صفة ثبوتية فهو ثابت فالمنفي ثابت هذا ،خلف وما يقال : ( من ( أن المعدوم الممكن ثابت عندهم ( لا) كل معدوم فيصدق ) حينئذ ( بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم من صدقه أي صدق المعدوم على المنفي ثبوته إذ يصير الاستدلال هكذا) المنفي معدوم وبعض المعدوم ثابت وأنه لا ينتج لكون الكبرى في الشكل الأول جزئية، فإنه بمعزل مما قدمناه من التحرير، وإنما خذلهم ذلك القول أنهم لم يحوموا على المراد، ولم يتفطنوا لأن قصدهم) أي قصد المستدلين بالوجه الثاني (الإلزام ) أي إلزام المعتزلة بما هم معترفون به من أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت وتوضيحه أن تحرير المصنف متعلق بمفهوم المعدوم، وأنه على تقدير كونه أعم من مفهوم المنفي يلزم أن يكون متميزاً عنه، فيكون أمراً ثابتاً، فيلزم أن يكون ما صدق عليه المنفي ثابتاً لاتصافه بأمر ثبوتي هو مفهوم المعدوم، وحينئذ لا يتجه عليه أصلاً ما قالوه : من أن الكبرى في الشكل الأول جزئية، وهناك تقرير آخر متعلق بما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم، وهو أن يقال : على تقدير كونه أعم من المنفي لا يكون ما صدق عليه المعدوم نفياً محضاً، وإلا لم يكن بينهما فرق وإذا لم يكن نفياً محضاً، كان ثابتاً فيصدق المنفي معدوم والمعدوم ثابت فيرد عليه أنه ليس جميع ما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم نفياً محضاً، بل بعضه نفي محض هو المعدوم الممتنع وبعضه ثابت هو المعدوم الممكن، وحينئذ تصير الكبرى في ذلك القياس جزئية، واعلم أن الأظهر على تحرير المصنف أن يقال : على تقدير كونه أعم من المنفي يكون ما صدق عليه المعدوم نفياً محضاً وإلا لم يكن بينهما فرق لكن الملازمة ممنوعة، ثم ذكر أن الأظهر أن يقال: لو كان المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً كان المنفي متميزاً عنه إلى آخره. ثم ذكر: وأعلم أن الأظهر إلخ وجه الأظهرية أن صدق مفهوم المنفي على أفراده اللازم على هذا التقدير أظهر من صدق مفهوم المعدوم الذي هو أعم من مفهوم المنفي على أفراد المنفي اللازم على التقدير الأول .
The second point: If the possible non-existent were affirmed, then the absolute non-existent would be more general absolutely than the negated, because it encompasses both the affirmed and the negated together. Then the concept of absolute non-existent would be distinct from it, i.e., from the concept of negated. Otherwise, i.e., if it were not distinct from it, then the general concept would be identical to the particular concept, which is impossible. Then the concept of non-existent would be an affirmed thing, because everything distinct from another is affirmed according to you. And it, i.e., the concept of non-existent, is true of the negated, i.e., of what the negated is true of, and everything of which an affirmative attribute is true is itself affirmed. So the negated would be affirmed. This is a contradiction. And what is said: that the possible non-existent is affirmed according to them, not every non-existent, so then some non-existent is affirmed, so the truth of the non-existent about the negated does not necessitate its affirmation, because the inference becomes: the negated is non-existent, and some non-existent is affirmed, and this does not yield a conclusion because the major premise in the first figure is particular. But this is far from our previous formulation. Rather, that statement confused them because they did not aim at the intended meaning and did not perceive that their purpose, i.e., the purpose of those who argue by the second point, is to compel (ilzām) the Mu'tazila by what they themselves acknowledge: that distinctness requires affirmation. The clarification is that the author's formulation pertains to the concept of non-existent, and that on the assumption that it is more general than the concept of negated, it follows that it is distinct from it, so it is an affirmed thing. Then it follows that what the negated is true of is affirmed because it is qualified by an affirmative thing, which is the concept of non-existent. And thus what they said about the major premise in the first figure being particular does not apply to it at all. There is another formulation pertaining to what the concept of non-existent is true of, which is to say: on the assumption that it is more general than the negated, what the non-existent is true of would not be pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between them. And if it is not pure negation, it is affirmed. Then it is true that the negated is non-existent and the non-existent is affirmed. But the objection is that not everything of which the concept of non-existent is true is pure negation; rather, some of it is pure negation, namely the impossible non-existent, and some is affirmed, namely the possible non-existent. Thus the major premise in that syllogism becomes particular. Know that the clearer view according to the author's formulation is to say: on the assumption that it is more general than the negated, what the non-existent is true of is pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between them, but the implication is denied. Then he mentioned that the clearer view is to say: If the possible non-existent were affirmed, then the negated would be distinct from it, etc. Then he mentioned: 'Know that the clearer view etc.' The reason for its being clearer is that the truth of the concept of negated about its individuals necessary on this formulation is more apparent than the truth of the concept of non-existent (which is more general than the concept of negated) about the individuals of the negated necessary on the first formulation.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in the debate over whether the non-existent (ma'dūm) is a thing (shay'). The author constructs a conditional argument: if the possible non-existent (ma'dūm mumkin) were affirmed (thābit) in some sense, then the concept of absolute non-existent would be more general than the concept of negated (manfī), because it would encompass both the affirmed possible non-existent and the negated impossible. This generality implies that the two concepts are distinct. According to the Mu'tazila's own principle, anything distinct from another is affirmed. Therefore, the concept of non-existent would be affirmed. Since this concept applies to the negated, the negated itself would be affirmed (because it has an affirmative attribute), which contradicts its very definition as negated. The objection that the syllogism's major premise is particular (since 'some non-existent is affirmed' does not entail that the negated is affirmed) is refuted by clarifying that the argument is about the concept (mafhūm) of non-existent, not about its instances. The author explains that the purpose is to compel (ilzām) the Mu'tazila by their own admission that distinctness entails affirmation. A second formulation is also given: if the concept of non-existent is more general, then its instances would not be pure negation, otherwise they would be identical to the negated. But this formulation is vulnerable because the instances are not all pure negation. The author then notes that the clearer approach is to say that if the possible non-existent were affirmed, then the negated would be distinct from it, leading directly to the conclusion without the problematic major premise.

Key Terms
معدوم
منفي
ثبوت
تميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what conclusion follows if the possible non-existent is affirmed?
A) The negated would be affirmed, which is contradictory.
B) The possible non-existent would become impossible.
C) The absolute non-existent would be negated.
D) The concept of non-existent would be unaffirmed.

Key Takeaway
The assumption that the possible non-existent is a thing forces the conclusion that the negated is affirmed, which is impossible; thus the assumption must be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that Allah knows all things, past, present, and future. But do you treat the future—which does not yet exist—as truly 'real' in your mind? When you plan tomorrow, do you rely on it as if it were already settled, forgetting that only Allah's knowledge is certain? How does this argument challenge you to hold your future with humility, knowing that the non-existent is not a thing except in Allah's knowledge?
?
The argument shows that affirming a contradiction forces you to abandon your position. Are there beliefs you hold—perhaps about your own abilities, your past, or your relationship with Allah—that, if you followed their logical implications, would lead to a contradiction? Are you willing to trace those beliefs to their end and submit to the truth, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The Mu'tazila built their theology on rational principles. In your own life, do you ever rely on your reasoning to override clear revelation? When you encounter a logical argument that seems to conflict with what you know from the Qur'an and Sunnah, what is your first move: to adjust your reasoning, or to question the revelation? Ask yourself: are you willing to let the truth of revelation correct your logic, even at the cost of your own intellectual pride?

Responding to the Objection: The Argument is About the Concept
The author refutes the objection that the major premise is particular by clarifying the argument's focus on the concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that the syllogism's major premise is particular fails, because the argument concerns the concept of non-existent, not its instances.

Core Idea
The objection misinterprets the argument; the argument compels the Mu'tazila by their own principle that distinctness requires affirmation.

After presenting the second argument, the author anticipates an objection: the inference would be invalid because the major premise becomes particular. In this lesson, we see how the author refutes this objection by clarifying that the argument is about the concept (mafhūm) of non-existent, not about its individual instances.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. The opponent says that the major premise 'every non-existent is affirmed' is not universal; rather 'some non-existent is affirmed', so the syllogism is invalid.
2
Understand the response
The author responds that the argument is not about the instances (what the concept is true of) but about the concept itself. The universal claim is about the concept of non-existent, not about its individual referents.
3
Grasp the principle of ilzām
The purpose is to compel (ilzām) the Mu'tazila by their own acknowledgment that distinctness (tamayyuz) implies affirmation. Since the concept of non-existent is distinct from that of negated, it is affirmed.
This shows that the objection misses the point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a logical argument in your study of 'aqīdah because you thought you saw a flaw, only to later realize you had misunderstood the arguer's intention? What does that reveal about your need for intellectual humility?
?
The objectors failed to 'aim at the intended meaning' and instead imposed their own interpretation. In your own life, where are you most prone to misreading what others say because you are too quick to judge or too attached to your own perspective?
?
The author's argument only compels because it uses the Mu'tazila's own principle. Is there an aspect of your belief that you hold inconsistently? Where might you be refuted by your own standards?

Alternative Formulations and the Clearest Presentation
The author discusses an alternative formulation and then presents the clearest version of the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative formulation of the argument and why the author holds that the clearest approach leads directly to the contradiction without the problematic major premise.

Core Idea
The clearest formulation shows that if the possible non-existent is affirmed, the negated itself is distinct, entailing its affirmation directly.

The author presents an alternative way of framing the second argument, which also faces an objection. He then presents what he considers the clearest version, which avoids the difficulties. In this lesson, we trace these formulations and understand the author's final preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative formulation
Read the alternative formulation carefully. It focuses on what the concept of non-existent is true of (its instances). It argues that if the concept is more general, its instances cannot be pure negation, so they are affirmed.
2
Identify the problem
Note the objection: not all instances of non-existent are affirmed; the impossible non-existent is pure negation, so the major premise becomes particular again.
3
Read the clearer formulation
Read the formulation the author considers clearer: it directly states that if the possible non-existent is affirmed, then the negated is distinct from it, making the negated affirmed.
This avoids the need to talk about the concept's generality or instances.
4
Understand why it is clearer
The author explains that this formulation is clearer because the truth of the concept of negated about its individuals is more apparent than the truth of the concept of non-existent about the individuals of the negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني لو كان) المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً) كان المعدوم المطلق (أعم) مطلقاً ( من المنفي) لشموله الثابت والمنفي معاً ( فيكون ) مفهوم المعدوم مطلقاً متميزاً عنه) أي عن مفهوم المنفي ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن متميزاً عنه ( لكان) المفهوم ( العام عين) المفهوم الخاص) وهو محال (فيكون ) مفهوم المعدوم أمراً ( ثابتاً لأن كل متميز عن غيره ) ثابت عندكم وإنه ) يعني مفهوم المعدوم ( صادق على المنفي) أي على ما صدق عليه المنفي (و) كل ما يصدق عليه صفة ثبوتية فهو ثابت فالمنفي ثابت هذا ،خلف وما يقال : ( من ( أن المعدوم الممكن ثابت عندهم ( لا) كل معدوم فيصدق ) حينئذ ( بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم من صدقه أي صدق المعدوم على المنفي ثبوته إذ يصير الاستدلال هكذا) المنفي معدوم وبعض المعدوم ثابت وأنه لا ينتج لكون الكبرى في الشكل الأول جزئية، فإنه بمعزل مما قدمناه من التحرير، وإنما خذلهم ذلك القول أنهم لم يحوموا على المراد، ولم يتفطنوا لأن قصدهم) أي قصد المستدلين بالوجه الثاني (الإلزام ) أي إلزام المعتزلة بما هم معترفون به من أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت وتوضيحه أن تحرير المصنف متعلق بمفهوم المعدوم، وأنه على تقدير كونه أعم من مفهوم المنفي يلزم أن يكون متميزاً عنه، فيكون أمراً ثابتاً، فيلزم أن يكون ما صدق عليه المنفي ثابتاً لاتصافه بأمر ثبوتي هو مفهوم المعدوم، وحينئذ لا يتجه عليه أصلاً ما قالوه : من أن الكبرى في الشكل الأول جزئية، وهناك تقرير آخر متعلق بما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم، وهو أن يقال : على تقدير كونه أعم من المنفي لا يكون ما صدق عليه المعدوم نفياً محضاً، وإلا لم يكن بينهما فرق وإذا لم يكن نفياً محضاً، كان ثابتاً فيصدق المنفي معدوم والمعدوم ثابت فيرد عليه أنه ليس جميع ما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم نفياً محضاً، بل بعضه نفي محض هو المعدوم الممتنع وبعضه ثابت هو المعدوم الممكن، وحينئذ تصير الكبرى في ذلك القياس جزئية، واعلم أن الأظهر على تحرير المصنف أن يقال : على تقدير كونه أعم من المنفي يكون ما صدق عليه المعدوم نفياً محضاً وإلا لم يكن بينهما فرق لكن الملازمة ممنوعة، ثم ذكر أن الأظهر أن يقال: لو كان المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً كان المنفي متميزاً عنه إلى آخره. ثم ذكر: وأعلم أن الأظهر إلخ وجه الأظهرية أن صدق مفهوم المنفي على أفراده اللازم على هذا التقدير أظهر من صدق مفهوم المعدوم الذي هو أعم من مفهوم المنفي على أفراد المنفي اللازم على التقدير الأول .
The second point: If the possible non-existent were affirmed, then the absolute non-existent would be more general absolutely than the negated, because it encompasses both the affirmed and the negated together. Then the concept of absolute non-existent would be distinct from it, i.e., from the concept of negated. Otherwise, i.e., if it were not distinct from it, then the general concept would be identical to the particular concept, which is impossible. Then the concept of non-existent would be an affirmed thing, because everything distinct from another is affirmed according to you. And it, i.e., the concept of non-existent, is true of the negated, i.e., of what the negated is true of, and everything of which an affirmative attribute is true is itself affirmed. So the negated would be affirmed. This is a contradiction. And what is said: that the possible non-existent is affirmed according to them, not every non-existent, so then some non-existent is affirmed, so the truth of the non-existent about the negated does not necessitate its affirmation, because the inference becomes: the negated is non-existent, and some non-existent is affirmed, and this does not yield a conclusion because the major premise in the first figure is particular. But this is far from our previous formulation. Rather, that statement confused them because they did not aim at the intended meaning and did not perceive that their purpose, i.e., the purpose of those who argue by the second point, is to compel (ilzām) the Mu'tazila by what they themselves acknowledge: that distinctness requires affirmation. The clarification is that the author's formulation pertains to the concept of non-existent, and that on the assumption that it is more general than the concept of negated, it follows that it is distinct from it, so it is an affirmed thing. Then it follows that what the negated is true of is affirmed because it is qualified by an affirmative thing, which is the concept of non-existent. And thus what they said about the major premise in the first figure being particular does not apply to it at all. There is another formulation pertaining to what the concept of non-existent is true of, which is to say: on the assumption that it is more general than the negated, what the non-existent is true of would not be pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between them. And if it is not pure negation, it is affirmed. Then it is true that the negated is non-existent and the non-existent is affirmed. But the objection is that not everything of which the concept of non-existent is true is pure negation; rather, some of it is pure negation, namely the impossible non-existent, and some is affirmed, namely the possible non-existent. Thus the major premise in that syllogism becomes particular. Know that the clearer view according to the author's formulation is to say: on the assumption that it is more general than the negated, what the non-existent is true of is pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between them, but the implication is denied. Then he mentioned that the clearer view is to say: If the possible non-existent were affirmed, then the negated would be distinct from it, etc. Then he mentioned: 'Know that the clearer view etc.' The reason for its being clearer is that the truth of the concept of negated about its individuals necessary on this formulation is more apparent than the truth of the concept of non-existent (which is more general than the concept of negated) about the individuals of the negated necessary on the first formulation.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in the debate over whether the non-existent (ma'dūm) is a thing (shay'). The author constructs a conditional argument: if the possible non-existent (ma'dūm mumkin) were affirmed (thābit) in some sense, then the concept of absolute non-existent would be more general than the concept of negated (manfī), because it would encompass both the affirmed possible non-existent and the negated impossible. This generality implies that the two concepts are distinct. According to the Mu'tazila's own principle, anything distinct from another is affirmed. Therefore, the concept of non-existent would be affirmed. Since this concept applies to the negated, the negated itself would be affirmed (because it has an affirmative attribute), which contradicts its very definition as negated. The objection that the syllogism's major premise is particular (since 'some non-existent is affirmed' does not entail that the negated is affirmed) is refuted by clarifying that the argument is about the concept (mafhūm) of non-existent, not about its instances. The author explains that the purpose is to compel (ilzām) the Mu'tazila by their own admission that distinctness entails affirmation. A second formulation is also given: if the concept of non-existent is more general, then its instances would not be pure negation, otherwise they would be identical to the negated. But this formulation is vulnerable because the instances are not all pure negation. The author then notes that the clearer approach is to say that if the possible non-existent were affirmed, then the negated would be distinct from it, leading directly to the conclusion without the problematic major premise.

Key Terms
تقرير
أظهر
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the clearer formulation (al-aẓhar) clearer?
A) Because it avoids the need to consider the concept of non-existent.
B) Because the truth of the concept of negated about its individuals is more apparent.
C) Because it uses a different logical figure.
D) Because it relies on the Mu'tazila's rejection of distinctness.

Key Takeaway
The clearest formulation directly links the affirmation of the possible non-existent to the distinctness of the negated, leading to the contradiction without the problematic generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm a possibility about yourself—like the ability to change a bad habit—do you treat that possibility as real or as mere concept? How does this lesson's distinction between affirmed and pure negation expose the difference between wishful thinking and genuine reliance on Allah?
?
The objection shows that not all non-existents are pure negation; some are possible. In your spiritual life, what do you treat as impossible (pure negation) that Allah says is possible? Are you hiding behind 'I can't' when you should be seeking His help?
?
The clearer formulation forces a direct link between affirming the possible and recognizing the distinctness of the negated. In your relationship with Allah, when you affirm His attributes (e.g., His mercy), do you distinctly recognize your own need as separate from His perfection? What change would that realization demand from you today?

The Non-Existent is Distinct, Therefore Established
Sanusi presents the first proof that the non-existent is a thing via distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the ontological status of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) as a thing (shay') based on its distinctness (tamayyuz) in conception.

Core Idea
Distinctness in conception proves the non-existent has a stable reality.

Sanusi begins his argument for the reality of the non-existent with a simple but powerful logical inference: if the non-existent can be conceived distinctly from other things, then it must have some form of stability (thubut). This passage walks through that reasoning and addresses a natural objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi presents the proof in a compressed syllogistic form.
2
Unpack the main argument
Identify the two premises: (1) the non-existent is distinct, (2) every distinct thing is established. Trace how these lead to the conclusion that the non-existent is established.
The argument hinges on the link between conception (tasawwur) and distinction.
3
Understand the objection and response
Read the objection that the proof only works if all possible non-existents are conceived, and Sanusi's reply that it only requires some to be conceived, which suffices for distinction.
This shows the proof's scope: it does not require exhaustive knowledge of non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجهان : الأول المعدوم متميز وكل متميز ثابت فالمعدوم ثابت ( أما الأول فلانه أي المعدوم ) متصور ولا يمكن تصور الشيء إلا بتميزه عن غيره، وإلا لم يكن هو بكونه متصوراً أولى من الغير لا يقال : إن أرادوا أن كل معدوم ممكن متصور وإن اقتصروا على البعض لم يثبت مدعاهم لأنا نقول : لعلهم أرادوا أن بعضه متصور دون بعض، وكل منهما ممتاز عن الآخر
Two aspects: the first: the non-existent is distinct, and every distinct thing is established, so the non-existent is established. (As for the first, it is because it (i.e., the non-existent) is conceivable, and a thing cannot be conceived except by its distinction from other things; otherwise it would not be more deserving of being conceived than another. It is not said: if they meant that every possible non-existent is conceived, and they limited it to some, their claim is not established. For we say: perhaps they meant that some of it is conceived and not some, and each is distinguished from the other.)
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs for the position that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is a thing (shay') and hence has some form of stability (thubut). The argument is built on the premise that the non-existent is distinct (mutamayyiz) and whatever is distinct must be established (thabit). The author begins by stating that the non-existent is conceivable, and conceivability requires distinction from other things, otherwise no particular non-existent would be conceived rather than another. An objection is raised: if the proponent means that every possible non-existent is conceived in detail, then limiting the claim to some does not prove it. The response clarifies that only some non-existents are conceived distinctly, yet each is distinguished from the other, preserving the proof. This first proof relies on the epistemological necessity of distinction for conception.

Key Terms
معدوم
متميز
ثابت
متصور
وجهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That distinction requires actual extramental existence; that the non-existent must exist to be distinct.
Correction: The proof only requires mental discrimination through conception, not actual existence. The non-existent can be conceived and distinguished mentally from other things, which suffices for thubut (stable reality).

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must the non-existent be established (thabit)?
A) Because it is conceived through general concepts without distinction.
B) Because it is distinct and every distinct thing is established.
C) Because it is a possible thing by definition.
D) Because it is the subject of divine power.

Key Takeaway
The non-existent can be conceived distinctly, proving it has a stable reality (thubut) even before existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You conceive of many things that do not exist—future plans, past regrets, abstract ideas. Do you treat these mental realities as completely unreal, or do they shape your decisions and emotions? If they have a stable reality, how does that change the weight you give to your thoughts?
?
You often say 'that is nothing' about things that do not exist. But if the non-existent is distinct and established, then even your worst fears or highest hopes have a real conceptual existence. Are you dismissing possibilities too easily, or clinging to illusions? How will you distinguish between a worthy possibility and an empty fantasy?
?
The distinction between two non-existents is real—even if neither exists. You hold many conflicting desires in your mind. Which one will you choose to actualize? By what standard, knowing that each has its own distinct reality before existence?

Proof from Intentionality and Power
Sanusi argues that differential intentionality and possibility prove non-existents are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fact that some non-existents are intended or possible while others are not proves that non-existents are distinct from each other.

Core Idea
Selective intentionality requires distinction among non-existents.

This lesson presents the second proof that non-existents are distinct. The argument is deceptively simple: we can meaningfully say that some non-existents are intended (murad) or possible (maqdur) while others are not. But if non-existents were all undifferentiated, such selective attribution would be unintelligible. Therefore, non-existents must be distinct in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the proof's structure emerge naturally.
Notice the concise style; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the two categories
The proof mentions two ways non-existents can be characterized: intended (murad) and possible (maqdur). Find these in the text and note that the contrast is between 'some' and 'others'.
3
Understand the conditional logic
The phrase 'were it not for distinction... would not be understood' is a conditional. Reflect on why distinction is necessary for the attribution of intentionality and possibility to make sense.
Think about what would happen if all non-existents were identical in their non-existence.
4
Address the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that this proof assumes an actual agent. The commentary clarifies that it only points to conceptual intelligibility, not actual agency.
The proof works even without assuming God's existence at this stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإن بعضه مراد دون بعض وبعضه مقدور دون بعض ولولا التميز بين المعدومات لما عقل ذلك أي اتصاف بعضها بالمرادية أو المقدورية دون بعض
And also, some of it is intended and not some, and some of it is possible and not some; were it not for distinction among non-existents, that would not be understood, i.e., the characterisation of some of them with intentionality or possibility without others.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof that non-existents are distinct. The argument is based on the observation that some non-existents are intended (murad) by an agent while others are not, and some are within power (maqdur) while others are not. If all non-existents were undifferentiated, there would be no basis for such selectivity. Therefore, non-existents must be distinct from each other in themselves, which implies that they have some reality (thubut) that grounds these distinctions. The proof is concise and appeals to the manifest fact of differential intentionality and possibility.

Key Terms
مراد
مقدور
تميز
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument assumes that an actual agent already exists who intends or has power over non-existents, making it reliant on prior theological commitments.
Correction: The argument does not require an actual agent; it only points to the conceptual intelligibility of selective attribution. Even in abstract reasoning, we can distinguish between possible and impossible non-existents without invoking a real agent.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof use to demonstrate distinction among non-existents?
A) The fact that non-existents are conceived in the mind.
B) The fact that some non-existents are intended or possible while others are not.
C) The fact that non-existents are all equally nothing.
D) The fact that God knows all non-existents.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that differential intentionality and possibility among non-existents logically require that non-existents have distinct realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something that you intend to do but have not yet done (e.g., a good deed). According to this lesson, before you act, that intention has a distinct reality. Does this change how seriously you take your intentions? How can you ensure that the 'non-existent' acts you intend are worthy of being intended by God? Examine your heart: are your intentions pure and distinct in their goodness? Commit to one intention today that you will actualize with sincerity.
?
The passage argues that non-existents are distinct—otherwise selective intention would be unintelligible. How does this apply to your own reckoning of your sins and good deeds? Are they mere abstract nothings, or does each have a distinct reality before God? How does that affect your sense of accountability for what you have not yet done? Make a list of things you have intended but not done; reflect on their reality in God's knowledge. What does that demand of you today?
?
If all non-existents were identical, then God's will would be arbitrary. But the passage shows that distinction is necessary for rational attribution. How does that deepen your trust in God's wisdom? In what area of your life are you acting as if God's will is arbitrary? Repent and align your will with His.

Objection and Answer: Impossibles and Imaginary Things
Sanusi raises and answers the objection that impossibles are distinct yet non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction argument for thubut is defended against the objection from impossibles and imaginaries through a rebuttal that both sides agree these have no thubut.

Core Idea
Impossibles are distinct but have no thubut, so distinction alone does not prove thubut.

Sanusi presents an objection that tries to use the distinction argument against the proponents of thubut. The objector points to impossibles and imaginary things that are distinct yet non-existent. Sanusi's answer is a rebuttal (naqd) that the opponents themselves agree these have no thubut, so the objection fails. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a rebuttal.
The passage is dense; take your time to parse each part.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objector uses impossibles (like a partner of God) and imaginaries (like a mountain of ruby) as examples of things that are distinct from each other but lack existence. This is meant to challenge the claim that distinction implies thubut.
Note that the objection assumes distinction as evidence for thubut, but the rebuttal will counter this.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Sanusi's rebuttal is that the opponents themselves agree that these things have no thubut. Thus, they do not serve as counterexamples. The passage then clarifies what the proponents of thubut actually affirm: essences of substances and accidents, but not composites, existence, or composition in non-existence.
The key is that the objection misrepresents the claim of thubut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تعين له في نفسه ولا إشارة عقلاً إليه والجواب عن هذا الوجه هو النقض بما وافقونا على أنه منفي كالممتنعات فإن بعضها كشريك الباري متميز عن بعض، كاجتماع الضدين والخياليات كبحر من زئبق وجبل من ياقوت، وإنسان ذي رأسين فإن بعضها متميز عن بعض ولا ثبوت لها اتفاقاً لأنها عبارة عن جواهر متصفة بالتأليف، والألوان والأشكال المخصوصة وعندهم أن الثابت في العدم ذوات الجواهر والأعراض من غير أن تتصف الجواهر هناك بالأعراض ونفس الوجود فإنه متميز عن العدم وغيره أيضاً، ولا ثبوت له في العدم اتفاقاً وبالضرورة والتركيب فإن ماهيته متميزة عن غيرها، وليست متقررة حال العدم وفاقاً لأنها عبارة عن اجتماع الأجزاء وانضمام بعضها إلى بعض، وتماسها على وجه مخصوص، وذلك لا يتصور حال العدم
It has no determination in itself and no intellectual reference to it. The response to this aspect is the rebuttal by what they agree with us that it is negated, like the impossibles. For some of them, like a partner of God, are distinct from each other, like the conjunction of opposites, and imaginary things like a sea of mercury, a mountain of ruby, and a two-headed human; some of them are distinct from each other, yet they have no stability (thubut) by agreement, because they are composed of substances qualified by composition, colors, and specific forms. According to them, what is established in non-existence are the essences of substances and accidents, without the substances being qualified by accidents there; and existence itself is distinct from non-existence and other things, yet it has no stability in non-existence by agreement and necessarily; and composition: its quiddity is distinct from others, yet it is not established in the state of non-existence by consensus, because it is the aggregation of parts and their joining together and contact in a specific manner, and that is not conceivable in the state of non-existence.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the argument that distinction implies establishment (thubut). The objector points to impossibles (mumtani'at) and imaginary things (khayaliyat) that are distinct from each other yet have no real existence or thubut by agreement of all parties. For example, the concept of a partner of God is distinct from the concept of the conjunction of opposites, but no one affirms their reality. The answer (jawab) is a rebuttal (naqd): the opponents themselves (those who affirm thubut of non-existents) agree that these things are negated (manfi) and have no thubut—so the objection does not damage their position, because they already exclude impossibles from thubut. The passage then clarifies the position of the affirmers of thubut: what is established in non-existence are the essences (dhawat) of substances and accidents, but not imaginary composites. Existence itself, though distinct from non-existence, is also not established in non-existence. Composition, as a relation, is also not established in non-existence. Thus the objection is deflected by noting that the class of things used in the objection are not claimed to have thubut by the proponents of thubut.

Key Terms
ممتنعات
خياليات
ثبوت
جوهر
عرض
تركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection from impossibles refutes all forms of thubut for non-existents.
Correction: The objection only targets an overgeneralization; the proponents of thubut do not claim thubut for impossibles or imaginary composites, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the rebuttal (naqd) against the objection from impossibles?
A) Impossibles are actually possible in some sense.
B) The opponents of thubut agree that impossibles have no thubut, so they do not serve as counterexamples.
C) Distinction does not require any reality.
D) Imaginaries are not really distinct.

Key Takeaway
The objection from impossibles does not undermine thubut because the opponents themselves agree that impossibles have no thubut, so they are not counterexamples to the distinction argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine something impossible, like a partner to God, do you ever catch yourself giving it a kind of mental reality? How does the thubut doctrine protect you from inadvertently attributing reality to falsehoods? What can you do today to reinforce the conviction that only God has necessary existence?
?
The objector assumes that because two things are distinct they must have some reality. Have you ever made a similar mistake in your life—assuming that because you can think of something, it must be true or important? How can you train your heart to distinguish between genuine truths and mere fantasies?
?
Sanusi's answer appeals to what the opponents themselves agree upon. Are there areas in your faith where you hold inconsistent views—agreeing with a premise but denying its logical conclusion? How can you seek consistency in your aqeedah, and what is one step you can take this week to align your beliefs more perfectly with the truth?

The Impossibility of Attributes in Non-Existence
Opening argument that existence and states are absent from non-existence,

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical kalam scholars argued that non-existent entities cannot possess any attributes or states, based on the principle that such attribution would lead to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot have attributes because that would combine existence and non-existence.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into a foundational debate in Islamic theology: can non-existent things have attributes? The author argues that they cannot, because claiming otherwise would force a logical contradiction. Here we examine the central argument that if non-existence had existence, existence and non-existence would coexist, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic claim.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that existence and states are not established in non-existence. Notice how the author singles out existence as the clearest case.
3
Understand the proof
The author gives a proof by contradiction: if the existence of the non-existent were established in non-existence, then existence and non-existence would coexist, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Consider why this argument is fundamental in kalam: it establishes that non-existence cannot have any attributes, a key point against the Mu'tazilite concept of states.

The First Observatory - The Sixth Objective: Is the non-existent a thing or not?
Existence (and states) are distinct and are not established in non-existence according to you. And it is as if he specified existence for mention despite its inclusion in states, because its being established in non-existence is denied by agreement and necessity, for if the existence of the non-existent were established in its state of non-existence, the coexistence of existence and non-existence would be necessary. Then the objection by states is only directed against the deniers of states. And motions [السكنات] and that leads to the doctrine of the Sophists. Some said that attribution [الاتصاف] is possible and they distinguished by saying that sophistry only follows if we say: the ordering of external effects and judgments in the state of non-existence. And in it that attribution to sensible accidents is from external effects.
His saying: 'in non-existence' i.e. in the state of non-existence of what is qualified by them.
His saying: 'And it is as if he specified existence etc.' means that existence, even if it is included in states, the objection by it is directed against the deniers of states from the aspect that it is a state, but from the aspect of its particularity the objection by it is directed against both groups, so it has an advantage over the rest. That is because it is valid to say: existence is qualified by distinction [التميز] in the state of the annihilation of what is qualified by it from the essences, so its establishment is necessary in the state of their annihilation, and that entails the existence of the non-existent in its state of non-existence, whether it is said that existence is a state or not, unlike the rest of states.
His saying: 'existence of the non-existent' i.e. the particular existence that the non-existent is qualified by in its state of non-existence, i.e. the non-existence of the non-existent.
His saying: 'the coexistence of existence etc. would be necessary' necessarily because subsistence [الثبوت] and existence and other states have no state of non-existence at all, so from where does their establishment in non-existence follow? So existence is only in the non-existent because it is an abstract [انتزاعي] matter.
His saying: 'Then the objection etc.' is a response to what the author of al-Maqasid raised, that the opponent's principle is only that every known thing is established in the external: if it is existent, then in existence; if it is non-existent, then in non-existence; or if it is neither existent nor non-existent, then in that state. And existence and other states have no state of non-existence at all, so from where does their establishment in non-existence follow?
His saying: 'And they are not established in non-existence according to you.' If you say: more appropriate in his saying in what follows is 'and they are not established according to you neither in existence nor in non-existence nor in anything else'; he omitted 'in non-existence', so what is the reason for mentioning it? I say: since the objection is with respect to the deniers of states, and they say that they are non-existents, this restriction was more appropriate. As for what he will mention, it is a generalization intended to harmonize with the wording of al-Maqasid who raised his saying 'Then the objection etc.' as a refutation, as is indicated by examining it.
His saying: 'And it is as if he specified existence for mention etc.' It is said: the upshot of this apology is that what is meant by existence in the preceding is the existence of the non-existent, by the indication of his saying 'for if the existence of the non-existent were established etc.' and it is not a state. And the more apparent from the context is that it is something whose necessity and agreement of all on the non-establishment of the existence of the non-existent is verified, even if its statehood is assumed, considering that existence subsists in the existent in general. As for the rest of states, there is no necessity in the negation of their establishment, nor even agreement of all, even if the agreement of the deniers of the state is verified.
His saying: 'the coexistence of existence and non-existence would be necessary.' It is said: They say that the essences of accidents are established in non-existence from
against the deniers of the state, as if it is said: the concepts that some call states are undoubtedly distinct, and they are not established according to you at all, neither in existence nor in non-existence nor in anything else. As for the proponent of the state, he says that they are established as intermediaries. (This) as mentioned. And (indeed) we have shown that its establishment, i.e., the establishment of the possible non-existent, contradicts its being an object of power and its being an object of will. For what indicates the negation of power also indicates the negation of will. So it cannot be proven by it, i.e., proving its establishment by its being an object of power and will, some of them without others. In summary, distinction [التميز] that you claim to be established for the possible non-existent: if you mean by it the fixed status in the negated, which is mental distinction, then it is apparent that it does not necessitate establishment; otherwise the negated would also be established. And if you mean by it other than that, i.e., other than that status, then its meaning is, i.e., we do not affirm the establishment of distinction that is other than that status for the possible non-existent, and upon you, first, its conceptualization so that we know what it is, and its verification, i.e., demonstrating its establishment for the non-existent.
His saying: 'There is no doubt that they are distinct' for you do not doubt their distinctness; you only doubt their statehood.
His saying: 'And they are not established according to you' i.e., they are completely negated; you do not affirm them at all, let alone establishment.
His saying: 'And in summary etc.' What has passed was a general objection, and this is a detailed objection, making the form of the objection a basis for denial. So the meaning of his saying 'in summary' is: the sum of the discussion and its essence is this, not that it is a summary of the preceding.
Except that it subsists in substances, and the like is permissible in existence without the necessity of a single thing being existent and non-existent. And the response is that his saying 'for if it were established etc.' is a notification of the negation of the establishment of the non-existent in non-existence, which was claimed to be necessary in the sense of self-evidence. And allow its establishment in non-existence without subsisting in the non-existent contradicts necessity and agreement, so it is disregarded. Even if the objection was against the claim of agreement on the negation of existence in the state of non-existence, it is also not valid because states in his saying 'then the objection by states' includes existence under them; so what is meant is the agreement of the deniers of the state. So reflect. And the conclusion is that the basis of the saying is on heedlessness of the addition [الإضافة] in 'existence of the non-existent', so do not be heedless.
His saying: 'neither in non-existence' because the concepts that some call states are mental considerations, not of the nature that existence occurs to them according to you, so they are of the category of impossible things, while you affirm the establishment of possible non-existents.
His saying: 'He says that they are established as intermediaries.' If you say: The Mu'tazila restrict subsistence in non-existence to possible things, and the state according to its proponent is not among possible things, because they are not objects of power, so how is his saying valid? 'As for the proponent of the state etc.' I say: They only restrict subsistence in the state of non-existence to possible non-existents, not absolute subsistence.
His saying: 'indicates the negation of will as well' because will, as will come in the discussions of accidents, only relates to a concomitant object of power according to the people of verification.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the argument regarding whether non-existent entities have any attributes or states. The author begins by stating that existence and states (such as distinction) are distinct but not established in non-existence according to the opponents. He notes that the original text specifically mentions existence despite its inclusion within states, because the claim that existence is established in non-existence is universally denied by agreement and necessity; to affirm otherwise would entail the coexistence of existence and non-existence, a contradiction. The objection by states is directed only against deniers of states (those who deny statehood to attributes). Some opponents attempted to avoid sophistry by distinguishing between mere attribution and the ordering of external effects, but the author counters that attribution to sensible accidents is itself an external effect. 

The commentary then explains the phrase 'in non-existence' as meaning in the state of non-existence of the thing qualified by those attributes. It clarifies why existence is singled out: because it is the most problematic case. Even if existence is considered a state, asserting that existence is qualified by distinction during the non-existence of the essences implies that the existence of the non-existent is established, leading to the contradiction of existence and non-existence. The argument emphasizes that subsistence, existence, and other states have no state of non-existence at all; they are abstract concepts. The objection from states is then addressed by referencing the position of the author of al-Maqasid, who claimed that all known things are established in the external—existing things in existence, non-existing things in non-existence, and things neither existent nor non-existent in a third state. The commentator refutes this by insisting that states have no existence in non-existence. 

The discussion proceeds to address why the original text used 'in non-existence' instead of a more general phrase, explaining it was tailored to the deniers of states who consider states non-existent. The commentator then delves into a nuanced point: the apology that 'existence' means the existence of the non-existent is problematic; the context suggests that the necessity and universal agreement on the non-establishment of the existence of the non-existent is indeed verified, even if one assumes existence is a state. For other states, there is no such necessity or agreement. 

The commentary then turns to a detailed refutation of the claim that non-existent possibles have distinction. It argues that the establishment of the possible non-existent contradicts its being an object of power and will, as these attributes only apply to existents. Distinction, if mental, does not imply external establishment; if it is something else, the opponent must first conceptualize and prove it. The passage concludes with an explanation that the concepts called states are mere mental considerations, not liable to existence, and that the Mu'tazila restrict subsistence in non-existence only to possible things, not to states. Finally, it notes that will only relates to a concomitant object of power, reinforcing the argument against the establishment of non-existent possibles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate over whether non-existence can have attributes is merely a linguistic or conceptual issue.
Correction: The argument is ontological: attributing existence to non-existence leads to a logical contradiction, demonstrating that non-existence has no attributes at all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason why the existence of the non-existent cannot be established in non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is nothing and cannot have attributes.
B) Because it would lead to the coexistence of existence and non-existence.
C) Because the concept of existence is abstract.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny the possibility of states.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of non-existence having attributes is proven by the logical contradiction that would arise if existence were attributed to non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you cling to past mistakes or failures as if they are still real and defining you? How does the truth that non-existence cannot have attributes free you from being chained to what no longer exists? Will you release that grip today?
?
You claim to have faith in the afterlife, yet you live as though this world alone has lasting reality. Since existence and non-existence cannot coexist, what does your attachment to perishable things say about the reality of your faith in the eternal? What will you change starting now?

Rational Judgments
