Refuting the Fixedness of Nonexistent Possibles
Critique of thubut for nonexistent possibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that nonexistent possibles have fixedness (thubūt) contradicts their being objects of power and will, and how mental distinction does not entail external fixedness.

Core Idea
Mental distinction is not the same as real fixedness.

This passage addresses a key debate in kalām: do nonexistent possible things have some kind of fixedness? Some argue that they do, based on power and will or mental distinction. Here, the author refutes this by showing that such fixedness contradicts the very attributes used to prove it, and that mental distinction falls short of establishing external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: it sets up two opposing views and then argues against one.
2
Identify the two main views
Identify the deniers of states (nufāt al-ḥāl) and the proponents of states. Understand what they claim about fixedness of nonexistents.
The deniers deny any fixedness; proponents assert it as an intermediary.
3
Understand the contradiction argument
Focus on the claim that fixedness contradicts being an object of power and will. Note that the same proof denying power also denies will.
This shows the interdependence of these attributes.
4
Analyze the distinction point
Examine the distinction (tamayyuz) argument: mental distinction vs. something else. Understand why mental distinction does not prove external fixedness.
The author shifts the burden of proof onto the claimant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على نفاة الحال كأنه قيل : المفهومات التي يسميها بعضهم أحوالاً، لا شك أنها متمايزة، وليست ثابتة عندكم أصلاً لا في الوجود ولا في العدم ولا في غيرهما، وأما القائل بالحال فيقول : إنها ثابتة على أنها واسطة (هذا) كما ذكر (و) قد (بينا أن ثبوته ) أي ثبوت المعدوم الممكن ( ينافي كونه مقدوراً (و) كونه (مراداً ) فإن ما يدل على نفي المقدورية يدل على نفي المرادية أيضاً ( فلا يمكن إثباته به ) أي إثبات ثبوته بكونه مقدوراً ومراداً بعضه دون بعض، وبالجملة فالتميز) الذي ادعيتم ثبوته للمعدوم الممكن ( إن أردتم به القدر الثابت في المنفي) وهو التميز الذهني ( فظاهر أنه لا يوجب الثبوت ) وإلا لكان المنفي أيضاً ثابتاً ) وإن أردتم به غيره ) أي غير ذلك القدر ( معناه ) أي لا نسلم ثبوت التميز الذي هو غير ذلك القدر للمعدوم الممكن وعليكم) أولاً ( تصويره ) حتى نعلم أنه ماذا ( وتقريره ) أي بيان ثبوته للمعدوم
Against the deniers of the state (al-ḥāl), it is as if it were said: The concepts that some call states are undoubtedly distinct, and they are not fixed (thābita) for you at all, neither in existence nor in non-existence nor in anything else. As for the proponent of the state, he says: They are fixed as an intermediary. This is as mentioned. And we have shown that his fixedness (thubūtuhu) – i.e., the fixedness of the nonexistent possible (al-maʿdūm al-mumkin) – contradicts his being an object of power (maqdūr) and his being an object of will (murād). For what indicates the negation of being an object of power also indicates the negation of being an object of will. So it cannot be proven by it – i.e., proving his fixedness by his being an object of power and will in part without the whole. In summary, the distinction (al-tamayyuz) that you claim is established for the nonexistent possible: if you mean by it the fixed measure in what is negated (al-nafy) – which is mental distinction – then it is apparent that it does not necessitate fixedness; otherwise the negated would also be fixed. And if you mean by it something other than that, then it is rejected – i.e., we do not concede the establishment of a distinction other than that for the nonexistent possible. Upon you first to depict it so that we may know what it is, and to establish it – i.e., to demonstrate its fixedness for the nonexistent.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of those who deny the reality of states (aḥwāl) and who claim that nonexistent possible things have some kind of fixedness (thubūt). The argument begins by noting that the concepts some call states are undoubtedly distinct from each other, but they are not fixed for the deniers at all—neither in existence, nor in non-existence, nor elsewhere. The proponent of states, however, claims they are fixed as intermediaries. The commentary then recalls a preceding point: the fixedness of a nonexistent possible would contradict its being an object of power (maqdūr) and will (murād), because any argument that negates its being an object of power also negates its being an object of will. Hence, one cannot use these properties to prove fixedness for part of it without the whole. The passage then examines the notion of distinction (tamayyuz) that is claimed for the nonexistent possible. If the distinction is merely the fixed measure in the mind (al-nafy al-dhihnī), then it does not entail external fixedness—otherwise, even the negated (the impossible) would be fixed. If they mean something else, the opponent must first depict what that distinction is and then prove that it necessitates fixedness. The argument leaves the burden of proof on the claimant, establishing that the fixedness of nonexistents is not self-evident and cannot be derived from power, will, or mental distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental distinction or conceptual fixedness implies real external fixedness.
Correction: The passage argues that mental distinction (al-tamayyuz al-dhihnī) does not necessitate external fixedness; otherwise even the impossible would be fixed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the fixedness (thubūt) of a nonexistent possible contradict its being an object of power (maqdūr) and will (murād)?
A) Because power and will require actual existence in the external world.
B) Because nonexistent things cannot be objects of will according to the Mu'tazila.
C) Because the same proof that negates power also negates will, making fixedness impossible.
D) Because distinction (tamayyuz) is sufficient for fixedness.

Key Takeaway
The fixedness of nonexistent possibles cannot be proven by power, will, or mental distinction; each argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat your mental distinctions—like neat categories or assumptions about what is possible—as if they were fixed realities outside your mind? When has that led you to mistake a convenient thought for truth?
?
The argument here shows that being an object of power and will cannot prove fixedness. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that because you desire something or feel capable of it, it must have some reality or legitimacy? Where might that assumption be deceiving you?
?
If the fixedness of nonexistent possibles is rejected, what does that mean for how you think about God’s knowledge and power? Are you comfortable with the idea that some things have no fixed 'reality' before they exist, or does that unsettle a system you rely on? What are you willing to let go of in your understanding of God’s attributes?
